This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission that
visited Chile in June/July 1992. Mission members included Mesdames/Messrs.
Evangeline Javier (Mission Leader), Patricio V. Marquez (Public Health Specialist,
LA4HR); Eduardo Talero (Information Technology Specialist, ASTIF); Jose Carvalho
(Senior Counsel, LEGLA); Jean Pillet (Consultant, Public Health Specialist), Luis
Duque (Consultant, Public Health Specialist), Anita Artaza (Consultant, Operations
Assistant), and Suzzane McQueen (Summer Intern). Messrs. Philip Musgrove (Senior
Health Economist, LATER); Willy de Geyndt (Senior Public Health Specialist, ASTPH),
Harry Go (Architect, ASTPH), Daniel Puzin (Consultant, Architect) and Angel
Gonzalez-Malaxechevarria (Financial Management Specialist, LATPS) participated in
separate missions and provided valuable technical advice and substantive
contributions. Special acknowledgment goes to the ingenious work done by the Chilean
project preparation team. Ms. Lucine Juskalian (Staff Assistant, LA4HR) provided
assistance in the production of the report. Task Manager: Evangeline Javier
(LA4HR), Division Chief: James W. Adams (LA4HR); Director: Ping-Cheung Loh (LA4)i
Peer Reviewers: Philip Musgrove (LATHR) and Jose Luis Bobadilla (PHRHN).

REPUBLIC OF CHILE
HRALTH SECTOR REFORM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Loan and Project Summary
Bo>ower:                  Republic of Chile
Beneficiary:              Ministry of Health (MOH)
Amount:                   US$90.0 million, equivalent
Terms:                    Repayment in 17 years, including a 5 year grace
period with interest at the Bank's standard variable
rate.
Prolect Objectives:       The proposec. project would assist the Government to
enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of resource use in the public
health system, as well as to improve the quality of health services in
selected areas. Specifically, it would:
(a) strengthen the decentralization of health services delivery through
reforms in MOH's mission and organizational structure, and improve the
system of generating and transferring financing resources;
(b) upgrade the institutional organization and management capacity for
procuring and distributing drugs and food supplements, as well as for
the quality control of drugs and food products;
(c) improve the management and clinical skills of health personnel, and
improve the sector management and health information system; and
(d) assist in the implemention of a new Chilean health care model to
develop specialized ambulatory services at the secondary and tertiary
levels, improve the deteriorated conditions of selected hospital
facilities, and fill the health services gaps in eight Health Service
Areas (HSAs) in Metropolitan Santiago, Antofagasta, and Llanchipal.
Prolect Description:      The proposed project constitutes the second phase in
the Bank's lending assistance to the sector. It would support the
implementation of many of the policy and institutional reforms defined
under the first operation, the Technical Assistance and Rehabilitation
Project (TAHRP). The project has four principal components:
(a) Decentralization of Health Services Delivery (15.2 percent of total
groiect cost): This component, a critical feature of the proposed
operation, would finance the pilot tests or start-up activities and
nationwide implementation, as appropriate, of the reorganization of the
MOH, the conversion of the Central Supply Facility (CSF) into an



vi
autonomous agency, the str6ngthening of the Institute of Public Health
(ISP), the institution of a new health care financing system and
improved cost recoveoy measures, the training of sector personnel, and
the upgrading of the sector information system. For this component, the
project would tinanco consultant services, training expenses, computer
hardware and software, and operating costs.
(b) Facility IZMastmnts for a New Health Care Nbdal (82.5 £ercent of
the total nrolect cost): This component would enhance the integration
of health services delivery in eight HsAs by: (a) establishing
ambulatory care centers (CR8 and CDT) at the secondary and tertiary
level complementiing the primary care services; and (b) rehabilitating,
reconstructing, or constructing, as needed, hospital facilities to fill
identified gaps in the health services delivery network. The project
would finance the civil works, equipment, and recurrent costs of
selected facilities in six HSAs in metropolitan Santiago, one HSA in
Antofagasta, and one H5A in Llanchipal.
(c)  SuY=ort for Soecial Proarams (0.  nercent of total orolect cost):
This component would initiate or strengthen actions addressing two major
health concerns in Cbtle, financing equipment and operating costs for
the implementation of the Cervical Cancer Program and Environmental
Health Bducation Program.
(d, Proiect Administration (1.5 percent of total oroject cost): This
component would support the operation of a PCU to assist the MDH to
coordinate and manage project implementation, provide training to the
HSAn, and undertake monitoring and evaluation.
Rx=ected Benefits: By supporting critical policy and institutional reforms
and priority health facility investments, the projuct, in conjunction
with the on-going TAHRP, would help improve the efficiency of resource
use in the public health sector, enhance the quality of ambulatory and
inpatient care, strengthen the capacity of the system to respond more
effectively to the emerging health needs of an aging population, and
increase the complementarity of public and private providers. The
project facility investments are estimated to benefit approximately 30
percent (about 4.0 million) of the Chilean population mostly belonging
to the indigent and low-income groups. The sector reforms intended to
increase the health systom's efficiency coupled with improvements in
cost recovery measures would generate benefits nationwide in the long
term. An integrated health services network and improved delivery of
cost-effective health care interventions would help diminish mortality
and morbidity from chronic diseaoes and injuries.
Risks and safeauards: The major risks include: (a) the complexity of reforms
in the area of health financing; (b) the long-term horizon of the sector
reforms, most of which would be fully implemented either towards the end
of the current Government's tenure or during the next administration;
and (c) the lack of project implementation experience of some HSAU and
hospital managers. These risks would be minimized by the Bank's close
supervision at the different stages of the execution of the reforms;
institution of a Mid-Term lroject Review by the Bank and the Goiernment
to evaluate implementation progress; and technical a3sistance and



vii
supervioion to be provided by the PCU to tkl. HSAa, as wvll as the
training under the rroject. Also, the ongoing TAHRP implemntation io
providing valuable hands-on training and project management experiences
to HSA   Iso involved in this project's execution.

VHZINIT,IONS
Age-Specific Mortality      Death rates broken down for each age group
Rates                       population.
Average Length of Stay      Number of hospital patient-days/number of patient
admissions.
Average Occupancy           Number of hospitals patient-days/number of beds
Rate                        multiplied by 365.
Child Death Rate            The number of deaths among children one to four
years of age per 1,000 children in that age group
in a given year.
Chronic Diseases            Those illnesses characterized by a long latency
period, prolonged clinical course, and
debilitating manifestations.
Contraceptive               The percentage of married women in reproductive
Prevalence                  ages who are regularly using a method of
contraception.
Crude Birth Rate            Number of live births per year per 1,000 people.
Crude Death Rate            Number of deaths per year per 1,000 people.
Dependency Ratio            Population 14 years or under and 65 years or older
as a percentage of the population aged 15 to 64
years.
Xncidence Rate              The number of persons contracting a disease as a
proportion of the population at risk, per unit of
time usually expressed per 1,000 persons.
Infant Mortality Rate       Annual deaths of infants less than 1 year old per
1,000 live births during the same year.
Life Expectancy             The number of years' a newborn child would
at Birth                    live if subject to the age-specific mortality
rates prevailing at time of birth.
Maternal Mortality         iUumber of maternal deaths per 10,000 live birth
rates in a given year attributable to pregnancy,
childbirth, or post-partum.
Morbidity                   Disease and illness in a population.
Mortality                   Death in a population.
Prevalence Rate             The number of persons having a particular disease
at a given time per population. Usually expressed
per 1,000 persons.

Rate of Natural              Difference between crude birth and crude death
Increase                     rates; usually expressed as a percentage.
Rate of Population           Rate of natural increase adjusted for (net)
Growth                       migration, and expressed as a percentage of the
total population in a given year.
Total Fertility Rate         The average number of children a woman will have
if she experiences a given set (i.e., those then
prevailing among women of those ages) of age
specific fertility rates throughout her lifetime.
Serves as an estimate of the number of children
per completed family.

REPUBLIC OF CHILE
HEALTH SEMcOR REFORM PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
r.   BQgROUND
1.01      The promotion of social justice with macroeconomic stability and
growth is the centerpiece of the Chilean Government's development agenda.
President Aylwin assumed office in early 1990 with a platform focussed on
poverty alleviation and economic growth. In 1.991 Chile's open economy, with
robust private sector participation, grew by 6 percent; the inflation rate
declined significantly; the fiscal accounts were in surplus; and the current
account of the balance of payments for the first time in many years also
registered a surplus. This notable performance was fueled in part by a strong
growth in exports, mainly non-copper products. Within the strictly guarded
constrainto of a balanced fiscal budget, the Government has started to enhance
the quality and efficiency of key social programs as a means to assure acceos
of the less privileged to select basic needs, particularly health and
education services. Public expenditures in 1991 increased at a real rate of
12 percent benefitting largely the social sectors.
1.02      Chile's social development over the last three decades has been
impressive. While Chile's GNP per capita of US$2,129 in 1990 places it among
lower-middle-income countries, its social indicators closely resemble the
profile observed in developed countries. Chile's average life expectancy at
birth of 72 years (1990) is well above the Latin American average of 66 years;
infant mortality rate is a relatively low 17/1,000; the prevalence of moderate
and severe malnutrition (measured by weight-for-age) among children under six
years of age is less than . percent (1988), similar to that of developed
countries; and the average level of educational attainment of 10 years of
schooling among the population 15 years of age or older (1987) and overall
adult literacy rate of 94 percent are achievements to which many developing
countries aspire.
1.03      The foregoing developments have been fostered by a strong political
ccamitment to social welfare by successive governments of widely disparate
political philosophies. Sustained public investments in maternal and child
health care and food supplementation programs, basic education, and potable
water have played a major role in health improvements. In the 1980's, Chile
aspired to redefine the role of the State in the health sector, decentralize
primary health care responsibilities to the municipalities, revise health
financing mechanisms, promote private sector participation, and target public
health and nutrition expenditures to low-income groups. However, serious
imbalances and inefficient resource use continue to constrain health sector
productivity and service quality. In addition, there are pockets of the
population, especially in marginal areas, with health status worse than the
majority of the populatioa.
1.04       Major problems in the health sector include the lack of coordina-
tion between levels of care; low internal efficiency in service delivery,
particularly within the public hospital network; distortions in the price
schedule and in the pattern of utilization of health services; and weakness in
the institutional and human resource capability of the Ministry of Health and
its autonomous agencies. Moreover, numerous municipalities lack the
administrative and financial capacity to provide adequate primary care. An



2
additional challenge is to strengthen and expand the complementarity, an
appropriate, of public and private health care provision to ensure that the
basic health service needs of the underserved population and the changing
health needs of an aging population are met.
1.05      The present Government is implementing a National Health Plan to
institute critical reforms in the sector. A major objective is to achieve a
balance in the public health care system, continuing its traditional focus on
maternal and child health while responding more effectively to the changing
health needs of adults and the elderly. Furthermore, the Government is
resolved to increase efficiency in the use of existing resources; provide
incentives for cost-savings; strengthen the public/private mix in the
financing and delivery of health services; and delegate most decisions on
resource allocation to local authorities. In addition, the Government is
committed to increase total public health speuiding. Savings from planned
efficiency measures, wider cost recovery from public patients, and increases
in the sale of hospital services to private, fee-paying patients would allow
the Government to meet changing needs without adding significant pressure on
budgetary resources.
1.06      One of the Government's major actions in 1990 was the adoption of a
tax reform to help finance program improvements in health and education. The
Government has also adopted as the recommendations of a study on the
reorientation of the health care system, which will attempt to improve
sectoral capacity to address health problems at the secondary and tertiary
levels of care. In addition, the Government has introduced other changes such
as extending the operating hours of primary health care facilities to attend
to the needs of working adults; increasing the proportion of paid (private)
beds in the public hospitals to augment their revenues; and improving the
monitoring and transparency of the ISAPRBs (the private health insurance
schemes) by providing information to consumers on ISAPREs policies and costs.
1.07      In December 1991, the Bank approved a US$27 million loan for a
Technical Assistance and Hospital Rehabilitation Project (TAHRP) to assist the
Government in the continued definition and pilot-tesding of critical health
policy and institutional reforms, as well as to finance urgent small-scale
hospital rehabilitation works in Metropolitan Santiago. In essence, part of
the TAHRP financed the preparatory studies for this proposed Health Sector
Reform Project (HSRP), which aims to address the efficiency and quality
improvement problems of health services delivery. The Government will
implement many of the recommendations emerging from the TAHRP studies. Some
of the policy choices are already being pilot-tested with TAHRP financing
(e.g. health financing reforms). Other decisions on sector strategy and
policy directions, as well as the facility investments for a new Chilean
health care model would be implemented with the support of this proposed HSRP.
1T.  SECTORAL
A. Population. Health and Nutrition Status
2.01      Pogulation.  In recent decades, Chile has experienced profound
demographic changes. Total population in the country is estimated at 13.5
million in 1992, of which more than 85 percent live in urban areas, particu-
larly in the metropolitan areas of Santiago, ValparaXso-Vifla del Mar, and
Concepci6n. Population density is 18 per square kilometer, which places it



3
among Latin America's least densely populated countries. The crude birth rate
and crude death rates are, respectively, 23.3 and 6 per 1,000 population,
giving a natural population increase of 1.7 percent per year (Annex 1, Table
1). Age structure of the three main groups is as follows:
0-14 years: 30.5 percent;
15-64 years: 63.5 percent;
65 and over:   6.0 percent.
The dependency ratio therefore is 57.5 percent.
2.02      The total fertility rate (TFR) has fallen rapidly.  While in 1965
the average women had about 5 children in her lifetime, by 1990 the TFR had
declined to 2.7 children. As a result of the sustained decline in the
fertility rate, in conjunction with an increase in life expectancy due to
medical advancer .e' improved social conditions, the proportion of elderly
(people aged 6C years and older) to total population is growing rapidly. This
group now accrmnt for 8.8 percent of the populatior.. It is estimated that by
2025 the proportion will increase to 17 percent.
2.03      Health Status.  The health of the Chilean population has improved
substantially, with life expectancy at birth increasing from about 58 years in
1970 to about 68 years in 1989 for males and from 64 years to 76 years for
women. The major cause of the decline in death rates has been a significant
reduction in the incidence of infectious and communicable childhood diseases.
Infant mortality fell from 120 deaths per 1,000 live births in 1960 to 16
deaths per 1,000 live births in 1990, comparing favorably with most,developed
countries (Annex 1, Table 1). The maternal mortality rate is 4 deaths per
10,000 live births, which is low compared to other developing countries.
These advances in life expectancy have resulted from an interplay of a number
of factors, among them reductions in birth rates; high coverage of vaccination
programs for vaccine preventable childhood diseases (97 percent for polio, 94
percent for DPT, and 90 percent for measles); increased access of pregnant
women to prenatal care; greater number of births attended by health
professionals (99 percent in 1990); and provision of milk and other
supplementary foods to both pregnant and lactating women and children. Large-
scale investments to expand access to basic education and improved housing
conditions have also played a role.
2.04       Rapid urbanization and industrialization, as well as significant
changes in lifestyles, have contributed to the emergence of a new set of
health problems. An a result, the mortality profile and the disease burden
have been altered from a developing country profile characterized by the
prevalence of infectious and communicable diseases of childhood to a developed
country profile that includes chronic diseases and injuries among an aging
population. Hypertension, smoking, fat intake, sedentary lifestyle, daily
ingestion of alcohol, occupational hazards, and pollution have become
important determinants of health and disease in Chile.
2.05      The principal causes of death in the 1980s were cardiovascular
diseases, cancer and accidents, accounting for about 58 percent of deaths, up
from 28 percent in the 19605 (Annex 1, Table 2). Among cardiovascular
diseases, ischemic heart disease is the leading cause of mortality, with an
age-adjusted death rate of 36 per 100,000 inhabitants in 1987, below the rates
observed in the United States (67) and in Canada (64), but twice as high than



4
those in other Latin American countries such as Ecuador and Peru. The age-
adjusted death rate from cancer in 1987 was 73 per 100,000 inhabitants, a
relatively high rate camparable to that of the United States or of Canada.
The age-adjusted death rate from accidents and violence in 1987 was 61 per
100,000 inhabitants, exceeding the rates of the United States (48), Argentina
(44), and Canada (38). In 1983, injuries, cancer, chronic liver disease, and
diseases of the heart accounted for 62 percent of total years of potentially
productive life lost for both sexes before age 65. In 1987, injuries and
cancer alone accounted for close to 40 percent of total years of potential
life lost.
2.06      In the future, as the Chilean population evolves toward an older
age structure, a consequence of declining fertility rates and the aging of the
large number of children who were born during past periods of high fertility,
the relative importance of chronic diseases awd injuries as principal causes
of mortality and morbidity will grow.
2.07      Nutrition Status.  The nutrition status of the Chilean population
is generally satisfactory. Although its economic development progressed
slowly over much of the past few decades, Chile significantly reduced mal-
nutrition (measured by weight-for-age) among children under the age of 6 years
from 15.9 percent in 1976 to 8.6 percent in 1988. The majority of malnutri-
tion in this age group is mild (i.e., 12.1 percent prevalence in 1976 and 7.7
percent in 1988); the prevalence of moderate and severe malnutrition is among
the lowest in the developing world, affecting 0.8 and 0.1 percent,
respectively, of children under 6 years of age. Various evaluations of the
National Supplementary Feeding Program (PNAC) have indicated that integrated
health and Rutrition interventions have had a significant impact in preventing
and controlling malnutrition. A different type of nutrition problem is now
emerging among adults due to the strong relationship between certain dietary
habits (e.g., consumption of saturated fats, sugars) and the incidence of
chronic conditions (e.g., atherosclerosis, obesity).
B. The Health Care System
2.08      Organized medicine in Chile dates back to the 1920e, when social
insurance arrangements were established to provide health services for blue
collar workers.  With the creation of the National Health Service (NHS) in
1952, diverse public institutions were consolidated under a unified health
structure. Up to the late 1970s, health care in Chile was provided by four
distinct delivery systems. The two largest systems were the NHS, responsible
for providing preventive services to the entire population and free hospital
care to blue-collar workers and indigents, and the National Medical Service
for Employees (SERNENA), a government administered health insurance plan for
white-collar workers and their dependents, established in 1968. Both systems
covered approximately 85 percent of the population; about 10 percent of the
population was covered by private providers working on a fee-for-service
basis; and 5 percert by the armed forces medical program.
2.09      With the introduction of major institutional and financial reforms
beginning in 1979, the health care system was reorganized. The reforms
included: (a) decentralizing the old NHS through the establishment of a
National Health Service System (NHSS) comprised of 26 operational regional
autonomous services plus an environmental health service in Santiago;



5
(b) transferring responsibility, infrastructure and personnel for the delivery
of primary health care to the municipalities; (c) creation of a financial
institution (FONASA) for administering health sector resources; and (d)
establishing the legislative framework and financing mechanisms to support the
development of prepaid private health insurance plans. The resulting
institutional structure, which is characterized by a complex combination of
public and private providers and financial agencies, is shown in Annex 1,
Figure 1. The present roles and relationships among the main sectoral
institutions are described in paras. 2.10-2.17 below.
2.10      The Ministry of Health (MpH).  With the abolition of the NHS, the
M0H assumed responsibility for the formulation of all public health policies,
definition of norms and standards applied on a nationwide basis, and broad
health planning and monitoring. In performing these functions, the MOH
coordinates and supervises the delivery and financing of health services
through: (i) its Undersecretary of Health, which oversees health care delivery
through the National Health Service System (para. 2.11); and (ii) four
autonomous agencies: the National Health Fund (FONASA), the principal health
care financing mechanism in the country; the Central Supply Facility (CSF), in
charge of procurement and distribution of pharmaceuticals and other medical
supplies to public health facilities ordering directly from it, as well as
milk and other products provided via public health facilities by the National
Supplementary Feeding Program (PNAC); the Institute of Public Health
(Instituto de Salud Publica. ISP), responsible for quality control of
pharmaceuticals and food products; and the Superintendency of ISAPRES,
recently created to regulate the private health insurance market (see the
current MOH Organization Chart in Annex 1, Figure 2).
2.11      The National Health Service System (NHSS). The NHSS consists of 26
decentralized Health Service Areas (HSAs) plus one that administers the
Environmental Health Service in Santiago. The HSAs, which are distributed
across the nation's 13 administrative regions, assumed the operational
responsibilities of the former NHS. HSAs do not necessarily correspond to
other existing administrative/political boundaries in the country; they were
defined on the basis of health catchment areas (i.e., factors such as
population size, existing health facilities, and local health status were
considered in defining the HSAe). HSAs are responsible for the operation of
all public hospitals in their respective geographical area, as well as for
technical oversight of the municipal-based primary care system (para. 2.13).
2.12      Each HSA is legally an autonomous service run by a Director
(generally a physician), who reports directly to the Secretariat (SEREMI) of
the respective administrative region in which the HSA is located (the SEREMIS
are composed of staff from each major ministry who report directly to the
intendente or administrator of the region and to their respective ministry
headquarters in Santiago). In practice, however, the HSA director implements
mostly the decisions made at the M0H central office with regard to health
service delivery. The Director, however, has some discretionary control over
personnel and financial matters.
2.13      Municioalities.  Increased administrative decentralization in the
public system was achieved in 1987 by transferring the management of orimarv
health car services to the municipalities, including personnel recruitment
and management. The primary care network transferred to the municipalities
consists of urban and rural clinics, rural health posts, and rural medical



6
stations. In contrast to HSAs' personnel who are civil service employees,
staff operating these facilities are municipal employees governed by the labor
code applicable to the private sector. As stipulated in the 1985 Health Law,
all NHSS users are classified into one of four groups according to their
ability to pay for services rendered. Those classified in categories A and B
as indigents or poor (76 percent of all beneficiaries under the FONASA system
in 1989) are not required to pay user charges in public health facilities
although they may pay the mandatory 7 percent payroll deduction.
2.14      National Health Fund (FONASA).  The Fund exercises the financial
functions previously under the responsibility of NHS and SERMENA.  It is
responsible for administering and distributing financial resources of the
public system, including a mandatory 7 percent payroll deduction earmarked for
health from all active and pensioned salaried workers which is collected by
INP (Instituto de Normalizacion rrevisional), as well as central government
allocations for health. FONASA finances the HSAs through (a) allocations
based on the number and grades of personnel, and (b) a fee for service
mechanism (FAP) that takes into account the amount and type of services
rendered in the different hospitals of the region. In principle, FONASA
reimburses the municipalities under a special billing scheme (FAPEM) for
facility-specific primary care services rendered. In practice, however, there
are ceilings and floors on the amount reimbursed. Resources are also
transferred under a scheme of incentives based on selected biomedical and
process performance and financial indicators.
2.15      Preferred Provider System (PPS). All workers contributing to
FONASA have the option to select providers in the private sector under the PPS
arrangement. Private providers participating in the PPS are reimbursed
according to a fixed price system for specific medical services rendered.
FONASA pays only a portion of the required ambulatory or inpatient service;
the user pays the other part with a voucher purchased from FONASA. The
provider redeems the voucher for 100 percent of the pre-agreed price at one of
FONASA's offices, or at selected commercial banks. Under the PPS copayment
system, users pay 50 percent of the cost of ambulatory medical consultations.
For other services, the copayments vary by level of provider: users pay 50
percent of the cost of service in level 1; 66.7 percent in level 2; and 75
percent in level 3. Usually, new physicians and general practitioners are
clustered in level 1 and specialists in level 3; the level of a clinic or a
hospital is determined by its technological sophistication. The more
expensive the provider, the higher the copayment.
2.16      Private Providers.  In addition to physician offices and inpatient
facilities concentrated in large cities, and sickness funds (mutuales) for
administering work-injury benefits which are financed by an 0.8 percent
payroll deduction paid by employers, health care is provided in the private
sector through prepaid health insurance schemes (ISAPREs). The rapid growth
of the ISAPREs in recent years has been the result of the introduction of
public policies that have encouraged private sector activity in the health
sector. In 1981 a government decree was enacted to allow ISAPREs to collect
directly the obligatory 7 percent salary deduction of persons willing to
purchase private health care coverage. Additionally, the ISAPREs receive
yearly public subsidies (approximately US$44 million in 1991) through: (a) a
full tax rebate to those employers who contribute an additional 2 percent of
payroll to complement the compulsory 7 percent salary deduction (about US$19
million); (b) government benefit payments for maternity leave to ISAPRBs



7
affiliates, whereas this benefit is paid by FONASA for affiliates to the
FONASA eyetem (about US$23 million); and (c) free distribution of vaccines to
children 0-9 years of age (about US$250,000), and food supplementation
products to children under 6 years of age and pregnant women (about US$1.2
million), who are ISAPREs affiliates. Two other implicit subsidies accorded
to the IWAPRB system are: (a) exemption of the 7 percent salary deduction from
taxable income; and (ii) exemption of health services from the value added tax
(VAT) up to an amount equivalent to PONASA level three prices. ISAPREs offer
subscribers and their dependents both outpatient and inpatient care on a cost-
sharing basis via either their own clinics and/or diagnostic centers or
through contract with priva.e providers or public facilities. ISAPRE
affiliates have to pay a monthly premium that varies according to the cost of
the policy they choose to buy. ISAPWR premiums are financed from the
following sources: transfer or assignment to the ISAPREs of the compulsory 7
percent salary deduction; payments on behalf of members by their employers (in
"closed" plans organixed by employer groups); and out-of-pocket payments by
affiliates varying between 10 to 40 percent of the cost of each service.
Highly paid affiliates, therefore, may pay nothing beyond their 7 percent
salary deduction, while the payment by others depends on their salary level
and the policy they choose to buy.
2.17      Although there is a multiplicity of health care providers in Chile,
the bulk of preventive and curative services is provided by the public sector
through a vast network of ambulatory facilities and hospitals. Official
figures indicate that in 1990, 71 percent of the population was covered by the
EMSS (including HSAE, municipalities, armed forces and state enterprises);
about 13 percent by the FONASA-administered PPS; and 16 percent by the ISAPREs
(Annex 1, Chart 1).
C.  Health Care Resources and Utilization_
2.18      Amulatory Services.  Over the last two decades, with the priority
attached to the expansion of primary care, health investments shifted signi-
ficantly away from hospitals in favor of primary care facilities. As a
result, the number of primary care facilities increased substantially in urban
and rural areas. The primary care system that was transferred to the munici-
palities over the 19803 consists of four basic types of fa^ilities: rural
medical stations, rural health posts, rural health centers, and urban health
centers. The rural medical stations, the most peripheral echelon, are
buildings used as temporary sites for periodic visits by medical teams. Rural
health posts, staffed with resident health auxiliaries, provide health
promotion and protection services to catchment areas of approximately 1,000
persons. Rural health centers, located in communities of between 2,000 and
5,000 population, offer round-the-clock nursing and medical care for general
health problems of limited complexity. Permanent professional and auxiliary
staff operate these facilities. Urban health centers provide ambulatory care
for general health problems. These facilities have fixed hours, generally
providing care eight to nine hours daily, and the composition of their staff
varies with the size of the locality. There are 1,214 rural medical stations,
1,040 health posts, and 345 health centers distributed across the country.
2.19      Secondary and tertiary care is provided in hospitals and their
outpatient departments (consultorior, adosados do emsecialidades). While
primary health care facilities were constructed in recent decades according to
an MUR master plan, the current hospital network consists of diverse hospitals



a
inherited from religious or welfare institutions--many of the hospitals are
close to a century old. Only a few additional hospitals were built in the
last four decades to complement the public sector network.
2.20      Hosnital Services.  Public hospitals in Chile are classified under
four categories, ranging from Type 4 rural hospitals with minimal technology
to Type 1 with the maximum technology available in the public sector. The
NHSS has 105 Type 4 facilities, which offer the first echelon of inpatient
care. Type 3 hospitals, of which there are 25, are located in small towns and
have only the four basic specialties (internal medicine, pediatrics,
obstetrics-gynecology, and general surgery). Dispersed across the major
cities are 30 Type 2 or general hospitals, which offer most medical and
surgical specialties and have intermediate technology. Tertiary care is
offered in 20 Type 1 facilities, which include all specialties and sub-
specialties. Some of these facilities are specialized hospitals, such as the
Pediatrics, Cancer, and Psychiatric Hospitals. Aside from outpatient
departments of hospitals, there are no free-standing medical centers for
ambulatory secondary or tertiary care (outpatient departments of hospitals are
usually the only providers of specialized care for low-income groups).
2.21      In the private sector there are 190 hospitals (called clinicas),
encompassing both general and specialized inpatient facilities, and 1,069
ambulatory care centers for single or group medical practice. There are also
30 hospitals under the administration of universities, the armed forces or
state enterprises.
2.22      Consultations.  For the sector as a whole, the number of medical
consultations averaged 2.5 per person per year in 1989, a figure about half
the corresponding figure for developed countries (number and type of consul-
tations in the public health sector are shown in Annex 1, Table 3). The NHSS
reported 2.4 medical visits per person per year, and accounted for 75 percent
of ali outpat;ieAL visLt; in thc cc=-tr .  B%, coawrsorie^, the T!Eoso haA A"
average of 3.5 physician visits per beneficiary, showing a higher per capita
use of services in this system. Overall, emergency visits in outpatient
departments of hospitals represent a striking 30 percent of all consultations,
indicating that although geographic coverage of health care in Chile is good,
access may be less adequate because of the limited hours of service in primary
care facilities and lack of specialized ambulatory centexs.
2.23      Hosnital Performance.  The number of public hospital beds, both
short and long term, has remained nearly stable at a ratio of 2.5 beds per
1,000 population. As opposed to other countries, Chile never went through a
period of rapid hospital expansion. On the contrary, between 1981 and 1989,
the number of public beds decreased from 33,692 to 32,818 (Annex 1, Table 4A).
It is only in the 1980s that the private sector grew significantly, adding
another 0.8 beds per 1,000 population, to reach an overall total for the
country of 3.3 beds per 1,000 population. This bed ratio is below the ratio
of 5.1 in the United States and 5.0 in Argentina and Uruguay. The hospital
occupancy rate in the public sector is a low 75 percent; this national average
may be misleading, however, as it includes rural hospitals where a lower
occupancy rate is expected given the need to maintain a minimum infrastructure
in remote facilities. Also, a number of available beds in the public sector
are not operational because of the deteriorated condition of hospital wards or
inoperative equipment. Due to the larger, older and technically outdated
nature of most public hospitals and their more complex case mix (i.e.,



9
patients with more severe illnesses), the average length of stay in these
facilities is 7.9 days (Annex 1, Table SA). Lack of a secondary and tertiary
outpatient care infrastructure in the public sector also contributes to a long
average length of stay in public hospitals. ISAPRE's data show an average
length of stay of 4-5 days in the private facilities. Unlike private
hospitals, public hcspitals cannot carry out pre-admission tests on a less
expensive, ambulatory care basis.   The public sector accounts for almost 75
percent of total national hospital bed capacity, an estimated 90 percent of
all hospitalizations and 52 percent of surgical interventions.
2.24      Medical Technoloav.  The priority attached to the development of
primary health care in recent years meant the neglect of the public hospital
network. Amid limited resources, both new construction and equipping of
hospitals, as well as modernization and maintenance of existing hospitals, was
virtually abandoned in favor of expansion and upgrading of primary care
facilities. As a result, physical plant and equipment within the hospitals
deteriorated. Negligible investments led to a failure to adopt new
technologies, even in such critical areas as care for high-risk newborn
infants and trauma, as well as in outpatient departments. Many hospitals
suffer from antiquated or inoperative equipment, even in such basic areas as
X-ray machines and incubators. This obsolescence is not limited to medical
equipment, but also affects supportive ser-:ces such as kitchens,
communications, and information systems. In addition, most hospitals suffer
from serious defects in their physical plants, plumbing, heating/cooling
systems, and sanitary systems. It is estimated that the cumulative effect of
this situation decreases bed utilization across the country by 20 percent.
This is further evidenced by the low bed occupancy rate in some facilities
(i.e., beds often remain empty because of incidental reasons such as the
disrepair of buildings, or a breakdown of the heating or plumbing systems).
2.25      Health Personnel.  With six medical schools, Chile graduates appro-
ximately 600 physicians per year. The total number of physicians in the
country is close to 14,000, which is about one physician per 1,000 population
(public sector health personnel are shown in Annex 1, Table 6). This ratio is
two to three times below the ratio of developed countries, but considerably
exceeds the average for lower middle income economies. The most important
feature of the physician labor market is the geographic maldistribution of
physicians. In general, most of the physicians, particularly those with
specialized training, are concentrated in the few major urban areas. The
rural scholarship program for young physicians who have served three or four
years in rural areas has been an important vehicle to attract qualified young
doctors to underserved areas. Another striking aspect of Chile's physician
market is the low number of general or family practitioners. Indeed, it is
estimated that only 7.5 percent of the nation's physicians are general
practitioners. In Santiago, close to 90 percent of all physicians practicing
in the city are specialists or subspecialists. The growing supply of
specialists is due largely to the specialized orientation of medical education
and the lack of stringent standards regulating the clinical specialties.
There are, however, acute shortages of some medical specialists and health
technicians such as ophthalmologists, radiologists, and anesthesiologists.
2.26      The number of dentists is about 5,000, or one dentist for 2,700
population. Here, too, more than 60 percent of the dentists are concentrated
in Santiago.



10
2.27      The total nurse population is approximately 2,872; the ratio of
ntftse per population is 1 nurse per 4,700 persons. As the number of available
nurses fell below the evolving needs of the primary care system, their
functions were systematically delegated to auxiliary nurses. The simultaneous
growth of the ISAPREs has provided an alternative source of employment for the
relatively few graduate nurses. At present, fewer than 300 nurses graduate
each year, or half the number at the beginning of the 1980s.
2.28      The total number of fixed-term employees at the MHf and the HSAs is
approximately 57,000. In recent years the major change in human resources was
at the primary care level, where about 14,000 health services staff were
separated from the MOH and employed by the municipalities.
D. Health Care Exoenditures and Financino of Services
2.29      Total health care spending in Chile (including direct household
expenditures for medical care and pharmaceuticals) has increased from an
estimated 3.3 percent of GDP in the 1960s to about 6.4 percent in the early
1990s. This ratio is as high as that in sevfiral upper middle-income
developing countries (6-7 percent), but below that of lower-income OBCD
countries (7-8 percent). Total public and private health expenditures are
approximately US$2 billion per year. Per capita expenditure in health is
about US$145, far lower than that in industrial ',ountries (e.g., about 9
percent in Canada and 6 percent in the USA). Public health expenditures are
about 2.5 percent of GDP or 39 percent of total health expenditures. Private
health expenditures account for about 3.9 percent of GDP or 61 percent of
total expenditures. Annual expenditures per beneficiary in the public sector
amount to approximately US$44; in the FONASA-administered PPS, US$55; and in
the ISAPREs, US$160. In 1992, total public health expenditures would amount
to about US$900 million or 9 percent of the nation's budget.
2.30      Sources of Funds.  The MOH depends on the following major revenue
sources administered by FONASA (Annex 2): (a) cotizaciones or transfers
generated by the mandatory 7 percent payroll deduction from all active and
pensioned salaried workers, which accounts for more than a third of total
income; (ii) central government contributions (anorte fiscal), which
contribute about 40 percent of revenues (a significant decline from its 1980
level of 55 percent); (iii) sale of vouchers to FONASA affiliates for
selective services rendered by private providers under the PPS mechanism,
representing 6 percent of revenues; and (iv) fees from the sale of services in
public facilities, representing 9 percent.  In addition to tlv MOH resources,
capital investments in the health sector, particularly for p&imary care
facilities and rural hospitals, are financed through the Ministry of the
Interior-administered National Regional Development Fund (FNDR) and the Social
Fund (SF) of the Office of the President.
2.31      Uses of Funds.  About 98 percent of total public expenditures in
health consists of recurrent expenditures (Annex 2). One-third of these went
to wages; the rest was accounted for by the PPS, PEAC, subsidies paid for
illness of workers, and other transfers and payments. Meanwhile, MOH-financed
investments (construction and equipment for hospitals), which practically came
to a halt in the 1980s accounting for only 2 percent of public health
expenditure. This share increased to about 8 percent in 1992 reflecting the
current Government's priority for social investment.  The average investmnt
in the HSAs between 1986-90 hardly exceeded US$10 million per year, a major



11
cause of the accumulated deterioration and obsolescence of hospital
infrastructure and equipment in the last decade. In contrast, a strong
infusion of investment funds from the IDB-financed FNDR projects has helped
expand and update the primary health care network nationwide (para. 4.05).
FNDR health investments averaged about US$19.5 million per year in the last
five years. It is estimated that FNDR financing will meet the investment
-equirements of the primary care network in the next five years.
2.32      HosDital Financing.  Chilean hospitals are financed in part accord-
ing to production criteria adopted in the late 1970s. Financing varies
according to the number and type of services rendered, with a one month lag in
reimbursements from FONASA. FONASA reimburses hospitals through FAP (Factura-
cion por Atencion Prestada Integral), a fee-for-service schedule. The FAP
transfers are intended to cover all operating expenditures, except salaries
(para. 2.33?. FAP includes a list of p,aestablished prices for more than
2,000 medical interventions (prices have been determined on the basis of the
average costs in one public and two pri.ate hospitals, as well as the opinions
of Especialists). These prices have not been updated since 1980. The only
unregulated hospital cost is the daily fee charged te' private patients for the
use of hospital beds (excluding medical services related thereto) for which
hospital authorities can set their own prices. Up until July 1991, MOH's
regulations dictated that hospitals may use only 5 percent of their beds for
treating private patients in special wards or nensionados. Recognizing the
spare capacity in some hospitals and their need for additional operating
revenue, the MOH has raised the limit to 10 percent of each hospital's total
number of beds without cutting back on services for public patients .
2.33      In addition, FONASA pays as a budget allocation to the HSAs all
wages and salaries, part of investment, and other fixed costs of the
hospitals. Because personnel and investments costs are covered directly, the
reimbursed FAP amount is a percentage of PPS level 1 prices, and it varies by
type of service, with higher percentages for more complicated procedures.
There are no ceilings on the maximum level of reimbursement a given hospital
can receive from PONASA. Hospitals obtain additional income from fees charged
to private patients and from service copayments paid on a preestablished
sliding scale by beneficiaries of the public health system that are classified
by FONASA as non-indigent (classes C and D).
2.34      Financial information on the performance of Chilean hospitals is
provided by a hospital reporting system called SIGMO. However, data collected
on average costs per service category come in aggregates and are not seen by
hospital administrators as a useful management tool to identify areas for
efficiency gains, or for comparing hospitals with similar patient mix.
2.35      Primary Care Financing.  Municipalities are reimbursed on a
facility-specific basis for the amount and type of services rendered, b-dsed on
FAPEM (Facturacion nor Atencion Prestada en Establecimientos Nunicivalizados),
a detailed, preestablished fee system. FAPEN includes 15 categories of
primary care services, and precise levels of reimbursement per service within
each category are delineated. In practice, however, each facility is subject
to a monthly ceiling on the total amount of fees that could be reimbursed by
FAPEM, regardless of the level or mix of services provided. The ceiling on
expenditures was set initially at the amount the municipality expended in
1982. Thus, facilities that had been receiving the largest transfers prior to
imposition of the ceiling in 1983 continued to be favored in resource



12
allocation. Periodically these ceilings have been adjusted according to
changes in the Public Sector Wage Index; these adjustments, however, have
lagged considerably behind the Consumer Price Index, aggravating the financial
problems of poor municipalities. Additional revenue to finance the priAnary
health care network comes from supplementary transfers and incentiven (e.g.,
achievement of health targets) paid by FONASA and from municipal budgets.
2.36      FAPEM transfers are expected to cover the costg of all goods and
services, personnel, and maintenAnce expenditures incurred in the process of
delivering service at a minimum acceptable standard. Inivestment in infra-
structure and equipment is a municipal responoibility.   v this end,
municipalities can apply for funds from the FNDR or the SP.
Ill.  SE    . SUES AMD gOVEMENT STRATEGY
.        ~~A.  Isauge
3.01      In Chile, widespread basic health service coverage has been
achieved crer the last four decades. The challenge in future years will be
improving the quality of care, managing expenditures, and increasing resource-
use efficiency. Broad reviews of the health sector have hiighlighted five
critical issues: lack of complementarity between the different levels of
health care; low internal efficiency in the public hospital network;
distortions in the financing of health services; weak institutional capability
in the MOH and its autonomous agencies; and the need to develop further
public/private linkages to optimize the use of the limited fiscal resources.
3.02      Articulation Between  evAes1 of Care.  The pyramidal referral flow
between levels of care does not work efficiently. Poor coordination and
complementarity between primary care facilities and hospital services are
major functional defects of the public health system. In particular,
important constraints to optimal utilization patterns in the system, including
limited availability of ambulatory secondary and tertiary facilities (Annex
3), contributes to inadequate backup for the primary care level and continued
high dependence on emergency care at hospitals. Long waiting lines for
obtaining care and inconvenient clinic hours for the working population at the
primary care level aggravate this situation as highlighted by the aforenoted
30 percent of medical consultations being of an "emergency" nature. Failure
of the HSAs to fulfill their monitoring and supervision responsibilities over
the primary care facilities that were placed under the local authority of the
municipalities added to the dissociation between levels of care. In addition,
the FAP-PAPBM reimbursement mechanisms lhave had adverse effects on the
referral of patients to and from hospitals (see para. 3.08). The lack of a
fluid referral of patients threatens the credibility of the entire public
health system. In addition, the emergence of chronic conditions and injuries
as principal causes of disease and death in the country implies increasing
importance of hospitals. Therefore, as a result of the changing
epidemiological profile, hospitals would have to operate in a referral system
in order to control the escalation of costs observed in some developed and
developing countries facing similar challenges. Specialized ambulatory care
facilities are also needed to improve the system's utilization patterns.
3.03      Internal Efficiencv in Hosnitals.  Low internal efficiency
characterizes the public hospital network and results largely frcm the
deteriorated condition of its facilities, including buildings, medical and



13
*lec.ro-mechanical equipment, and support services (Annex 3). In Metropolitan
Santiago, two natural disasters in the mid-1980s worsened this situation. The
1985 earthquake damaged various hospitals; the most evident damages were
repaired with the support of a special fund and donations. Over the following
years, the need for miscellaneous repairs rose significantly, particularly for
physical infrastructure such as rcofo and basements, as well as for plumbing
and heating systems that were left unrepaired. This problem was compounded by
the 1988 flood, which further deteriorated sewer systems and all hospital
infrastructure located in the basements, such as furnaces, laundry, and
storage rooms in the Metropolitan Santiago Area.
3.04      Hospital administrative practices are also outdated and impede the
adequate combinat.f on of input factors to extract good quality services from
limited resources. A wide range of modern management skills and systems are
lacking. Although ample health and financial data are collected, these are
not oriented to management requirements (e.g., the lack of information about
the costs of services makes it difficult to determine the amount of resourcen
that could be released by the reduction of some services or by the adjustment
of fees which could generate financing for other priorities). Also, data
processing and analysis are done manually in most hospitals hampering the
timely flow of information for decision-making and monitoring performance.
Another element affecting efficiency is the lack of established performance
motivation programs for increasing the productivity of both medical and non-
medical staff.
3.05      Inefficient use of resources in public hospitals is exacerbated by
limited resources. This affects all hospital activities, such as:
(a) emergency care for round-the-clock assistance for accidents and other
medical emergencies, due to the lack of technology necessary to respond to
current patient mix (e.g., trauma cases, neonatal problems); (b) outpatient
departments, because of insufficient equipment and staffing, especially weak
diagnostic capabilities, as evidenced by waiting lists and slow processing of
patients; and (c) hospital admissions, due to the inadequate capacity of
hospitals to provide timely secondary and tertiary inpatient care,
particularly for specialized services, such as surgery, orthopedics, and
cancer treatment.
3.06       ealth Finamcina.  Public health financing in Chile faces three
critical sets of issues: the under-investment of the public hospital network;
the distortions introduced by the application of the FAP and 7APRM resource
allocation mechanisms; and limitations of copayment arrangements in the public
sector to mobilize additional resources.
3.07      The general de_erioration of the public hospital network was caused
by a decrease in capital investments and maintenance budgets over the last two
decades despite population growth. Budget figures for the last decade show
declining allocations for hospital investments, maintenance, operating
budgets, and even staffing (see para. 2.30). This situation has translated
into acute shortages in equipment and supplies, insufficient mainteriance, low
staff morale an4 decreasing quality of services in the hospitals. Hospitals
continue to operate due to staff competence and dedication and the ingenuity
of hospital directors and administrators. However, the network has been
stretched to its limits. Now it is recognized that substantial investments
are needed to bring hospitals up to an acceptable standard of care.



14
3.08      The fee-for-service systems adopted for reimbursing hospitals (PAP)
and municipal facilities (FAPEM) were intended to direct money to where
services were actually being provided. The FAP is still partly effective for
the hospitals, but it has been eroded since PAP payments have not kept pace
with inflation and a larger share of hospital revenues has come from other
sources, including direct, supplementary central budget allocations. In
effect, these supplementary allocations centralize financial decision-making,
partly nullifying the decentralization attained by the fee-for-service system.
For municipalities, the incentive to hold down unit costs is completely gone
because ceilings were imposed on FAPEM transfers, converting them into block
grants. In addition, FAPEM ceilings began to be paid even to municipalities
which had not provided enough services to reach the ceiling. Two other
failings can be attributed to FAP and PAPEM. First, they set fees in such a
way as to encourage provision of curative over preventive care; and second,
they reimburse on the basis of each individual procedure rather than for
treatment or for management of specific health conditions; thus they encourage
multiple visits when not required as well as promoting overuse of any
treatment that is priced high, and under-use if prices do not cover costs. In
addition, municipalities have an incentive to refer patients to secondary and
tertiary facilities instead of treating them at the primary level because they
can reduce their expenditures without affecting their revenue.
3.09      Copayments for public sector services range from zero in the lowest
two income classes to 25 percent in the third and 50 percent in the top class.
Three-quarters of users are now in the zero-payment categories. This is due
to a combination of three factors: the fact that higher income groups do not
use public services, the system fails to adjust for real income boundaries,
and there is leeway in classifying patients. This situation hampers efforts
to mobilize additional resources for financing health care.
3.10      Institutional Caoacitv.  Managerial problems at the MOH and the
HSAs constrain sector development. Planning capability is weak, particularly
in regard to multi-year planning that responds to national policies and norms
while recognizing the sps-ificity of local conditions. Also, there is limited
capability for harmonizin.j short-term investment and operational needs with
longer-term investment rtquirements.  This is aggravated by the shortage of
staff with training and c-perience in strategic planning and budgeting.
3.11      The MOH and the PlAs in general do not exercise effective manage-
ment control.  At the centrh & level of the MOH, where detailed technical norms
and standards are developed ror all public health services in the country,
there is a lack of sufficient personnel to monitor compliance with norms and
standards in HSAs except thrc'u.h reviews of essentially quantitative reports.
In addition, there is no orga7i-:ed and systematic effort to monitor and assess
compliance from the point of view of quality of services. To date, much of
the transfer of administrative responsibility to HSAs remains more theoretical
than real. HSAs have limited latitude to adjust MOH norms and standards for
potentially important variations at the local level, and there generally are
not enough trained staff to super,rise activities at the service level.  While
supervision of activities at facilities under the administrative jurisdiction
of the HSA is good, most HSAs have far too few staff to carry out their
responsibility to supervise primary care at the municipal level.
3.12      In terms of personnel management, major issues include: (a) the
need to change the staffing pattern in the central and regional MOH offices in



15
line with the proposed changes in the public health system; (b) lack of
adequate incentives to ensure that qualified personnel serve in isolated or
difficult areas; and (c) need for adequate staff training to develop medical
expertise and management skills required by the changing the health system.
Training opportunities serve as incentives which can stimulate the interest of
qualified health personnel to be assigned in rural areas. Training is also
important to develop career paths in the sector. The most difficult
personnel-related issues are probably those found among staff working at the
primary-care level. Problems include the lack of job security in municipal
employment relative to employment in the MOH and HSAs; the absence of visible
career paths in health especially smaller or poorer municipalities; limited
opportunities for further training, given that many municipalities do not, or
cannot provide financial subsidies to staff for this purpose; and inadequate
compensation, benefits, and working conditions in poorer municipalities.
3.13      Chile has a comprehensive national health information system, which
is well established for collecting, consolidating, and forwarding information
to higher levels of the MOH. Decailed demographic, epidemiologic, healt;A
resources, and health services data are collected at all levels of the system.
Also, extensive financial data are compiled by FONASA on services rendered and
reimbursements provided. Little effective use of this large body of data is
made, however. An assessment of information currently collected is needed to
determine their necessity or priority considering the enormous staff time
required to complete numerous reports, thus reducing available patient time.
Other problems include the need to identify information gaps; to judge the
accuracy, timeliness, and usefulness of information; to analyze whether the
information is properly integrated (e.g., interrelation of activity data and
cost data); to determine whether available information is used for planning
and management purposes at various levels of the system; and to identify
resource needs to ensure an adequate information system.
3.14      Central SuoDDV Facility (CSF).  CSF was created by law in 1979 as a
public service institution subordinate to the MOH. Its legal status gives it
some functional flexibility but it is subject to all rules and regulations
governing the civil service. The Director is a presidential appointee.
Number and type of personnel are fixed and their management follows civil
service rules. The mission of CSF is to Durchase, store, sell, and dialribu&2
pharmaceuticals, medical supplies and equipment, and related items for the
institutions and services that are part of the NHSS. The HSAs have the
freedom to purchase from the provider that they prefer, both CSF and private
providers. In 1900 the HSAs purchased 38 percent of their supplies from CSF
with a range of 28 percent for Santiago's Metropolitan Central HSA to 62
percent for Magallanes. CSF is an important supplier for the distant HSAe
with small volume purchases where the private sector is not present. Finally,
it fulfills an important role in stocking life saving supplies for use in case
of national emergencies and natural catastrophes.
3.15      Currently the CSF faces a ser4es of operating problems, among which
the most significant are: (a) inability to set goals and carry forth
activities to achieve them; (b) insufficient flexibility of the organizational
and administrative structure to respond or adapt to the needs of users;
(c) deficiencies in inventory and warehouse management; (d) problems with the
system for transporting supplies; (e) information systems that do not
adequately meet manager's needs; and (f) lack of trained personnel in modern
management practices. This situation at the CSF is exacerbated by the fact



16
that the NSA units responsible for managing the procurement and distribution
of supplies at the local level suffer from lack of adequate resources to
perform their duties, particularly in terms of training, information systems,
and in many cases, availability of human resources.
3.16      National Institute of Public Health (ISP).  The Institute is
responsible for most matters related to pharmaceuticals, including
registration, quality surveillance, and control of manufacture.  It is also in
charge of ensuring the quality of food products for domestic consumption.
Additionally, the Institute produces vaccines and other biological products,
and functions as a reference laboratory, as a coordinator of public health
laboratories, and as a research and training center.  Numerous restrictions
constrain the effectiveness of the ISP, among these:  (a) insufficient trained
staff in modern management skills; (b) absence of information systems to
support management decisions and operation of multiple data base for drug
accreditation; (c) lack of flexible and dynamic systems for supply acquisition
and inventory management; (d) lack of information on the real costs of
services provided by the ISP; (e) absence of a policy for setting fees and
procedures for revising and for collecting them; (f) technological
obsolescence of equipment and inadequate maintenance of infrastructure and
equipment; and (g) inadequate incentives for personnel.
3.17      PA.  This program, initially launched in 1937 as the national
milk program, is widely credited with contributing to Chile's success in
reducing infant mortality and malnutrition. It covers approximately 1.2
million people (80 percent of infants under one year of age and 70 percent of
preschoolers 2 to 5 years of age, as well as pregnant and lactating women).
They receive monthly rations differentiated by beneficiary age and nutritional
status, and distributed via the public health system. PNAC's budget is about
US$56 million or the equivalent of US$47 subsidy per beneficiary per year.
Existing data consistently indicate that program benefits are most heavily
concentrated among lower income groups. Nevertheless, program data also
suggest that operational improvements are needed to reduce program costs.
Among them are: (a) improvements to targeting to concentrate on groups at
highest risk of malnutrition; (b) product development to ensure these goods
are accepted by intended beneficiaries; (c) improved nutrition surveillance
data for effective program management; and (d) improvements in procurement
practices for milk powder to minimize costs.
3.18      Adult Health and Environmental Proorams.  To date Chile has not
dealt adequately with its changing demographic and epidemiologic profile.
Support for preventive and/or early detection and treatment adult health
programe is weak. For example, screening programs to detect cervical cancer
at a sufficiently early stage so that treatment can be efficacious in reducing
the number of premature deaths among women have been limited. A major effort
is also needed for designing incentive mechanisms and education campaigns to
reduce the sources of air and water contamination which affect adversely the
health of the Chilean population.
3.19      Public/Private Linkaaes.  Formal public/private linkages in the
sector occur in three different areas: (a) the sale of services by public
hospitals to ISAPREs affiliates and private users under a cost recovery
scheme, particularly for the use of private rooms (mensionados) for inpatient
care; (b) the purchase of private health services by FONASA for rPS
beneficiaries; and (c) the regulatory role of the Superintendency of ISAPREs



17
in the private health insurance market. A major challenge in the 90's is to
expand the complementarity, as appropriate, of public and private health
provision. In line with this goal, there is a need to: increase efficiency
and equity in the financing and delivery of public and private health
services; mobilize additional resources for health; and improve consumer
choice. Efforts are now underway to improve the organizational and
administrative framework of the public health system, as well as to enhance
the provision of benefit packages to include both preventive and curative
care. Also, regulatory measures to standardize the eligibility criteria in
the private health insurance systsm are under consideration.
B. Government Sectoral Strateov
3.20      A high priority on the development agenda of the Government is to
improve the effectiveness, efficiency, and quality of health services
rendered, particularly to the poor. One of the major objectives is to achieve
a balanced health care delivery system, i.e., responsive to changing health
needs of adults and the elderly while maintaining adequate coverage of
programs for maternal and child health. The Government is resolved to
increase efficiency in the use of existing resources; provide incentives for
cost-savings by delegating most decisions on resource allocation to local
levels; and stimulate the increased participation of the private sector in the
financing and delivery of health services. In addition, the Government is
committed to increase total public health spending. Savings from efficiency
measures and wider cost-recovery would allow the Government to meet changing
needs without adding significant pressure on budgetary resources.
3.21      The principles of the Government's health sector development
strategy include: (a) guarantee of the population's basic right to good health
through access to quality health services; (b) promotion of the complementary
roles of public and private providers in the health sector; and
(c) strengthening the decentralization in the sector begun in the 19806.
3.22      The Government has taken some strategic decisions on the
modernization of the public health sector based principally on the results and
recommendations of some of the PPF and TAHRP - financed studies.  The major
strategies are discussed below. The corresponding implementation tools and
detailed activities are financed under the TAHRP (e.g. the pilot test of the
health financing schemes) or by the HSRP as discussed in Section V.
Reorientation of the Health System
3.23      In 1991    e MOH analyzed the country's health care delivery
system, focusing oin: (a) current and projected needs of the population; (b)
the functions and technological capabilities of health facilities at different
levels of care; and (c) the process of referring patients between levels of
care. The analysis showed the type and extent of service deficits in each
geographic area and the principal bottlenecks in the referral system. A
summary of the method used in this analysis is presented in Annex 4; detailed
analysis is part of the Project Files. Based on the findings, a set of
reforms were identified to make the system more responsive to the geographic
and demographic conditions, and the changing health needs in the HSRP project
areas. The recmmendations (Annex 5), adopted by the Government in July 1991,
have wide-rangir.g implications for the modernization of the public health
system and provide a much needed investment framework for the sector.



18
3.24       The thrust of the reforms for reorganizing the health care
delivery system is two-folded: (a) to bridge the gap between primary and
secondary care by establishing specialized outpatient services to support
referrals from primary care levels; and (b) to upgrade medical technology at
all levels of care. Since the present system provides primary care through a
network of low-technology dispensaries of varying size, and secondary care is
only available at the overcrowded outpatient departments of tertiary
hospitals, a new network of ambulatory care facilities has been proposed--
rural outposts, rural dispensaries, urban clinics, reference centers, and
diagnosis and treatment centers--to provide care of progressive complexity at
different levels of the system. The rehabilitation and upgrading of
hospitals, and the provision of resources for their appropriate operation and
maintenance will also be given high priority under the proposed new system.
The investment requirements of the proposed reforms in Metropolitan Santiago,
Antofagasta, and Llanchipal would be financed under the HSRP.
3.25      A major element of the reform is the creation of two new outpatient
specialized care facilities: the Health Referral Center (Centro de Referencia
de Salud. CRS), and the Diagnostic and Treatment Center (Centro Diaanostico y
Teraneutico. CDT). The CR is an outpatient center of medium technological
complexity to be located in large cities (more than 100,000 inhabitants) as a
free-standing facility or attached to a Type 2 or 3 hospital. This facility
is proposed to serve as a specialized referral center for 4-6 urban health
centers, covering on average a target population of 150,000 to 250,000'
inhabitants. The CRSs would be staffed with physicians trained in the four
basic medical specialties (internal medicine, pediatrics, OB/GYN, and general
surgery), psychiatric personnel, dentists, and nursing and auxiliary
personnel, and would be equipped with basic diagnostic equipment (e.g., X-
rays), laboratories for basic tests, including electrocardiography. The CDT is
a specialized ambulatory care facility located in the principal city of an
HSA, endowed with higher level technology (e.g., laboratories for specialized
tests, sonograms, CAT-scanners, and ambulatory surgery equipment) for the
provision of complex diagnostic and therapeutic services, such as oncology,
neurology, and traumatology. These facilities would be attached to a Type 1
or 2 hospital. The CDT staff would include physicians trained in all clinical
specialties, dentistry, and nursing and auxiliary personnel.
3.26      The new system would serve a great number of patients in health
facilities closer to their residence in those places currently lacking them,
with less time and travel costs incurred by the patients. By making
specialized services available on an ambulatory basis, it is expected that the
users of public health services would be encouraged to seek care at local,
lower cost facilities rather than overcrowding outpatient departments at the
tertiary care hospitals. The shifting of patients from hospitals to less
complex facilities may result in a more efficient service provision.
Decentralization of Health Service. Deliverv
3.27      Based on the recommendations of the TAHRP studies on the structure
and operation of NDH, CSF, and ISP, and in line with its decentralization
policy, the Chilean Governmant has decided to remove all executive
responsibilities from the MDH's central administratior and to implement the
MON's legal mandate to delegate service provision to its executing agencies:
the 27 RSAs, the CSF, and the ISP. To this end, MOH would strengthen the
organizational capacity of its central administration and of its executing



19
agencies to implement the decentralization of health services delivery in the
most cost-effective manner. The implementation strategy would consist of:
(a) redefining the MOH mission, accompanied by a change in its structure;
(b) introducing management tools to facilitate delegation of authority, to
enhance supervision and performance evaluation of HSAs; and (c) strengthening
and upgrading three key organizational resources: staff (numbers, skills),
financial resource allocation and transfer mechanisms, and technologies
(information and communication systems) (paras. 4.12-4.32).
3.28      Under the proposed decentralized scheme, the MOHWs central
administration would have a normative and supervisory role. MOH plans to
effect the delegation of service delivery to HSAs by establishing annual
Service Provision Aareements (Comoromisos de Gesti6n) with each HSA,
estimating the type and number of health services to be provided, and the
level of resource allocation and transfer payments, as well as for determining
performance indicators to monitor the mutually agreed annual targets (see
para. 3.31). The process of effectively delegating authority from MOH's
central administration to the HSAs and its autonomous agencies would be
gradual and would take several years to complete. The speed of implementation
would depend on a change in MOH's organizational culture, the introduction of
a few legal changes, the development of necessary support systems, especially
the training of senior and middle level managers, and the upgrading of the
information system. The CSF would be transformed into an autonomous self-
financing state enterprise. The managerial capacity of ISP would be
strengthened to increase its operational effic4ency and effectiveness to carry
out its public health mandate. The detailed reforms and the project support
for their implementation are discussed in Section V.
Health Financina Reforms
3.29      The Government has decided to replace the PAP and the FAPEM
resource allocation and transfer systems, and reform the copayment system for
public health services, with the goal of addressing the issues discussed in
paras. 3.08-3.09. Alternative inpatient and primary health care payment
systems have been designed on the basis of the recommendations of various
health care financing studies that were conducted with the financial support
of the TAHRP and reviewed and approved by the Government in August 1991.  In
view of the complexity of the proposed reforms, the Government would pilot
test the new hospital services payment system in select HSAs (Atacama,
Valparaiso-San Antonio, Viffa del Mar-Quillote, San Felipe-Los Andes, and
Llanchipal) during the 1992/93 period (Action Plan; Annex 6). The pilot tests
are financed under the TAHRP; subsequent nationwide implementation would
require minimal additional financing. Meanwhile, the proposed per capita
payment system for primary care has been incorporated in a draft law (Ley de
Atencion Primaria) recently submitted for Congressional approval. On or
before June 30, 1993 the Government and the Bank would review the status of
