the aforementioned pilot test, as well as the status of adoption of the new
primary care financing scheme and agree on appropriate subsequent actions
(Action Plan, Annex 6).
3.30      Per Cse Payment Associated with Diacnosis (PADs).  The current
system for transferring resources to the HSAs based on a fee-for-service
method (RAP) would be replaced with a Diagnostic Related Groups (DRGs)-type
per case system of payment associated with diagnosis (PADs) for secondary and
tertiary inpatient care for the majority of hospital patients. As a first



20
step toward designing the new system, a revised prospective fee schedule or
PPP (oacpo orosnectivog nor poestacfiC) was prepared based on the results of a
study on the actual production costs of 103 secondary and tertiary services
(these PPPs cover a large share of all individual services offered in the
public hospitals). While the PAP fee schedule covers all recurrent costs
except personnel (which, like investment, are now budgeted separately), the
PPPs would be based on the average full labor and non-labor input costs (other
than capital) for each of the services. The Government's rationale for going
from FAP to PPP is to adjust service fees so they cover all inputs except
capital, thus, eliminating separate payments for salaries. To encourage the
provision of quality health care rather than rewarding the over-provision of
services which generate revenue from fee-for-service payment, the definition
of outout in the public health system would involve paying for complete
treatments rather than for specific services. To this end, payments for the
discrete services would be grouped into fixed PAD payments for each diagnosis
as the basis for public hospital reimbursement.
3.31      As designed, the PAD system is based on an all-inclusive payment
rate for the care of a hospital patient over the course of the hospital stay
for 23 diagnoses. These 23 diagnoses represent 60 percent of all inpatient
stays, and were selected using the criteria of expense, frequency, and degree
of standardization of care of patients with such diagnoses. The PAD rate is
intended to cover the full cost of providing a norm of care based on expert
judgment (clirnicians in 13 selected hospitals were asked to identify the
services on the FAP list of 2,200 services which are requ!red in the care of
patients with each diagnosis, and the quantity of there services). For each
of the 23 diagnoses, therefore, an aggregate cost was estimated by applying
the prospective payment per service to each of the required services and
summing the total. Payment for the remaining hospital inpatient diagnoses and
specialized ambulatory services and procedures would be based on revised PAP
fees or PPPs until PADs for these services are established.
3.32      PADs would help link the planning process to resource allocation in
the production and delivery of services at the hospital level. The PPPs and
the PADs would be paid prospectively according to the Service Provision
Agreement, which becomes the planning instrument for the operation of the HSAs
(see paras. 6.16-6.17), and differences between services planned and those
actually provided would be reviewed at the end of each planning or payment
period. In the initial phase of the reform, however, the Government would use
only the non-labor input costs of the PPPo for transferring resources under
the new PAD system.  The MOH would continue to pay salaries until there is a
change in the law governing health personnel.
3.33      Prosnective Per Caoita P&amnt System For Primary Care.  The
resource transfer system based on a schedule of charges for individual
services (FAPEM) provided by primary care facilities at the municipal level
would be replaced by a fixed capitated rate system for paying the full primary
care of enrolled patients. On the basis of actual FAPEM outlays from a sample
of municipalities, the basic capitation rate would be set prospectively to
cover the full cost of a norm of care for each of four categories of primary
care (child care, maternal care, adult care, and oral hygiene). The estimated
number of users of each primary care service would be multiplied by the annual
recommended norm of visit frequency and services per user. The rate includes
the labor costs of providing the recommended level of services, adrinistratlve
costs, and a percentage allowance for non-labor input costs such as



21
pharmaceuticals. The estimated total costs of a primary health facility would
be converted to per capita costs by dividing by the area population. This
mechanism would offer providers an incentive to maintain the quality of
services rendered and to expand the number of affiliates.
3.34      Cooavment Systems.  The Government is adjusting the boundaries of
the income categories A,B,C and D for classifying FONASA beneficiaries, so as
to increase the number of affiliates who would have to pay copayments (see
para.4.09). The Government would not charge FONASA beneficiaries more than
what they are now paying in order that copayment percentages would be reduced
to offset fee increases the'. may result from the introduction of the PPPs and
PADs systems. Specific proposals for modifying the FONASA cost-sharing
arrangements are being reviewed, e.g.; copayment percentages, protection of
FONASA beneficiaries from medical expenditures too high relative to their
incomes possibly through FONASA loans to spread the cost over time, and total
catastrophic protection or zero copayment beyond some expenditure ceiling. To
redistribute some resources froli public hospitals, which generate high
revenues from FONASA beneficiaries' copayments and fees charged to private
patieuts, to those hospitals which are not able to do so, the Government is
reviewing another proposal that would tax away for redistribution a fixed
share of the discretionary revenues earned by hospitals. Under review also is
the proposed requirement that fees for private patients be a constant mark-up
over the PADs or PPPs, with each hospital free to choose its own mark-up. The
Government has already changed public hospitals' rules for charging private
patients the full amount of fees for discrete services so as to induce
hospital directors to tap the private market.
3.35      The proposed health financing reforms would lead to a more
equitable allocation of resources across geographic areas at the primary care
level, and at the secondary and tertiary levels, resources would be allocated
on the basis of actual utilization. The reforms would replace the current
systems based on historical levels. Over time the proposed systems are
expected to provide automatic incentives for improving efficiency and
productivity, and increasing quality of care at the lowest possible cost.
Strengthening of the Public/Private Mix
3.36      The interrelation between the public and private sectors will be
strengthened by encouraging sale and purchase of services between the sectors.
In addition, the ISAPRE law is being reviewed in Congress with the goal of
encouraging the development of a fully transparent private insurance market,
and to ensure that ISAPREs continue to cover those affiliates that develop
chronic conditions and the elderly.
IV. ASSISTANCE STRATEGY FOR THE SECTOR
A. Bank's Role and Strateay
4.01      In the 1980s the Bank's involvement in the Chilean health sector
consisted of the health components of a Structural Adjustment Loan and a
social sector report that analyzed selected aspects of the health system. To
support the Government's vision of a modern, efficient health sector in the
19906, the Bank will participate actively through its lending and economic and
sector work.  Bank's assistance will stress, among other things, improvement
in the quality, accessibility and efficiency of health carG while maintaining



22
fiscal discipline. The Bank's major role will be to encourage the Government
to forge ahead with its analysis, pilot test, and subsequent implementation
of many policy and institutional changes, which are also of paramount interest
to health sector development in many other middle-income and developed
countries.
4.02      The Bank's lending assistance {to the sector is through a two-stage
operation. First, the TAHRP (US$27 mill$.on loan), approved in December 1991,
will assist the Government in responding to urgent, small hospital
rehabilitation needs in Metropolitan Santiago (e.g. repair of pavilions,
bathrooms, roofs, kitchens, laundry rooms, etc.) while supporting the
definition and pilot-testing of critical policy reforms for the entire
country. Second, a policy-based investment operation,the proposed HSRP would
assist in the implementation of chosen policy and institutional reform
measures and in financing investment requirements for the modernization of
health services delivery in Metropolitan Santiago, Antofagasta and Llanchipal.
In essence, a portion of the TAHRP served as the preparation phase of the
HSRP, complementing the Project PreparrAtion Facility Advance (PPF) of
US$750,000 (approved in August 1990), which financed the first stage of the
policy reform studies.
4.03      Through these operations the Bank would actively participate in the
policy discussions in the sector. Also, the policy reforms being adopted
would serve as a framework for mobilizing complementary resources from other
donors, as well as for the Government's own investments in other areas of the
country. In fact, other multilaterr.l and some bilateral institutions have
referred to the PPF- and TAHRP- financed policy reform studies, as well as the
subsequent Bank-financed implementation of the reforms as a major
justification for their financing in the sector. Thus, the Bank's involvement
encourages sector investments to be made in line with much-needed system-wide
policy reforms.
4.04      The Bank's economic and sector work would also support the analysis
of new health issues facing Chile. The Bank is currently preparing an Adult
Health study focusing on the demographic, epidemiological, financial, and
institutional aspects of chronic diseases and injuries in Chile. The Study is
expected to be completed in the second semester of 1992.
B. The Role of Other Donors
4.05      The Interamerican Development Bank (IDB) has been the principal
source of external financing for Chile's public health system in the past
decade and a half. In 1978, IDB financed a National Rural Health Program with
a loan of about US$41.3 million. Thereafter, it provided two consecutive
loans for the FNDR under which the health sector received in aggregate about
US$64 million from 1984 to 1990. Another US$84 million is programmed for the
next four years. These nationwide investments were, and will continue to be,
directed mostly to the rehabilitation of, as well as equipping primary health
care facilities and tvoe 3 and 4 hosoitals nationwide (para. 2.30).
4.06      Commencing in 1990, the IDB and the Bank began to coordinate their
respective plans and design of assistance programs for the health sector.
These institutions complement each other in planned area coverage and scope of
technical assistance for the sector. Particularly in the case of technical
assistance, the Bank and IDB have agreed to adopt in their respective project



23
areas, policies or systems designed under projects being financed by the other
institution. The national policy and institutional reforms being supported by
the Bank-proposed loan help justify the physical investments to be financed
under the IDB projects. The active participation of the MOH's Office of
Multilateral Credit in the Bank and IDB project preparation missions ensured
that both institutions work in parallel to assist the Government in the
definition of its national health strategy.
4.07      In June 1991, IDS approved a project of US$100 million (loan of
US$70 million) to finance the rehabilitation and upgrading of hospital
facilities in Iquique, San Felipe-Los Andes and Valdivia (covering about 5.2
percent of the population), as well as studies on management, information,
human resource, and facility maintenance issues in these areas.
4.08      The bilate':al sources of assistance include USAID, KFW, and the
Governments of France and Italy. In 1990, USAID approved a grant of US$10
million primarily to assist in strengthening the delivery of services,
management, and infoxrmation systems at the primary health care level. France
and Italy approved assistance totalling about US$20 million for the purchase
of ambulances and other basic equipment for some health facilities. The German
Government is providing assistance in three stages. The first two, currently
under implementation (about US$31 million), are financing the rehabilitation
of secondary level hospitals in seven HSAs (outside the IDB and Bank project
areas). A third phase (US$16 million) would finance the construction of ten
health care centers in Metropolitan Santiago and a specialized ambulatory care
center (CDT) in Temuco. This phase would complement the Bank-financed
investments in Metropolitan Santiago.
C. Lessons From Past Exoeriince
4.09      Since the proposed project is one of the first Bank projects to
initiate health sector reforms combined with investments for secondary and
tertiary care facilities, an extensive review of past experience was
undertaken examining 37 Bank-financed completed and on-going health projects
with diverse objectives and design. The majority of these projects focused on
construction of primary health care centers. Bank assistance for hospital
projects on a large scale has barely started, with two projects in the EMENA
Region, one in the East Asia Region, and another in the LAC Region (Chile
TAHRP). These four projects are in the early stages of implementation. An
effort was made to review the lessons learned by other development
organizations in financing health sector reform and hospital rehabilitation,
and other relevant empirical studies in and outside the Bank.!'
2/ XSources include (i) 37 SARs, PCRs, Audit Reports and Bank Board notes on
health projects in LAC, Asia and Africa; (ii) World Bank reports
including: Case studies of oroiect sustainabilitv: implications for colicv
=an  M2rations from Asian exoerience by Michael Bamberger and Shabbir
Cheema,  Ogerational Policy Review: Comliance with Loan Covenants (Report
No: 4090), and Population. health. and nutrition annual sector review for
1990; (iii) the following peer review articles: Can they get along without
us?: sustainability of donor-sumoorted health oroiects in Central America
and Agrica by Thomas Bossart, Problems of imolementation by Peter Bowden,
Recurrent costs in the health sector of develonina countries by Catriona
(continued...)



24
General findinas.  A number of the above Bank-financed health
projects have been affected adversely by the following factors:
(a) An overly ambitious and complex design, which overloaded
institutional capacity for project coordination and implementation;
(b) Lack of borrower's full commitment to the project, leading to lack
of compliance with loan covenants; the situation is aggravated whenever
there is a change in government, often leading to change in priorities;
(c) Lack of organizational design that lends itself to quick decisions,
throwing the project in a quagmire of bureaucratic delays. A number of
projects tried to counter this problem by creating a special project
implementation unit in the Ministry of Health. While this expedited
decisions, it also led to communication problems, jealousies, and lack
of cooperation from the regular public sector staff;
(d) Lack of sustainability once the Bank loan terminates because of the
borrower's inability or unwillingness to finance recurrent costs;
Ce) In the construction of health facilities, projects ended up with
poor quality construction because of lack of supplies, inexperienced
contractors, and inappropriate building designs for the project area.
In large projects, the physical construction component often
overshadowed all other project components in terms of planning, budget
allocation, training, and supervision. As a result, infrastructure was
often built but not put into operation; and,
(f) Failure to establish a long-term monitoring and evaluation system
with which to assess the full impact of the project. This system should
not only evaluate the number of physical achievements, but also the long-term
distributive impact the project has had on general welfare.
4.10      Meanwhile, successful Bank-financed projects were characterized by
built-in flexibility, allowing review and revisions as the project progressed;
involvement of highly-qualified staff; high project visibility; and a
significant level of public support for the government's initiatives.
4.11      Sector Reform and Hospital Prolects.  A more specific review of
health sector reform and hospital rehabilitation projects found that the only
successful projects were those which clearly defined the role of hospitals and
other lower level facilities within a well coordinated referral system. New
facilities and services must fit logically into the health care delivery
system and be consistent with the overall health policy for the country.
1/(...continued)
Waddington and Margaret Thomas, and The elace of local social realities in
develonment work by C.R. Willson-Pepper; (iv) project reports from other
development agencies including: the Inter-American Development Bank
Summary of ex-post evaluations of public health oroarame, the Netherlands
Development Cooperation, Hoseital based health care; evaluative summar. of
Dutch supported activities in the field of hospital-based health care
1975-128g and the Overseas Development Administration, Review of ODA'S
Experience with Hogsital Proiects.



25
Also, the impact of the hospital rehabilitation projects depends as much on
the functioning of the health system as on the hospitals. In projects where
the lower level facilities were strong and accessible hospitals were
efficient; improvements in the integration of health care delivery often
played a key role. In several projects, overutilization of newly renovated or
constructed referral hospitalb was reduced by restricting admissions to those
referred from the lower level. One project in particular found that by adding
three to five beds to health posts the number of nonessential referrals to the
hospitals was reduced.
4.12      Also it has been demonstrated that large scale investment irn
infrastructure is not sufficient to improve health sector performance;
complementary measures are needed. There should be sufficient health,
maintenance, and management personnel, whc are given specific tasks.
Supervisory systems should be carefully designed and supervision ratios should
be such that on-the-job training is facilitated. In line with this, a well-
chosen management information system must be established. By rapidly
detecting workers ano health facilities that fall behind or perform poorly,
the system can assist in maximizing health sector productivity. Should the
system not contain financial information on the project hospitals, agreements
should be reached with the respective government to obtain these data in order
to monitor the hospitals and evaluate the impact of the project.
4.13      The TAHRP Connection.   While the TAHRP was approved only in
December 1991, some lessons can already be gleaned from its implementation.
As of June 1992, about 98 hospital rehabilitation subprojects (small, urgent
works and equipment purchase) amounting to about US$17.0 million have been
approved for TAHRP financing. Of these about 14 subprojects have been fully
implemented. The decentralized decision-making process for setting subproject
priorities and allocating budget has been a most striking feat. The HSA's
responsibility for these decisions, as well as for the actual subproject
preparation, subsequent procurement arrangements, and management of subproject
execution is proving to be an important training for their participation in
the HSRP. Also, this involvement and the concrete availability of investments
are creating a climate of receptiveness for the anticipated reforms under the
HSRP. As regards the reform studies, the TAHRP continued financing for the
detailed design and pilot testing of their recommendations is allowing the
Bank to accompany the evolution of Government's sector policy choices.
4.14      The HSRP Design.  Considering the above experiences, the design of
the proposed project emphasized the following characteristics: the project
links the financing of priority investments to critical policy and
institutional reforms in the Chilean health sector. The infrastructure
investments would implement a new Chilean health care model based on the
recommendations of TAHRP-financed studies (paras. 4.22-4.25). While the
rehabilitation/reconstruction of health facilities would claim the bulk of
project financing, these investments per se would not be the project's only
focus. The project's support for the pilot-tests and implementation of policy
and institutional reforms defined under the TAHRP would be essential for the
adequate functioning of these facilities.   Furthermore, the project would
bolster the development of health personnel management skills and efficient
information systems to strengthen the referral network from the primary to the
tertiary care level. The project has built-in flexibility in that the design
of most activities allows a test period or a period of consultation with users



26
and/or beneficiaries, a review with Bankt'  involvement, and revisions as
neceosary.
4.15      The proposed project facility investments were based on an
extensive diagnosis of the needs of their intended beneficiaries and the
status of the referral system in each of the project areas. In designing
these facilities, the HSA's, which would be the local level project
implementers, would take into account, through consultations, the perceived
needs of the users (i.e., the nompital staff who have day-to-day contact with
patients from the beneficiary communities). Lastly, to facilitate project
decisions at the central level, a PCU which has now gained valuable experience
during project preparation of both the TAHRP and this project would continue
to coordinate and monitor project implementation. The PCU is fully integrated
into the MOH management structure and makes all decisions with active
participation of MOH department heads to ensure the latter's full involvement.
V. TH PROJECT
5.01      In line with the Bank's assistance strategy for Chile's health
sector, the proposed HSRP would continue to pave the way for implementation of
reform measures and assisting in enhancing the health care delivery system in
selected project areas. The major activities and investments financed under
the TAHRP and the HSRP have been programmed as shown in Annex 7. Hence, while
the pilot-testing of some reforms (e.g. changes in the health financing
methods) is being financed under the TAHRP, the HSRP would finance the
detailed design and gradual nationwide implementation of complementary reforms
(e.a. HSA Service Provision Agreements, information system upgrade, etc.).
5.02      An HSRP project document was prepared by the MOH and submitted to
the Bank in January 1992. The Project was preappraised in January/February
1992 and appraisal was carried out on June 22 - July 3, 1992. Negotiations
were held in Waslington D.C. on October 5 - 7, 1992. The Chilean Delegation
included Messrs. Hernan Vega, Chief of Investment and Public Credit, MOF;
Hector Sanchez, Adviser to the Minister of Health; Cristian Baeza, PCU General
Coordinator; Pedro Crocco, PCU General Coordinator (effective October 19,
1992); and Carlos Manzi, PCU Administration Coordinator.
A. Prolect Objectives. Rationale and Area
5.03      Prolect Oblectives.  The proposed project would assist the
Government to enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of resource use in the
public health system, as well as improve the quality of health services in
selected areas. Specifically, it would:
(a) strengthen the decentralization of health services delivery
through reforms in MOHe mission and organizational structure, and enhance the
system of generating and transferring financing resources;
(b) upgrade the institutional organization and management capacity
for procurement and distribution of drugs and food supplements, quality
control of drugs and food products, as well as supervision of the public
laboratory network;
(c) improve the management and clinical skills of health personnel,
as well as the sector management and health information system; and



27
(d) implement a new health care model to develop specialized
ambulatory services at the secondary and tertiary levels, improve the
deteriorated conditions of selected hospitals, and fill the health services
gaps in eight HSAs in Metropolitan Santiago, Antofagasta, and Llanchipal.
These proposed actions would integrate health services delivery by effectively
supporting the primary care system, and increasing the responsiveness of the
health care system to the chanting needs of the population. The model would
also provide the bases for subsequent nationwide reorganization of the health
system.
5.04      Rationale_for Bank Involvement.  The project is in line with the
overall country assistance strategy to alleviate poverty within a tightly
managed fiscal policy. It would enable the Bank to accompany the Chilean
Government's implementation of critical sector reforms that are also germane
to health sector development in many middle-income and developed countries.
The significance of the Bank's involvement goes beyond the loan financing  e
Mf. The Bank's interactions with the Government, drawing from varied
experiences with other countries, has been viewed as providing helpful inputs
into sector policy decisions. Bank staff from the Latin America Technical
Department, the Asia Human Resources Divisions, and consultant specialists on
health issues have participated not only in the preparation of the project but
in intensive technical discussions with Chilean officials. The Bank would
help finance also a slice of the sector's investment program, complementing
the ongoing IDB and bilateral assistance, as well as Government investments.
Bank assistance in the project areas would benefit largely the indigent and
low-income population as these are the major usern of the public hospital
system (Annex 3 and para. 5.05i. The public hospitals in Metropolitan
Santiago also cater to low-income patients from other regions of the count:cy.
The improvement in the quality of health services of the public hospitals
would proceed in tandem with the implementation of an improved cost recovery
system desired both by the Bank and the Government.
5.05      Proiect Area.  The proposed project includes: (a) a national
program for technical assistance, specifically for the implementation of
sector policy and institutional reforms, training for management and clinical
skills improvement, and information system upgrading; and (b) a specific area
coverage, i.e., eight HSAs in Metropolitan Santiago, Antofagasta and
Llanchipal, for investments in health care facility improvements. The
characteristics of these HSAs and their health network problems are described
in Annex 3. Project inteiventions in the eight HSAs are illustrated in the
attached maps (IBRD #23753 and #23754). Approximately 40 percent (about 5.0
million) of the total Chilean population, mostly from the indigent and low-
income groups, would benefit from improvement in the quality of care in the
public hospital system in th'se project areas.
B.  MAin Features of the Proiect
5.06      The proposed project constitutes the second phase in the Bank's
lending assistance to the sector. Project components presented below are
described in detail in Section C. The project's four principal components
are: (a) Decentralization of Health Services Delivery; (b) Facility
Investments for a New Health Care Model; (c) Support for Special Programs; and
(d) Project Administration.



28
5.07      The DeMputralization of Health Bervices Delivery component accounts
for less than 16 percent of the total project cost, but is a critical feature
of the proposed operation.  It would support the reorganization of the KOH;
the implementation of the delegation of authority to the HSAs through a
Service Provision Agreement incorporating, among others, new financing schemes
and improved cost recovery measures; conversion of the CSF into an autonomous
agency; strengthening the ISP; training of sector personnel; and upgrading the
sector management and health information system. The project would finance:
consultant services (US$1.3 million); training (US$7.6 million); computer
hardware and software (US$15 million); and operating costs (US$19.5 million).
5.08      The Facility Investments for a New Health Care Model component,
accounting for 82.5 percent of the total project cost, would enhance the
integration of the health services delivery in eight HSAs by: (a) establishing
ambulatory care centers (CRS and CDT) at the secondary and tertiary level
complementing the primary care services; and (b) rehabilitating,
reconstructing, or constructing, as needed, hospital facilities and health
care centers to fill identified gaps in the health services delivery network.
The project would finance the investment and incremental recurrent
expenditures of the facilities shown in Table 5.1. Proposed expenditures
would consist of: civil works (US$140.1 million); goods and equipment (US$67.6
million); and operating costs (US$32.1 million). Also, the project would
finance consultant services (US$ 3.0 million) to assist the HSA directors and
staff to execute the project.
5.09      Supoort for Special Programs (i.e. Cervical Cancer Program and
Environmental Health Education Program) while accounting for less than one
percent of project cost, would support the Government's efforts to address two
of Chile's major health concerns - cancer and environmental pollution. The
project would finance equipment and operating costs for these programs.
5.10      For Proiect Administration, support for a PCU (1.5 percent of total
project cost) would be provided to assist the MOH to coordinate and manage
project implementation, provide advisory services to the HSAs, and undertake
monitoring and evaluation.
C. Detailed Proiect Descri2tion
5.11      The following sections describe the first three project components
including the detailed implementation strategy, activities, and financing

Component 1: Decentralization of Health Services Deliverv
Redefinition of the NOH Mission
5.12      The MOH mission would be revised to focus on normative and
suervigory functions. Service delivery functions would be delegated to
organizational levels and service institutions that are closer to the users.
Specifically, the NOR central office would: (a) emphasize its responsibility
to formulate national health policies, strategies, programs and standards for



29
the purpose of guaranteeing all citizens the right to health care and ensuring
efficient use of resources; (b) ensure a comprehensive and harmonious
development of the Chilean health system; and (c) enhance the supervision,
evaluation and ongoing improvement of its strategies, programs and standards.
Thus, the KOH central office would no longer provide health services. This
dramatic shift reverses a conflict (i.e. responsibility for both oversight and
delivery of services) that has existed since the very first day of WOH
establishment. The project would support this change through assisting the
process of modifying the NOH structure and implementing the delegation of
authority to executing agencies, implementing a human resource development
program, and upgrading the sector management and health information system.
Details are explained below.
Ndification of MOH Oraanizational Structure
5.13      The proposed new MNH organizational structure (see organizational
chart in Annex 8) would consist of three divisions and an administration unit
reporting to the Undersecretary of Health: (a) the Health Programs Division
overseeing the definition of health norms and standards; (b) the Budget and
ilannina Division continuing the existing planning and budgeting function for
the human, financi&l, physical and data resources of the health sector, as
well as directing the evaluation of MOH institutional strategies; (c) a new
Operations Division created explicitly to facilitate and coordinate relations
between the HSAs and MOH in the implementation of the decentralization policy;
and (d) an internal Administration Unit consolidating all administrative
support functions. The Operations Division would be divided into five
sections with each one assuming responsibility for a number of HSAs. An
outline of the main functions of these Divisions is discussed in Annex 8.
5.14      Three Coordinating Committees would support MNH's proposed revised
mandate: (a) an Executive Committee presided by the Minister and consisting of
the Undersecretary and all Division Chiefs which would decide on health sector
strategy and policies including resolutions to emergency problems such as
labor conflicts, disease outbreaks, etc.; (b) a Develogment Committee also
chaired by the Minister and attended by the Undersecretary and the Chief of
the Budget and Planning Division which would analyze and decide on matters
related to sector quality, sector resource needs (i.e. human, infrastructure,
financial and technology) and sector investment allocations; and (c) an
operations Committee chaired by the Undersecretary which would decide on
operational issues with the Division Chiefs of the Programs, Budgeting and
Planning and Operations Divisions, including approval of the Service Provision
Agreements with the HSAs. Depending on the issues on the agenda each
comnittee would invite the Directors of the four autonomous services: FONASA,
CSF, ISP, and ISAPRES Superintendency.
5.15      The establishment of an Operations Division, and the three
Coordinating Committees responds to the challenge of making the decentralized
structure operational and ensuring a smooth implementation of the new policy.
The new Division would imply hiring eight new staff. The MNH has started to
create the Operations Division starting November 1992. Assurances were
obtained durino neaotiations that the new MOH structure and revised functions
would be in oneraticn on or before July 1. 1993. In addition, with project
assistance the MOH would prepare an amendment to Law No. 2763 of August 9,
1979 to ratify the changes in the MOH mission and organizational structure.
This amendment would require Congressional approval with simple majority. The



30
loan would finance about 45 man/months of consultant services (approx.
US$90,000) to draft the legal amendment and its detailed justification, define

The MOH restructuring would be accompanied by a training program for senior

Deleaation of Authority to Executina Agencies (HSAs)
5.16      Service Provision Acareements with HSA.  The delegation of MOH
service delivery functions to the HSAs is proposed to occur through annual
Service Provision Agreements between the MOH and the HSAs on the type and
volume of services to be provided and X the level of payment. The key
concept guiding this mechanism is to link performance with allocation of
resources. Performance would be monitored through a mutually agreed upon set
of indicators applying a "management by exception" rule in which only values
falling outside a predetermined range are analyzed and corrective action is
taken. Considering that the Service Provision Agreement would be a now
experience for the MOH and the HSA management, its application would be
carried out gradually starting with a few selected HSA9. Implementation would
be initiated in 1993 in the five HSAs currently subject to the pilot test for
the new health financing schemes (para.3.29). Possible inclusion of other HSAs
in the initial year would depend on existence of appropriate managerial
capacity and suitable information base to allow a performance review. Results
of periodic evaluations of t3e Agreement's implementation would be taken into
account as it is progressively applied to the other HSAs.
5.17      The detailed performance indicators to be applied under the Service
Provision Agreement would be designed by the MOH with project support, i.e.,
about 29 man/months of consultant services would be needed to analyze and
recommend specific indicators, prepare the methodology and reference benchmark
data for the chosen indicators, draft a Service Provision Agreement, and
design the initial implementation. Terms of Reference for these consultants,
some of whom have already been hired, are part of the Project File. Annex 9
shows a sample of process, impact, and exogenous indicators being analyzed,
and the general plan for the initial implementation phase. Considering the
different ongoing efforts in Chile to estimate the cost-effectiveness ratios
of a series of interventions in the areas of smoking, cervical cancer, and
accidents; to determine the actual cost of the d4fferent programs in the MOH;
and to establish modern cost and financial accounting information systems
under the proposed project, the Government expects that by the Project's Mid-
term Review a proper assessment of the feasibility of introducing cost-
effectiveness criteria in the Service Provision Agreement can be conducted
(para.7.20). In the meantime, the Service Provision Agreement would not
incorporate cost-effectiveness indicators. During negotiations, the
Government presented to the Bank a guideline for the execution, including the
contents, of a prototype Service Provision Agreement. This guideline
(Comoromisos de Gestion. Seotiembre 1992) is in the Project Files. Assurances
were obtained during negotiations that the Government would start the initial
imolomentation 2hase of the Aareement in at least five NSAs no later than



31
May 15. 1993. and raview with the Bank the results of this phase no later than
December 31. 1993.
5.18      Autonomy for the CSF.  To address the problems discussed in
paras.4.14-4.15, the Government proposes to take the following actions and the
proposed project would assist in their implementation: (a) prepare the change
in the legal status of CSF to make it an autonomous state enterprise and
thereby minimize present administrative and financial constraints; (b) develop
a strategic plan clarifying its organizational mission and values, assessing
the opportunities and threats in its external environment, assessing the
strengths and weaknesses of its internal environment, identifying strategic
issues facing it, and formulating strategies to manage the identified
strategic issues; (c) update its information and communication systems and
develop the corresponding operational plans for optimal use of these
technologies in providing a customer-oriented service; (d) improve producti-
vity and lower costs through improved human resource management, warehousing,
inventory management, and transportation systems; and (e) review its credit
policies, billing and collection procedures, profit margins, and cost and
pricing policies to improve profitability. While the legal amendment is being
prepared and discussed, detailed designs would be prepared and, as necessary,
actions would already be initiated to simplify the CSF's operating procedures
and upgrade personnel skills and information systems. Detailed justification
and description of the above actions are part of the Project Files.

muman Resources Develooment and Manaaement Programs
5.21      Essential for the success of MOH's decentralization policy is
training, managing and motivating the people who will guide and implement the
policy at the central level and at the level of the executing agencies.  The
proposed project would pay special attention to the development of the
organizational capacity of the public sector to train, retrain, absorb and
retain the required number of skilled senior and middle level managers.  It
would also strive to foster an organizational climate that helps managers and
other health workers to be productive and to derive satisfaction from helping
people in their q_ast for a healthy life.
A. Human Resources Develooment Prociram
5.22      The proposed project would support a Training Progtam to develop
human capital needed by the public health subsector in the short and medium
term.  NH and its four autonomous agencies, the HSAs, and the municipal
health services would be beneficiaries of the Program. Three types of
training programs would be offered: (a) senior management training; (b) middle
management training; and (c) clinical training. A fund of about US$7.2
million would be set aside to finance the training requirements of the
participating institutions over the six years of the project. The specific
training activities would not be pre-determined by the MOH. Nonetheless,
illustrative samples of training activities have been defined and are part of
the Project Files.
5.23      Four principles underlie the design and organization of the
TJraining Program: (a) provide support for the decentralization policy; (b)
ensure the effectiveness of training actions; (c) pursue efficiency in the use
of training funds; and (d) provide a stable structure and consistency with
M0H's mission and the goals of developing human resources. The Training
Program's decision criteria and administrative arrangements are summarized in
Annex 10. The participating institutions would present annually a set of
training projects using standard formats that would be evaluated according to
five transparent criteria. Three criteria would be related to national
priorities (level of care, inpatient vs. outpatient care, consistency with
national policies) and two criteria would reflect the priority and impact of
the projects as judged by the proponent (project priority and impact). An WOH
evaluation c-mmittee would rank the projects according to a pre-established
points system and recommend approval or disapproval. In line with the policy
of decentralizing decision-making, the points system would gradually assign
more importance to the priorities and the judgments of the proponents and less
weight to criteria that permit the central level to influence the evaluation
process. The details of the Training Program operational procedures are
contained in a Training Program Instructions Manual (para.7.05).
5.24      A Training Program Secretariat would be set up in the NOH staffed
by one full-time program manager, two part-time professionals and a full-time
secretary. The operational cost of the Secretariat is estimated to be about
US$460,000 during the project period. Annex 10 presents the Secretariat's
Terme of Reference which have been reviewed by the Bank and were found
satisfactory.   The settinci up of the Training Prociram Secretariat in the MOH
is a condition of loan effectiveness.



33
B. Human Resources Manapement Program
5.25      In order to foster an increasingly effective decentralization and
retain physicians in the public sector, two major functions would be
emphasized: appropriate and adequate staffing of health care delivery
institutions, and developing an effective organizational environment. Apart
from the strategic planning, management, and clinical training to be offered
under the project, the MOH would also aim to augment job satisfaction by
improving working conditions, communication systems, team work, team
leadership, work teams organized on the basis of performance and not status,
positive reinforcement, training and professional development.
5.26      The MOH would also institute a system of manpower planning and
review of the staffing patterns in all service delivery institutions. Current
staffing is based on a historical pattern and would be changed to reflect the
health needs of the target population. The project would finance a study to
develop a manpower planning methodology to determine the personnel structure
to be adopted by HSAs. The methodology would first be tested in a few HSAs
and then applied by the remaining HSAs. The terms of reference of this Study
is in the Project File. Consultant services needed to undertake the study and
the test would cost about US$115,000. Assurances were obtained durina
neaotiations that the results of this study and an action vrogram for the
imnlementation of its recommendations would be furnished the Bank no later
than June 15. 1993. The action oroaram would be carried out no later than
January 15. 1994 (Action Plan. Annex 6).
5.27      Current staffing patterns of all MOH service providers are fixed by
law. This includes the number anL type of staff by level of service. A
hospital director or an HSA director cannot hire additional staff except on
short-term contracts, or remove staff except for grave shortcomings in
performance, or increase efficiency by substituting or interchanging one type
of staff for another type. This rigid staffir.g system imposed by the rules
governing the civil service has been and continues to be a serious constraint
to increasing productivity. The ultimate goal of decentralizing the public
health sector is to change the HSAs into state enterprises with maximum
autonomy and self determination providing affordable quality care. A number
of project activities would put the public health sector on the road towards
reaching that stated goal. The project would be a bridge betweer the current
unsatisfactory situation and the desired situation of giving HSA or hospital
managers an unfettered opportunity to manage their resources efficiently and
effectively. It would develop the required support systems, i.e., skilled
managerial and technical staff capable of working with modern equipment,
creating an organizational climate fostering performance oriented work
behavior and team work, and modernizing and upgrading the physical
infrastructure and the equipment of the service delivery facilities.
5.28      In order to respond to the increased and varied activities that
would be generated under the project (institutional changes in KOH, CSF, ISP
and reorientation of the HSA delivery system), the Government has programmed
the creation of 1,340 new staff positions for the public sector and, under the
existing legal framework, a reassignment of approximately 4,500 staff
positions within their respective central departments or HSAs. The planned
areas of assignments of the new and reassigned staff, as well as the timing of
implementation are shown in Annex 11. Assurances were obtained durina
negotiations that not later than March 1. 1993 the Government would start



34
carrying out a proaram satisfactory to the Bank to recruit and reassian staff
(as shown in Annex 11). and review with the Bank not later than June 15 and
December 15 of each year startina June 15. 1993 the progress of carraina out
such grogLrm.  The MOH central office would undertake to hire new permanent
staff or reassign staff until the HSAs are legally empowered to do so.
C. Strengthen Suooort Technoloaies
5.29      Improvement in the quality and timeliness of sector information
systems would be a prerequisite to the successful implementation of the policy
and institutional reforms, especially for revised health financing schemes and
the Service Provision Agreements. This project would support the Government's
plan to strengthen the information systems through administrative reform,
introduction of computer-based information systems, and staff training.
Existing operational and administrative procedures need to be changed before
computerization can pay off, and staff need to be trained to understand the
potential of computers and become skilled in their use. These changes would
be carried out within existing legal framework during project implementation.
5.30      The project would thus support an Information Development Proaram
for the central MOH, the HSAs, the CSF, and the ISP. The Program would go
beyond the gradual procurement and installation of computers in sector
institutions. It would reform administration and streamline operations in
these institutions. Management leadership and staff cooperation would be
critical success factors in achieving the Program's objectives. Important
aspects of the implementation strategy are: (a) an initial design phase which
would include up-front planning, analysis, and design necessary to ensure
long-term integration of information systems and technology; (b) gradual
implementation of defined system priorities consistent with the absorptive
capacity of the participating institutions; (c) execution of agreements
between participating institutions and MOH to ensure the provision by these
institutions of adequate staff time, resources, and facilities for the
Program's implementation; (d) procurement by participating institutions of
qualified system engineering services to develop new systems; resulting
software would be owned by the institutions for use in many sites at no extra
cost; and (e) substantial investment in training to adopt the technologies and
work methods required by the new information systems.
5.31      The Programa would be implemented gradually over a four year period.
Basically two types of systems would be designed: a common system for all the
HSAs which would be centrally designed and complementary systems which could
be designed on a decentralized basis depending on the needs of each of the
participating institutions. The central design of the common systems however
would not be carried out by the NOH but by a sample HSA. After a test phase,
this design would be replicated in other HSAs. The PCU would be responsible
for coordinating the activities for the design of the common and
complementary systems. This coordination would be important to capture
obvious economies of scale in procurement, avoid unnecessary duplication of
efforts, and ensure compatibility in the information systems of the sector. A
corollary feature of the new program would be the modernization of the
telecommunication system through project-financed investments in new radio,
telephone, computer, and electrical power equipment. The modern system would
be important to carry and track messages, documents, distribution lists, and
pending actions between the NOH, the SEREMIs, the HSAs, CSF, and ISP.



35
5.32      The detailed institutional problems, program activities,
implementation schedules, and outcome ina cators proposed for the major
systeme to be developed in the MH0, the HSAs, the CSF, and the ISP are
discussed in detail in Annex 12. The total cost of the Informatics
Development Program is estimated to be about US$15.4 million of which
equipment would be about US$15.0 million.
Component 2: Facility Investments for the New Health Care Model
5.33      The project would finance facility investments for the
implementation of the new health care model as discussed in paras. 3.22-3.25
Eight HSAs have been identified as priority areas for investment: one in
Antofagasta (Region II, northern Chile); one in Llanchipal (Region X, southern
Chile); and six in Metropolitan Santiago. The facility and services problems
encountered in the project areas are summarized in Annex 3; detailed analyses
are in the Project Files. The following sections describe the implementation
strategy for the new model, and the costs of facility improvements shown in
Table 5.1. The implementation priorities and schedule are discussed in
Section para. 7.06.

NhTROPOLITAN SANTIAGO
5.34      Metropolitan North HSA.  The principal solution chosen to address
the service deficits in this area is to replace the 142 year old San Jose
hospital (see Nap). Specifically, the project would: (a) reconstruct and
reequip the 400 bed San Jose hospital; (b) set up a CDT facility adjacent to
the reconstructed hospital; and (c) set up an emergency ward for adults in a
designated wing of this hospital. These activities would cost about US$49.6*
million. Total operation and maintenance budget of San Jose after
construction completion is projected to be US$10 million per year reducing



36
significantly the present US$14 million annual recurrent costs.
Implementation would not disrupt the existing poor services as the San Jose
Hospital has ample land to build the new hospital without stopping the
operations in the old one. The physical transfer of beds and equipment in the
new San Jose Hospital would be carried out in stages to minimize service
disruption. Also, the CDT would be in operation shielding the hospital from
outpatient demand.
5.35      Metrooolitan Central HSA.  The project would assist the
organization of the health care delivery system in this HSA. The project
would concentrate secondary care services in one establishment to avoid
unnecessary costs of duplicating staff and equipment maintenance, as well as
uncoordinated service to the users, both of which result partly from the
existing geographical location of secondary care services in this HSA. The
project would finance the: (a) establishment of a CDT to replace San Borja
Hospital's outpatient department by completing and equipping an existing
facility annexed to the Hospital's main building; (b) establishment of an
emergency service for adults in San Borja; and (c) construction and equipment
of a CRS in Maipu, twelve kilometers west of the San Borja. This CRS would
meet most needs for secondary care for the peri-urban population and would
refer the more serious cases to the CDT. (see Map).  The Asistencia Publica
Hospital would then be relieved from elective cases and could concentrate on
emergency services -mostly surgical- for the entire Metropolitan Santiago, the
role for which it was built and equipped. The investment required for the
above construction and equipment purchase would be about US$16.9 million for
the San Borja Hospital and an additional US$5.4 million for the CRS Maipu.
5.36      Metropolitan West HSA.  The reform strategy to address the service
deficits in this area would be to strengthen the ambulatory facility in San
Juan de Dios Hospital while establishing centers at the periphery. The
project would support: (a) the creation of a CDT in the San Juan de Dios
hospital; (b) establishing an emergency service in this hospital; and (c) the
construction and equipment of a CRS in Pudahuel at the junction of the two
main roads leading to Santiago from the West (see Map). These interventions
would cost about US$21.2 million.
5.37      Metronolitan East HSA. The target districts in this HSA are those
where the low-income population lives; i.e. La Reina, Peffalolen, and Macul.
These people seek secondary care at Del Salvador Hospital. This overcrowded
and underequipped hospital cannot meet the demand and the deficit translates
into delays and/or rejections. The objective is to redistribute the Del
Salvador Hospital bed capacity to a new facility located closer to the
residence of the beneficiaries, and at the same time improve critical sections
of the Hospital. Hence, an intermediate echelon in Peflalolen (Santiago
Oriente), about midway to the Del Salvador/Calvo Mackenna Hospital complex
would be established (see Map). This intermediate echelon would provide
secondary care, both ambulatory through a CRS and inpatient care at a new Type
IX hospital (Peffalolen, Santiago Oriente). The planned new hospital would
have 200 beds, more than half of which would be for maternity care. The Del
Salvador's bed capacity would be reduced accordingly. The new hospital would
also have an Emergency Unit and an annexed CRS which would cover the need for
secondary ambulatory care. These interventions would cost US$15.4 million.
The Del Salvador Hospital outpatient capacity would be strengthened through
the rehabilitation and reequipping of its emergency unit and other clinical
services (US$5.0 million).  Also, the project would finance the rehabilitation



37
of surgical wards and referral center of the Calvo Mackenna Hospital (US$3.0
million).
5.38      Metronolitan South HSA.  The survey of health care facilities in
this HSA showed the worst situation in the entire Metropolitan Santiago area
in terms of physical facilities, support infrastructure and medical equipment.
The strategy for this HSA consist of: At the central level: (a) construction
and equipping of a CDT in the Barroe Luco Trudeau Hospital, replacing several
operationally inefficient sections; and (b) rehabilitation and reequipping of
selected services in this Hospital's adult emergency, surgery, internal
medicine, and maternity departments. At the peripheral level, the following
investments would be necessary: (a) construction and equipping of a CRS to be
annexed to Bl Pino Hospital; and (b) reconstruction and equipping of the El
Pino intermediate hospital (see Map). The total investment cost would amount
to US$42.6 million.
5.39      Metropolitan Southeast HSA.  The reform strategy includes: (a)
upgrading the Sotero del Rio Hospital, with replacement and modernization of
critical areas, specifically: (i) establishment of a CDT in the Sotero del Rio
complex; and (ii) replacement of the surgical ward and remodelling of hospital
wards for private patients; and (b) the creation of a new 400 bed Type 2
hospital with an Emergency Unit and a CRS in the densely populated district of
San Ramon. The San Ramon Hospital would serve both the South and the
Southeast HSAs as it would refer patients to both the Sotero del Rio and, as
necessary, to the Barros Luco hospitals. Considering the communication and
transportation lines between these two hospitals, the San Ramon Hospital would
help shield these two major hospitals from increased demand for their services
(see Map). Investment cost would amount to about US$38.4 million. Because of
the creation of a new hospital complex, the operating budget for the whole HSA
is projected to double over a ten year period.
ANiTOFAGA
5.40      The only two major hospitals in the Antofagasta region (Hospital of
Antofagasta and Hospital of Calama) need to be medically self-sufficient
because they serve as reference centers to the adjacent regions in this HSA
(Iquique and Copiapo) (See map). While the outpatient departments of the two
major hospitals do not exeitly meet the description of CDTs, the CDT concept
could be implemented without additional infrastructure investments.
5.41      However, there are severe deficits of medical equipment that impede
the operation of secondary care facilities.  The situation is especially
critical in the Hospital of Antofagasta and the Hospital of Calama compared to
the lower level facilities. A comparison of present demand and supply for
services, projections of future demand, and simulations assuming several
levels of additional equipment showed that the projected services deficit
could be met with investment in replacement/new equipment costing about US$5.3
million which would be financed under the project. This equipment would be
used for the ambulatory services and for the inpatient sections of the two
hospitals. The equipment list, which has been reviewed and found satisfactory
by the Bank, is part of the Project Files.  The provision of new/replacement
equipment would improve the timeliness and precision of diagnosis, shorten the
average hospitalization stay, and reduce the need for Antofagasta's population
to travel to other cities for required medical treatment. Operating and



38
maintenance costs, estimated to increase by about 4 percent per year, are duly
considered in the projected operating budget of the Antofagasta HSA.
LLANCHIPAL
5.42      To improve the health referral system in Llanchipal (especially to
diminish the gap in secondary care), it would be critical to carry out a
physical-functional rationalization of the Puerto Montt Hospital and the
Castro Hospital (see Map). The proposed upgrading of the Puerto Montt
hospital would enhance its diagnostic-therapeutic capacity, increase its
secondary care coverage, diminish the risk of intrahospital infection, and
augment the productivity of its general services and, thus, the
complementarity of health care services in the region. The proposed project
interventions in this hospital would complement the upgrading of the primary
care facilities and lower level hospitals under the IDB-financed FNDR project.
The proposed project would finance: (a) construction of an additional surgical
block; (b) replacing the heating and laundry systems; and (c) replacing most
of the advanced medical equipment. Refurbishing of critical sections of the
Castro Hospital would also be financed. These interventions would amount to
about US$9.5 million.
Facility Maintenance
5.43      High priority would be placed on the maintenance of existing and
new facilities that would be financed under the project. The maintenance
requirements, estimated at about 4 percent per year of the replac6-. ent value
of infrastructure and 3 percent per year of that of the equipment, would be
financed under the project. Maintenance would be carried out either through
the existing arrangements in the HSA's with installed capacity or
subcontracted to private services. The Government is currently carrying out
an IDB-financed Study on the National Engineering and Maintenance System for
the Health Sector. Recommendations of this study are expected to lead to a
more efficient and flexible maintenance organization for health facilities
nationwide. The Study is anticipated to be completed by May 1993. A summary
of the Study's terms of reference are part of the Project Files. The
adequacy of the facility maintenance budget would be assessed during the
annual budget reviews and the Project Mid-Term to take into account the
results of the IDB-financed Study.
Conwonent 3: Special Proarams
Cervical Cancer Program
5.44      To reduce the high incidence of women mortality due to cervical
cancer, the MOH Cervical Cancer Program (CCP) would be strengthened to: (a)
cover women aged 25 to 64 years, focussing on those aged 35 years and over;
(b) ensure the effectiveness of the diagnostic procedures through quality
control, supervision and training; and (c) ensure timely treatment of detected
cancer cases. At present the program covers approximately 20 percent of the
target group (about 2.4 million). By 1995 the CCP aims to cover about 50
percent, and by the year 2000 about 80 percent. It is estimated that the
Program could attend to about 90 percent of the detected cases either through
ambulatory services or inpatient surgery. The other 10 percent would be too
advanced and mdy receive only palliative therapy. Projections of the demand



39
for the services that would be required for cervical cancer prevention program
(e.g. consultations, laboratory tests, surgery) are in the Project Files.
5.45      The project would allocate about US$2.2 million for the CCP to
finance: (a) operational costa for pap smear tests in primary health care
centers with trained staff (matrona) in the country's 26 HSAs; (b) equipment
and operational costs to increase the capacity of 16 laboratories of
cytopathology for the analysis of pap smear tests; (c) equipment and
operational costs of colposcopy and cervical biopsy in the 26 HSAs, and
anatomopathology in 24 HSAm to strengthen the capacity for diagnosis of women
with positive pap smears; (d) equipment and operational costs of hospital
gynecology services for treatment of detected cancers in the project areas;
and (e) equipment for a national reference laboratory to be set up possibly in
the Hospital Del Salvador in the Southeast HSA. The standards used for
determining the scope of project support, and the location of the laboratories
to be financed are summarized in Annex 13.
Anvironmental Health Education Proaram
5.46      To address the growing concern about the adverse health effects of
air, wat .., and food contamination, the project would support a two-year pilot
environmental health education program. The project would primarily finance
an information and education program (US$202,000) on the prevention,
management, and control of environmental health hazards.  The pilot program is
anticipated to have a multiplier effect on the development of public awareness
of the environmental health problems (their causes, severity, and preventive
measures). The pilot program would be implemented by the NGHIS Environmental
Health HSA (Servicio de Salud de Ambiente) in Metropolitan Santiago.
5.47      The pilot education program would be carried out in four
communities in Metropolitan Santiago (San Jose de Maipo, Pirque,
Independencia, and Las Condes). The program would train concerned government
authorities, technicians, and field monitors (monitore) on the serious health
effects of environmental contamination. The field monitors would be
representatives of dwellers in the selected communities. At the end of the
vwo year pilot phase, about 120 technicians and 1,880 field monitors would
have been crained. An evaluation of the program would be carried out by the
last quarter of 1994. The different phases of the pilot program and
implementation plans are in the Project Files.

projected price increase for foreign costs estimated in US dollars at 2.8
percent for 1992, 3.9 percent for 1993 and 1994, and 3.8 percent for 1995 and
thereafter; and (iii) estimated changes in the exchange rate.
6.03        R,ties and Taxes.  Locally procured goods are subject to a value-
added tax (VAT) of 18.0 percent. Imported goods such as medical and
industrial equipment, vehicles, and computers are subject to 11.0 percent of
customs tax and 18.0 percent of VAT. Total cost of the project includes
US$55.2 million of local taxes.
B.  Finapcing Plan
6.04      The proposed loan of US$90 million would finance 37 percent of
total project cost net of taxes and duties. It would cover US$48.8 million,
39 percent of foreign exchange expenditures, and about US$41.2 elillion or 28
percent of the estimated local costs, net of taxes and duties. The Government
would finance US$153.6 million or about 63 percent of the total project cost,
net of taxes and duties, and 100 percent of taxes and duties (US$55.2
million). Table 6.2 shows the financing plan by project categories.

C. Recurrent Costs Arisina from the Proiect
6.Os      Total recurrent costs arising from the project activities would
amount to about US$52.8 million or 18 percent of total project cost. The



42
incremental recurrent costs that would continue beyond project completion
would amount to about US$20.0 million per year, i.e.,: (a) local salaries with
an annual cost of about US$13.8 million; and (b) other operating costs,
including maintenance, with an estimated profile of US$6.0 million per annum.
These costs represent about 2.3 percent of the 1992 MOH budget. The Ministry
of Finance (MOF) has reviewed the implications and agreed with the programmed
expenditures. Assurances were obtained durin. neaotiations that no later than
Aucust 31 of each year durincr the execution of the orolect. the Government and
the Bank would exchange views on the availability of staff and the amounts
reauired to carry out the nrolect for the followino year. Also. not later
than October 1 of each year durina project execution. the Government would
furnish the Bank for its comments the oronosed oublic seatcr health budoet for
the next succeeding year. The incremental financial needs would be offset
partly by: (i) increased revenues from the higher number of paid beds in
public hospitals, improved cost recovery system, and more accurate cost
accounting of services, among others; and (ii) savings from more efficient
management of personnel and supplies, reduced demand for inpatient care due to
availability of well-equipped ambulatory services, and reduced average length
of stay in hospitals.
D. Proiect Sustainability
6.06      The required financing for the proposed project including the
incremental recurrent cost and the detailed expenditure schedule has been
reviewed extensively and agreed to by the MOF. In fact, the Government chose
to finance a higher share of the project cost reflecting its ability to absorb
a significant demand for both local and foreign exchange resource
requirements. Chile's steady economic expansion and record of tight fiscal
discipline bode well for its continued capacity to sustain the required
expenditures beyond the project period. Durina neaotiations. the Government
furnished the Bank a Health Policy Letter outlinina the Government's medium-
term aoals for the sector. the sector reform 2roaram. and the imolications of
these reforms on sector financina (Annex 15). Also, a financial simulation
model developed for the sector using different assumptions for macroeconomic
and health financing parameters showed that projected public financing for the
sector to the year 2000 could support the incremental recurrent expenditures
implied by the programmed sector investments (including donor and budget-
financed projects; see Annex 2). The MOH would use the model to monitor and
update annually the implications of programmed sector investment expenditures
on the overall sector budget.
VII. PROJ83CT IMpL8MGATION
A. Prolect Manaaement and Coordination
7.01      The KOH would be responsible for overall implementation of the
project. To assist in the oversight of project implementation, provide
technical assistance to the HSA staff, and monitor and supervise the project
activities to be implemented by the MOH central office, the CSF and the ISP,
the organisation and functions of the existing PCU in the MOH were amended.
The PCU was originally created by Ministerial Resolution Nos. 2087 and 1317
dated December 31, 1990 and August 29, 1991 to coordinate the implementation
of the TAHRP and to continue the preparation of the HSRP. IDurina
negotiatons. the Government furnished the Bank Ministerial Resolution No. 530
dated September 25. 1992 amending the previous Resolutions in order to include



43
the PCU responsibilities under the HSRP. and to place the PCU under the
supervision of Budaet and Planning Division of the MOH. The new organizational
chart and terms of reference of the PCU are shown in Annex 16. The PCU
consultant staff would be hired on a need basis; the number of PCU staff, is
programmed to decline gradually during the implementation period.
7.02      The restructured PCU would be headed by a General Project
Coordinator who would be assisted by four unit heads in charge of the
following areas: operations; design and technical supervision; institutional
development; and administration and finance. During implementation, the PCU
would comprise a total of 6 senior level professionals, 30 technical staff,
and 7 support staff. The major functions of the PCU would be to: (a) direct
and provide technical assistance, as well as monitor and supervise the
implementation of the different components of the TAHRP and the HSRP; (b)
monitor the implementation of the policy and institutional reforms; (c)
supervise the goods and services procurement by the HSAs, and coordinate the
financial flows for project implementation; (d) implement the pilot programs
financed under the TAHRP; and (e' provide the Bank with a quarterly report on
the physical and financial status of project implementation (Annex 16).
Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the Government would
maintain the PCU lith a Proiect Coordinator and staffing satisfactory to the
Bank. The revised PCU terms of reference and the detailed operational
directives related to PCU responsibilities for project implementation are
contained in a PCU Operations Manual reviewed and agreed with the Bank.
Government's adoption of the PCU Operations Manual is a condition of Loan
effectiveness.
7.03      The executing agencies, i.e. HSAs, CSF, ISP, FONASA and the ISAPRE
Superintendency, would be responsible for the execution of their respective
project subcomponent. The MOH would sian an umbrella Subsidiary Aareement
with each of the executing aaencv covering aeneral obligations anplicable to
all oroiect comoonents in which these institutions are involved with terms and
conditions satisfactory to the Bank.  The signing of the umbrella Subsidiary
Aareements would be a condition of first disbursement for each of the
participating institutions. In additign. a separate oroiect aareement would be
Sian sd_f-ore disbursing for each subcomoonent (e.a. specific hospital work.
eguipment purchase) to be imolemented by the participating institution. Under
the Subsidiary Agreement the respective institution would be responsible for
the execution of the specific subcomponent and the MOH would transfer as a
grant the Loan proceeds required to finance such subcomponent. The specific
counterpart contribution of the institutions would also be specified. Drafts
of the umbrella Subsidiary Agreement and specific project agreement were
reviewed by the Bank. The PCU would furnish these institutions with an HSRP
Imolementation Manual, which provides the technical norms, administrative,
budgetary and procurement procedures to be followed by the institutions. A
draft of the HSRP Implementation Manual was reviewed by the Bank. Its
adoption by the Government is a condition of Loan effectiveness.
7.04   To guide the implementation of the Environmental Health component, the
Government prepared an Environmental Health Program Imolementation Manual to
be used primarily by the Environmental Health HSA. This Manual describes the
this HSA's responsibilities under the HSRP including budget allocation
procedures, accounting and auditing norms, reporting, and use of standard
forms. The draft Manual was reviewed by the Bank during negotiations. It
adoption by the Government is a condition of Loan effectiveness.

7.05   With regard the Training Program, the Bank reviewed during appraisal a
draft Training Proaram Instructions Manual which contains the indicative
training areas for each participating institution, as well as the technical
criteria and operational guidelines that should be followed in the application

Training Instructions Manual is a condition of Loan effectiveness.
B. Implementation Schedule
7.06      The project is planned to be imp3emented over a six year period,
i.e. 1993 - 1998. Most of the key activities under Component I
(Decentralization of Health Services) are anticipated to be compieted in the
first half of the project period. The target dates for start and/or
completion of these  activities are reflected in the Action Plan (Annex 6).  A
detailed implementation schedule for the Information Program is shown in Annex
12. With regard Component II (Faci.ity Investment for the New Health Care
Model), the Government has prepared an implementation plan for the execution
of civil works summarized in Annex 17. The preliminary drawings of the CRSs,
CDTs, the El Pino Hospital and the San Jose Hospital have been reviewed and
found satisfactory by the Bank. Schematic plans for the San Ramon and
Santiago Oriente Hospitals were reviewed during negotiations; preliminary
drawings would be submitted for Bank's approval during the first semester of
1993. The sites selected for the new facilities are satisfactory to the Bank.
The Bank has received proofs of site acquisition and surveys for most of these
facilities. The construction of CRS El Pino and CRS Cerro Navia would start
in the second semester of 1993; the rest would begin in early 1994. The
construction of the CDT Sotero del Rio, CDT San Juan de Dios, CDT San Jose,
and CDT San Borja Arriaran would start in the first semester of of 1994.
Hospital construction/reconstruction would start in 1995.
C. Procurement
7.07      The PCU would be responsible for monitoring all procurement
activities and for ensuring compliance under Bank Procurement Guidelines. All
bid documents and contract awards to be carried out by the NOH, HSAs, CSF and
ISP for their respective project activities would be subject to prior review
and approval of the PCU. Since there are no specific procurement laws in
Chile, the bidding documents for both international and local procurement
agreed with the Bank regulate the bidding procedures. The International
Competitive Bidding (XCB) and Local Competitive Bidding (LCB) standard
documents for works and goods were reviewed during negotiations and found
satisfactory by the Bank. Procurement arrangements are shown in Table 7.1 and
an estimated procurement timetable is shown in Annex 14, Table 6.
7.08      Civil Works. Civil works for the construction of the CRSs, CDTs and
hospitals as well as rehabilitaiton of hospital facilities would be managed by
the HSAs. Bank-financed civil works contracts valued at US$5.0 million and
above per contract would be awarded through ICB. Bank-financed civil works
valued at less than US$5.0 million and more than US$50,000 per contract
totalling about US$31.7 million equivalent would be awarded through LCB
procedures satisfactory to the Bank. Civil works less than US$50,000 per
contract totalling about US$2.0 million, which would be awarded following
local shopping procedures, would be financed entirely by the Government.
