7.09        Gagds and Materials. The bulk of goods and materials consiets of
hospital equipment and spare parts, vehicles, and office equipment and
supplies. Because of Chile's open market conditions, most reputable foreign
suppliers of hospital equipment are represented in the country assuring a fair
amount of competition and on site capacity for maintenance uervices for the
goods to be purchased under the project. Also, because of the project's
decentralized implementation in eight HAs, equipment procurement would be
mostly through LCB with average contract amount of about US$170,000. However,
purchases of similar types of hospital equipment and spare parts would be bulk
packaged as much as possible. Bank-financed goods and materials contracts
valued above US$250,000 per contract would be awarded following ICB with local
manufacturers being granted a preference for bid evaluation purposes in
accordance with the Bank Guidelines (May 1992). Bank-financed contracts
valued between US$250,000 and US$35,000 per contract totalling about US$49.4
million equivalent would be awarded following LCB procedures satisfactory to
the Bank. Local shopping procedures, which are satisfactory to the Bank,



46
would be used for individual contracts valued at below US$35,000 per contract
(e.g. CRS, CDT, hospital, and PCU equipment and supplies) totalling about
US$11.0 million.
7.10        Servicea.  Consultants services for the PCU, the detailed design
studies under Component I (Decentralization of Health Services Delivery), and
the monit-rKng and evaluation studies for the Mid-term Review would be
contracted following Bank Guidelines (August 1981).
7.11        Prior Review.  The Bank's prior review of procurement
documentation for civil works and goods will cover all major procurement steps
(advertising, bidding documents, evaluation reports and award recommendations,
and contracts), and would apply to: (a) all contracts under ICB; (b) all LCB
contracts for works above US$2.0 million; and (c) for every year of the
project, the first LCB contract for goods above US$100,000 for each of the
participating HSA.  All other Bank-financed contracts would be reviewed exa
post in the field during supervision missions.  The prior review process would
cover about 80 percent and 30 percent of the total value of Bank-financed
contracts for civil works and goods, respectively.  These review levels are
appropriate taking into account Chile's open market conditions (para. 7.09)
and the fact that standard bidding documents approved by the Bank would be
used for LCB procurement.
D. Status of Preparation
7.12        The Government would be ready to start the local bidding
procedures for civil works and equipment purchase in the last quarter of
CY1992. Some of these activities would be eligible for retroactive financing.
The Government has also progressed in hiring consultants to prepare the
detailed design of the institutional reforms and some of the civil works.
S. Disburseents and Accounts
7.13        The proposed project is expected to be completed by December 1997
and the loan fully disbursed by June 1998, i.e. over a period of six years in
line with the Bank's average country profile for Chile. The estimated loan
disbursement distribution is shown in Table 7.2; the quarterly disbursem nt
profile is shown in Annex 14, Table 7. The operational experience gained by
the PCU since October 1990 for the preparation and subsequent implementation
of the TAHRP would facilitate disbursement flows of the loan proceeds.
7.14        The PCU would manage the disbursement of the loan funds. The PCU
would oversee the authorization of the transfer of required loan financing to
the budget of the HSAs, the CSF, and the ISP. The PCU would review and keep a
summary of the disbursements to facilitate requests for Bank reimbursements of
the special account and subsequent loan audit. The HSAs, the CSF and the ISP
would keep all the records and supporting papers required of each expenditure.
Annex 18 illustrates the different steps anticipated in the budget,
procurement, and disbursement cycle involving the PCU and the participating
institutions. Appropriate models of internal Government memoranda and forms
needed to obtain the required decisions to allocate funds from one level to
the next have been drafted and incorporated in the PCU Operations Manual.
7.15        Withdrawal applications for expenditures under contracts valued
below US$2.0 million for civil works and US$250,000 for goods would be based



47
on statements of Expenditures; supporting documentation would be retained by
the PCU for review by Bank supervision missions and for annual audits. All
other disbursements would be fully documented.
7.16        SRecigml Accgut.  To facilitate timely project implementation, the
Government would ostablish, maintain and operate, under terms and conditions
satisfactory to the Bank, a Special Account in US dollars at the Central Bank
of Chile. The Bank would make an initial deposit of up to US$5.0 million, the
average four month project financing requirement, to the Special Account upon
request. Project expenditures would be monitored by the PCU. The PCU would
request replenishment of the Special Account on the basis of the standard
disbursement procedures.

7.17        Retroactive Financin.  The project would provide retroactive
financing of up to US$5.6 million (five percent of the proposed loan) for
expenditures incurred after February 15, 1992. Retroactive financing would
allow the early start of the detailed design of some institutional and policy
reforms, the hiring of short-term consultants to provide technical supervision
for the design of medical facilities, the refurbishing of some hospital
facilities, e.g. emergency unit of the Hospital San Ramon, and equipment
purchase for ISP and the Cervical Cancer Program.

G. Prolect Monitorina. Evaluation and Supervision
7.19        Monitoring and evaluation would include two sets of indicators:
(a) progress indicators that would reflect the smooth and timely
implementation of project activities; and (b) outcome indicators related to
project objectives. A list of these indicators is discussed in Annex 19.
Additional work is being done by the PCU to develop reference benchmark data
for each of the indicators. The corresponding benchmark reference data would
be sent to the Bank no later than April 30. 1993. and. as appropriate. qualitv
of care and efficiency of health services indicators (Action Plan. Annex 6).
Overall monitoring and evaluation of the project would be the responsibility
of the PCU. Specific tasks include: (a) preparing semestral report on the
physical and financial status of the hospital rehabilitation works to be
submitted to the Bank no later than the first week of the following semester;
(b) preparing annual implementation progress report and work plan, which would
be submitted to the Bank by December 30 of each year; (c) reviewing and
updating every six months the Action Plan; and (d) preparing a Project
Completion Report (PCR) that would be submitted to the Bank no later than six
months after the closing date of the Loan.
7.20        In addition, the Bank and the Government would hold  a Mid-Ter,
Review by June 15, 1995 to evaluate, among others, (i) the status of adoption
and execution of the different policy and institutional reforms; (ii) the
implementation progress of the hospital rehabilitation and construction works;
(iii) the adequacy of available and programmed sector financing; (iv) the
adequacy of the facility maintenance budget considering the results of the IDB
study (para.5.43); (v) the feasibility of incorporating cost-effectiveness
criteria in the Service Provision Agreement; and (v4) the project's estimated
benefits as reflected by the progress and outcome indicators being developed
for the project (para. 7.19).  Mid-term evaluation studies, as needed, would
be financed under the project.
7.21        The project is anticipated to require intensive supervision
because of its distinct policy-based design and the large scale hospital
facility construction and rehabilitation works. Supervision arrangements are
expected to require about 18 staffweeks in the first year, 15 staffweeks in
the second year, and 12 staffweeks thereafter.



49
H. Bnvironmental Imoact
7.22        The project would not have any significant adverse impact on the
environment. Standard safety procedures would be followed in the
implementation of the civil works, and the installation and operation of the
medical equipment supplied under the project. Procedures for safe handling of
hazardous wastes and materials (e.g., disposable needles) would be included in
continuing education programs for health personnel. Also, the project's
environmental health component would enhance the public's awareness of the
health effects of environmental conditions in and outside the project areas.
The Goverrment furnished the Bank an action plan (summarized in Annex 20) to
address the project's environmental concerns.
I. Imnact on Women
7.23        The upgrading of facilities and medical equipment under the
project would be gender neutral. However, the capacity of health facilities
to manage appropriately complications of delivery (the major causes of
maternal mortality) would be improved through upgrading of physical
infrastructure and equipment. In addition, the project's support for an
increased coverage of the Cervical Cancer Program would generate specific
benefits especially directed towards women (paras. 6.45-6.46).
VIII. &XPECTED BENEFITS AMD RISKS
A. Benefits
8.01        By supporting critical policy and institutional reforms and
priority health facility investments, the project, in conjunction with the on-
going TAHRP, would help improve the efficiency of resource use in the public
q*alth sector, enhance the quality of ambulatory and inpatient care,
strengthen the capacity of the system to respond more effectively to the
emerging health needs of an aging population, and increase the complementarity
of public and private providers. The project facility investments are
estimated to benefit approximately 40 percent (about 5.0 million) of the
Chilean population mostly belonging to the indigent and low-income groups.
The sector reforms intended to increase the health system's efficiency coupled
with improvements in cost recovery measures would generate benefits nationwide
in the long term. An integrated health services network and improved delivery
of cost-effective health care interventions would help diminish mortality and
morbidity from chronic diseases and injuries.
B. Riske
8.02        The major risks include: (a) the complexity of reforms in the area
of health financing; (b) the long-term horizon of the sector reforms, most of
which would be fully implemented either towards the end of the current
Government's tenure or during the next administration; and (c) the lack of
project implementation experience, especially of some HSAs and hospital
managers. These risks would be minimized by the close supervision by the Bank
at the different stages of the execution of the reforms; institution of a Mid-
Term Project Review by the Bank and the Government to evaluate implementation
progress; and technical assistance and supervision to be provided by the PCU
to the HSAe, as well as the training under the project. Also, the ongoing
TAHRP implementation is providing valuable hands-on training on project
management to HSAs that would be involved in this project.



50

agreement would be signed before disbursing for each activity (e.g. specific

institution (para. 7.03).

Annex 2: HEALTH SECTOR FINANCING
A. 3ISTORICAL  UDU
1.        Total public and private health expenditure* in Chile are
approximately VS$2 billion per year or about 6.4 percent of the gros dmestic
product. Total public expenditures in the health sector account for about 39
percent of all health expenditures, while prlvate expenditures account for
about 61 percent of total health expenditures. Table 1 shows the total public
expenditure in health for tho 1980-1992 period in relation to gross national
product and to total central government expenditures. while there have been
fluctuations in both of these relationships, the general trend from 1980 to
1989 in public spending for health care was downward. This was particularly
true for the 1985-1990 period.
2.         However, with the new government's *phasis on the social sector,
public expenditures in health began to rise. In 1991 expenditures increased
to 10.5 percent of total central government expenditures higher than any other
year in the previous decade. After adjusting for inflation the variation in
public spending in the health sector from 1990 to 1991 was +27 percent. In
1992 public expenditures in health will remain relatively high at an estimated
9.4 percent of the total government budget.

3.           Chart 1 further illustrates annual public sector health
expenditure trends. The augmentation of public spending in the health sector
beginning in 1991 is being financing by an increase in gov rnment revenues
from taxes. This increase in tax revenues can be attributed to three faetors:
(i) strong economic growth which has expanded the tax base; (ii) improved tax
collection; and (iii) a reform in the tax law increasing the tax rate.

5.        Table 2 demonstrates the changes that have occur-ed over the last
ten years in the sources of funding for public expenditures in health. The
most significant change has been in the distribution of financing from
treasury allocations and from mandatory payroll deduction tranfers.  In 1980
treasury allocations were the primary source of funding and financed 54.5
percent of all public health expenditures. By 1990, however, treasury
allocations financed only 38.7 percent of all public expenditures in health.
Treasury allocations to health as a percent of GP declined from 1.4 percent
in 1980 to 0.9 percent in 1990 (Table 3).   In contrast, the importance of
transfers from the mandatory payroll deductions increased during these years.
In 1980 transfers financed 28.4 percent of all pubic expenditures in health.
By 1990 transfers were the primary source of funding and financed 45.0 percent
of all public expenditures in health. However, it should be noted that
treasury allocations began to increase in 1990 as a result of the government's
new policies.

6.       The decline during the 1980s in the importance of treasury
allocations as a source of funding for public expenditures in health was also
reflected in annual health expenditures per beneficiary (Table 4). Although
total government health expenditures per beneficiary remained relatively
constant from 1980 to 1992 fluctuating between 16.7 and 21.7 million pesos
annually, treasury allocations to the health sector per beneficiary fell from
10.1 million pesos in 1980 to 6.9 million in 1989. This clearly demonstratem
the emphasis during this period of reliance on payroll transfers as opposed to
central government financing. In 1991 and 1992, after a decade of decline,
annual treasury allocations per beneficiary rose close to early 1980 levels.

7.       The impact these increased finances will have on public sector
health expenditures can be seen in Table 5 and Table 6. Table S illustrates
the break down of public health expenditures over the last decade.  As is
typically the case, the largest share of health expenditures is spent on
salaries. In contrast, the proportion of public expenditures for investment
were consistently low from 1980 to 1990 resulting in severe deterioration of
the health system infrastructure. However, in 1992 it is estimated that
health care expenditures for investment as a percent of all health expenditure
will dramatically increase from 2.4 percent to 7.9 percent of total health
expenditures. The increase in government funding for public expenditures in
health for each category of expenditure is clearly demonstrated in Table 6.

B. FIXNNCING OF TWE NEl LTK SECTOR REFORM PROJECT
8.        In order to determine the capacity of the Chilean government to
finance the incremental recurrent costs that could arise from the health
aector reform investments a financial simulation was undertaken. For this
study a model was developed utilizing assumptions about future flows of income
and expenditures in the health sector. In running the computer analysis three
separate economic scenarios were considered: (i) pessimistic; (ii) basel and
(iii) optimistic.
9.        Using these three scenarios two different series of simulations
were run. The first set of simulations focused solely on determining the
capacity of the government and the health sector to finance the incremental
recurrent costs associated with the utilization of external financing for a
portion of the health sector reform investments. WLth this as the goal, this
analysis concentrated only on the period 1992 to 1994. The second series of
simulations were done to determine the capability of the health soctor to

Annex 3:    IH STAI U AND EALTH
SiE:RVCES NEDTWORK IN MIETROPOL0A
SANTIAGO, ANTOFAGASTA, AND
LLANCHIPAL
The investment component of the project would be implemented in
three regions: Metropolitan Santiago, Antofagasta, and Llanchipal. These
regions were selected on the basin of demographic, epidemiological, and
socioeconomic criteria, as well ao on health service infrastructure needs in
each region. The GoverAnment's investment program for 'che public sector as a
whole, including the support from bilateral and multilateral organizations,
was also taken into account.
Overview. Notwithstanding Chile's substantial achievements in
improving the health status of the population, behind the national averages
are wide variations between the health status of different population groups
as reflected in the health indicators of the HSAs in Metropolitan Santiago,
Antofagasta, and Llanchipal --the proposed project areas. The efficiency and
effectiveness of the public health services network in these three regions are
constrained by a lack of specialized ambulatory services and by the decaying
hospital infrastructure. In various hosopitals, particularly in Met-iopolitan
Santiago, some wards have been decommissioned as unsafe, while others are
below normal standards for the type of services performed in such facilities.
Outdated medical equipment exacerbates this situation severely affecting
hospital performance. Support services such as water supply and heating,
sterilization, laundry and kitchen services, as well as sanitary facilities,
are also in serious disrepair. Maintenance of buildings and equipment is a
serious problem throughout the system. Discontent over the lack of
specialized ambulatory care facilities, both for diagnostic and treatment
services, and low quality of hospital services is widespread among low and
middle-income groups who are dependent on the public system.
A. metropolitan Santiago (NS)
Contrary to its name, NS covers rural as well as urban areas. It
extends far beyond the city limits and covers an extended area of
15,348 sq. km., reaching 40 km. north and 80 km. south of downtown Santiago.
NS is comprised of 6 provinces (Santiago, Chacabuco, Cordillera, Maipo,
Melipilla, and Talagante) and 51 communes. Municipal administration is fairly
decentralized; each municipality has ius own mayor and municipal council,
collects local taxes, and runs social services, including primary health care
facilities.
MS total population of 5.1 million in 1990, or approximately 40
percent of the country's populatior., is projected to rise to 5.8 million in
1995 as the result of the growth of satellite cities and neighboring rural
communes.  About '0 percent of the population is rural and is partly scattered
through the Andean and coastal mountains. A recent study has shown that two



70                     Annex 3
Page 2 of 18
different socioeconomic groups coexist in MS: the high income dwellers of
Northeast Santiago and the low-income population of the other districts.
Marked differences separate these two groups in terms of income, employment,
family composition, housing, sanitation, health and nutrition, and overall
quality of life.
For reasons of size, manageability and distribution of municipali-
ties, MS was divided into six health service areas (HSAs): North (570,000
pop.); West (961,000 pop.); Central (595,000 pop.); East (1 million pop.);
Southeast (925,000 pop.); and South (989,000 pop.).  Each of these areas
generally is demarcated by major thoroughfares radiating from downtown
Santiago, with their extension corresponding to the spatial dispersion of the
population. Overall, more than 60 percent of the MS total population is
beneficiary of the public health system.
Health Status. When infant morcality rates are disaggregated by
MS's 6 HSAs, variations appear reflecting social disparities, with the highest
rates in 1989 in relatively low-income Metropolitau West HSA (17.6/1,000),
Metropolitan North HSA (17.2/ 1,000), and Metropolitan South HSA (15.6/1,000).
The vast majority of infant mortality occurs in the perinatal period. Within
Metropolitan North, West, and South HSAs, 14.2, 12.4, and 10.7, respectively,
out of every 1,000 new-borns die before reaching 7 days of age. Variations
also exist in the maternal mortality rate (MMR), ranging from 0.5/10,000 live
births in affluent Metropolitan East HSA to 3.5/10,000 live births in poorer
Metropolitan North HSA. The rate of maternal deaths from miscarriages/
abortions varies considerably between HSAs in Santiago, with rates above the
national average (1.6/10,000 live births) observed in Metropolitan Southeast
HSA (2.7/10,000) and Metropolitan Central HSA (2.6/10,000). Chronic diseases
and injuries are leading causes of death in all 6 HSAs.
Health Facilities. The MS has 155 primary health care facilities
inder municipal control -nd 28 public hospitals, of which there are 17 Type 1
d Type 2 facilities (Table 1). These hospitals have 70 percent of the total
number of beds in the region. The 1.9 ratio of public hospital beds per 1,000
population in MS is much lower than the country's average ratio of 2.4 (Table
2). Most MS hospitals are over 60 years old (Table 3); little investment has
taken place over the last 20 years. Reflecting wide disparities in health
service availability, access to primary care facilities and public hospitals
varies greatly within MS (Tables 4-5).
The Metropolitan North HSA, with 26 health posts and centers,
provides primary health care services to its intended beneficiaries. In 1989,
it had an averapoG number of general and specialized consultations of 3.1 and
0.57 per person per year, respectively (these figures are above the national
average). This HSA also has 5 hospitals (4 Type 2 hospitals and one small
Type 4 hospital), and a ratio of 1.4 beds per 1,000 population. Three of the
four Type 2 hospitals are specialized pediatric, psychiatric, and oncological
facilities that serve as reference centers nationwide; the other Type 2
facility is the 160 years old San Jose Hospital which has reached a stage of
physical dilapidation beyond repair. Most of the secondary care and tertiary
care needed in this HSA is referred to centers in other HSAs in KS because of
the lack of secondary care facilities and the limited diagnostic and treatment
capacity of the San Jose Hospital. Less than 10 percent of its total
population has access to private health coverage.



71                      Annex 3
Page 3 of 18
The Metrogolitan Central NSA, which covers downtown Santiago and
two peri-urban districts, occupies a midway position in terms of hospital
resources with 2 beds per 1,000 population (it has one Type 1 and one Type 2
hospital). However, its 10 primary care f£cilities are insufficient to meet
the demand for basic ambulatory care, and access to secondary diagnostic and
treatment services is limited. As a result, in 1989 this HSA performed only
2.4 and 0.25 primary care and specialized medical consultations per person,
respectively, and the emergency room of its Type 2 Asistencia Publica hospital
was overburdened by cases which could have been treated at lower level
facilities. This situation is further aggravated by the lack of an outpatient
department in the Type 1 San Borja Hospital.
The Wuat L. , with a third of its population living in rural areas,
is the largest H8A in US. The average income in this HSA is one of the lowest
in MS, and it includet large pockets of population in extreme poverty. There
is a good balance between its 43 primary care facilities and 7 hospitals of
different levels of complexity (one Type 1, three Type 2, including one
specialized trauma hospital that serves the entire region, two Type 3, and one
Type 4). In 1989 it performed 2.8 primary care consultations, and 0.55
specialized medical consultations per person. However, secondary care for
ome specialized services is limited and available only in two large
hospitals, the San Juan de Dion and the Felix Bulnes, which are severely
deteriorated and underequipped. This HSA is also short of hoopital beds, with
a bed population ratio of 1.7 per 1,000.
The Matronolitan Bast HSA covers a relatively well-endowed, high-
income area. More than half of the population receive medical care from
private providers. The rest of the population in this HSA relies on the
public sector's 13 primary care facilities, a large Type 1 hospital, three
Type 2 hospitals, including two specialized hospitals for neurosurgery and
chest surgery, one Type 3 geriatric care hospital, and one Type 4 hospital.
in 1989, this HSA provided 3.2 and 0.86 primary care and specialized medical
consultations per person, respectively (these figures are above the national
average). The public sector's bed population ratio is 1.8 per 1,000. The
beneficiaries of the public health system are concentrated in the low-income
districts of La Reina, ilufioa, Peflalolen, and M&cul and seek secondary and
tertiary services at the Type 1 Del Salvador Hospital. However, this hospital
is very distant from its intended beneficiaries. In addition, this
underequipped and overcrowded hospital cannot meet the demand, particularly
for *emrgency and maternity services, and the deficit translates into delays,
slow processing of patients, and rejections. This situation is exacerbated by
the lack of specialized ambulatory care facilities that could relieve the
excess demand in hospital facilities. Insufficient surgical capacity affects
the operation of the Type 3 Calvo lckenna hospital.
The Metronolitan BouLft HNA shares with the Metropolitan Southeast
NSA the poorest segments of the MS population, some of whi h were relocated
from different slums into peripheral districts of these two HSAs. This HSA
show the fastent population increase in the last decade. As it is centered
around the main highlray to the country's souther territory, it is expected
that rapid population growth will continue during the 19903. The public
health network includes 36 primary care units and 6 hospitals (one Type 1
hospital, one Type 2 hospital, and four '7ype 3 hospitals). In 1989, it
provided 2.7 and 0.51 primary care and secialized medical consultations per



72                     Annex 3
Page 4 of 18
person, respectively, and has a ratio of 2.2 beds per 1,000 population.
Overall, the operation of the hospitals in this HSA, particularly the Type 1
Barros Luco Trudeau Hospital and the Type 3 B1 Pino Hospital, is seriously
affected by the deteriorated state of their infrastructure and equipment.
The Metrooolitan Southeast HSA, with 27 small primary care
facilities and only two hospitals (the Sotero del Rio, a Type ' hospital, and
one Type 3 hospital that is used as a nursing home), covers extensive rural
areas and serves the poorest segment of the MS population. The primary care
facilities provide limited coverage and low quality of services (a low 1.5
coneultations per parson were provided in 1989), and access to hospital
facilities, both for secondary and tertiary care, is difficult. This HSA has
a severe shortage of beds with a ratio of 1 bed per 1,000 population; in 1989
it provided only 0.25 specialized medical coa3ultations per person. Obsolence
and outdated equipment in the Sotero del Rio Hospital aggravates this
situation.
Hospitals in MS play a special role in that thley receive patients
from three different sources: (a) referred patients from other regions of the
country, particularly for specialized treatments, extensive surgery, and all
treatments still at the research or development stage, account for 20 percent
of admissions; (b) patients who are not referred from other regions, but who
have relatives or acquaintances in the city, represent another 15 percent of
all admissions; and (c) patients from the MS, routinely referred by their
local health services, make up the remaining 65 percent of admissions.
B.   Antofacasta Reaion (AR)
AR, the second northernmost and largest region in the country,
spans an area of 126,444 sq. km., and is composed of three provinces
--Antofagasta, Tocopilla and El Loa. AR total population of about 396,000 in
1990, represents 3 percent of the country's population for a low population
density of 3.1 inhabitants per sq. km. Rugged desert topography has led to
the concentration of the AR population in urban centers separated by wide
distances. As a result, more than 95 percent of the population is urban. The
two major cities are Antofagasta and Calama.
Health Status. The health status of the AR population is worse
than the rational average. In 1989, the infant mortality rate stood at
19.2/1,000 live births, and maternal mortality at 6/10,000 live births. The
high mortality rate from cancer observed in AR (22.5 percent versus the
country's average of 18.1 percent in 1989) is the highest in the country.
Studies have indicated that one the most important factors associated with the
onset of cancer in Ak may be the presence of arsenic in the soil and water;
this agent is also a risk factor for cardiovascular conditions. AR also has a
high prevalence of respiratory disorders, which are related to the mining
industry--the main economic activity in the region.
Health Facilities. The network of public health facilities in AR
is relatively simple and is locatad along the main highway system. AR has 26
primary health care facilities and 5 public hospitals; only one of them is a
Type 1 facility--the Antofagasta Hospital (Table 1).  In 1989, 2.5 ard 0.38
primary care and specialized medical consultations per person, respectively,
were provided.  There are 13 private hospitals, for a total of 621 beds, as



73                     Annex 3
Page 5 of 18
compared to 961 beds in the public sector. The relatively large private
health sector is a by-product of the development of the mining industry since
most of the mining copanies operating in AR have their own health facilities
or enter into contractual arrangements with private physicians and hospitals
for the provision of health care to employees and their families. The
bed/population ratio in the public sector is 2.4/1,000. Overall, the public
system covers about 60 percent of the population, and private providers the
remaining 40 percent.
Most of the health care services in AR are provided in Antofagasta
and Calama, particularly for specialized care that is offered on a referral
basis by the Antofagasta Hospital, and in some measure by the Calama Hospital,
a Type 3 facility (Tables 2-3). However, obsolescence, lack of maintenance,
and outdated equipment severely constrain the performance of these two
hospitals. According to an evaluation of medical and support services
equipment carried out in 1991, about half of all the equipment in these two
hospitals would only last in useful form two more years. If this situation is
not corrected promptly, there is a danger that a virtual paralyzation of the
hospitals would occur in two to nine years. In addition, it was observed that
in the Antofagasta Hospital there is a severe deficit of equipment needed for
the adequate provision of radiology, traumatology, cardiology, and oncology
services'
C. Llanchical Rection (LLR)
The LLR, located in the southern part of the country, comprises
thrze provinces: Llanquihue, Chiloe Island, and Palena. It presentb totally
opposite characteristics to those of the AR:a cold rainy climate, difficult
transportation in spite of relatively short distances, and localities
inaccessible in winter. The LLR, covering an area of 39,360 sq. km., has a
population of 380,000, or 3 percent of the total population irn the country,
scattered in small villages, agricultural settlements, and fisheries. There
are a number of communes, particularly in Chiloe Island, with a large
proportion of rural population. The average per capita income is low and
close to 90 percent of the population depends on the public system for health
care.
Health Statu. The level of health in LLR is below the national
average. In 1989 the estimated infant mortality rate was 18/1,000 live
births, while the maternal mortality rate stood at 4/10,000 live births. Late
fetal and perinatal mortality rates are the highest in che country; the LLR
also has a high proportior of children who are born underweight. These
indicators reflect deficiencies in the health care system, particularly
limited access to prenatal care. Degenerative diseases and chronic
conditions, such as cancer and cardiovascular diseases, are the principal
causes of morbidity and mortality. However, the LLR shows a large proportion
of ill defined causes of death, which is a result of death certification by
witnesses rather than by trained health staff.
Health Facilities. The public health network in the LLR is
composed of 286 small primary care facilities and 14 hospitals (Table 1). The
existence of a large number of primary care facilities has allowed wide health
services coverage, evan though some high risk rural areas of difficult access
are only partially covered (some of these areas are covered by hospital ships



74                     Annex 3
Page 6 of 18
that visit them sporadically). The Type 1 Puerto Montt Hospital is the
inevitable referral center for secondary and tertiary care as all
transportation in the LLR converges on this city. The LLR performed 2.7
primary care consultations and 0.23 specialized medical consultations per
person in 1989. The public sector's bed/population ratio is 2.5 per 1,000
(Table 2). There are only 3 small private hospitals in LLR.
An extensive survey of the facilities in the LLR, together with an
analysis of present demand and supply of services, indicated major
deficiencies in physical infrastructure, as well as disrepair and outdated
medical and support service equipment, in 9 of the 11 Type 4 facilities, and
in the Type 3 hospitals of Ancud and Castro in Chiloe Island. This situation
is particularly acute in the Castro Hospital, which was constructed after the
1960 earthquake with building materials that were inadequate for the climate
of the region. Insufficient surgical capacity, outdated medical equipment,
deteriorated heating, plumbing and laundry system, as well as limited
outpatient care capaci$y hamper the operation of the Puerto Montt Hospital.
The recent classification of the Puerto Montt Hospital as a Type 1 facility
requires additional investments in diagnostic and therapeutic technologies to
ensure that it operates at the same level as other hospitals in a similar
category (Tables 3-4).

Annex 4: METHODOLOGY TO EVALUATE
HEALTH CARE FACITIES AND HEALTH
SERVICES
The existing Chilean health care system, now in operation for fifty
years, had proved to be extremwey efficient and was universally recognized as
one of the best in Latin America. However, due to progress achieved in
primary care, changes in the pattern of diseases, growing urbanization, and
advances in medical technology the present system needs to adjust and be
modernized. This will be done as part of the Health Sector Reform Project.
To efficiently implement health sector reform it was necessary to
establish a framework for the impending investment projects. In order to do
thim the Ministry of Health (NOR) initiated a review of the health care
delivery system. In 1991 a study, known as "Adjustment of the Public Health
Care Delivery System" was c*ariid out in eight regional health service areas.
Through this study, the MDR identified the changes that were needed in the
health care delivery system in these eighi areas.
The Adjustment oZ the Public Health Care System study has two
parts. The first section analyzed the physical resources of the health care
delivery system, focusing on clinics and hospitals. The second part of the
study evaluated the supply ard demand for health care services at all level of
care.
A. SELECTION O  THE STUDY SITES
The eight Health Service Areas (HSA's) which the study focused on
were: Antofagasta in the 31 Loa region; Llanchipal in the Los Lagos region;
and Norte, Sur, Sur-Oriente, Occidente, Oriente, and Central in the
metropolitan Santiago region. Antofagasta was chosen because it is the
largest HSA in tls country and typifies a HSA in which the population is
concentrated in small towns separated by great distances. Llanchipal is
representative of a HSA with a widely dispersed and isolated rural population.
The HS's in the Metropolitan Santiago Region were included in the study
because together they pose the major health problem in the country, as well
as, include the majority of the country's population.
S. ERVALUATION OF PHYSICAL RESOURCES
The examination of the health Wystem's physical resources in these
eight HSA's focused on: (i) quantity and quality; (ii) productivity capacity;
and (iii) performance.
ouantity and Oualiy. in order to measure the quality of health care
faci3lties a quantitative methodology was developed. Information was gathered
for each service within the hospital (see Annex 4-1) on: a) condition of the

physical structure; b) number of installations in the building including but
not limited to water, sewers, electricity, liquid gas, heat, clinical gases
and communications; and c) operation of installations. Teams of architects,
encineers and construction workers then assisted in converting this
information into three separate numerical indicators (see Box 1). These
indicators were then utilized to evaluate the vulnerability of the facilities
at both the disaggregated and aggregated level.

Performance. The performance of health care facilities at the
hospital level was evaluated by two quantitative methnds. In the first method
a matrix was developed to measure the level of coordination within the
hospital between the different medical and support services. In the matrix at
the intersection of each row and column, where the different services were
listed, one of three codes (see box 2) were registered to indicate the level
of coordination that, based on the judgment of experts, ought to exist within
the hospital.  Next, an evaluation, based on observation and experience, was
done on the level of coordination between the services that actually occurs
within each given hospital. The information obtained was converted into three
quantitative indicators (see Box 2) and entered into a matrix. The numbers in
the matrix were summed to evaluate the performance of individual services,
hospital areas, and the hospital.

The second method for measuring the performance of health care
facilities focused on evaluating the specific medical and support services.
Individual services were rated according to: (i) size based on recommendations
(see Annex 4-1); (ii) number of resources based on the information obtained in
the analysis of productive capacity; and (iii) the flow of personnel and
materials. For each of these measures a numerical value was given (see Box 3)
which were then aggregated to evaluate the service and the facility.
A quantitative analysis was also undertaken which examined the
relationship among the different health care facilities. Emphasis was given
to evaluating the referral system and vias of access.

In order to simplify the evaluation of tertiary care three
indicators, which are typically used and relatively easy to measure, were
chosen: (i) consultations per admission; (ii) beds per population; and (iii)
admissions per population. The optimal ratio for each of these indicators was
calculated by adding the current level of care provided by the hospital to the
amount of additional care needed for those on the hospital waiting list.
Estimatina the Beneficiary ponulation. In order to convert the
standards per beneficiary into standards for an entire health service area,
the beneficiary population for each area had to be estimated. The number of
beneficiaries, defined as a persons utilizing the public health sector as
described under Law No. 18,469, was obtained from the 1990 National Socio-
Economic Characteristic Survey (CASBN). Approximately seventy-five percent of
the population was identified as public system beneficiaries. However,
although CASEN collected data on the utilization of health care by sector
(i.e. Public, ISAPRBS, OtherW) this information was nc* disaggregated by sex
or age group.  To obtain these figures the assumption was made that the age
and sex distribution within the beneficiary population would be the same as in
the total population. Using data from the National Institute of Statistics
(INN) and the Center for Latin American Demographics (CELADE), which collects
information on total population by age and sex for each HSA region, the age
and sex distribution of the beneficiary population was calculated.
For Llanchipal it was not possible to use CASBN to determine the
number of beneficiaries because in this HSA the CASEN data was not
disaggregated by province. Instead, the number of beneficiaries was assumed
to be equal to the percentage of children under the age of one who received
public health care services.
Standards for each HSA. By multiplying the standard number of services
per individual beneficiary by the total number of HSA beneficiaries (in that
sam age and sex group) the optimal level of services which should be
delivered in each HSA was obtained. Tlhese figures were calculated for the
base year 1990 and were projected for the next ten years based on an estimated
population growth of 1.8 percent.
Calculation of Deficits. Deficiencies in the availability of
health care services were calculated by subtracting the current levels of care
provided from the standard levels of care as defined above. (See Box 4) This
was done at the aggregated and the disaggregated level including: the entire
nation; HSA's; facilities; and individual medical and support services.

Annex 5: FRAME OF REFERENCE FOR HHEALTH
INVESTMENTS
Ihis Annex summarizes the major recomnendations of the Study on
the Reorientation of the Health Care System. Those recommendations have been
approved by the Government and will sorve as the guideline for investments in
health care establishments in Chile.
A. BACKGROUND
The Ministry of Health (MNH) initiated a reviow of the public
health care system for the preparation of the proposed Health Sector Reform
Project to be financed by the World Bank.
The profile of the public health caroe sytem consists of a
breakdown by levels of care and types of astablislments, taking into account
the geodemographic location, the type of services to be provided and the
referral systems linking them. The review focused on four main issues withir.
the health care system:
-    health policy
=    epidemiologic profile
=     geodemographic situation
-    potential sector resources
Flexibility will be required in the application of the results of
the study in order to be able to take local circumstances into account and to
ensure that the instrument developed will be useful within a general framework
for investment decisions.
D. OBDJCTIZS
The study has a dual purpose: to design a support instrument for
investment decisions and to contribute to the restructuring of the public
health care system.
The profile seeks to provide guidance in rosolving ome of the
problems observed in the health care system; i.e. how open care is valued,
how care can be decentralized and regional autonomy increased, and how levels
of complexity and referral systems can be organized through a range of
alternative solutions. The mouol proposed for solving some of these problems
emphasizes social participation, intersectoral work and decen';,il.ation as
basic components of the care sytem.
*     In this docuasnt, *ope cara  refers to outpatifant/bulatory services wnd aclosed care" to
hospitatfzation (see Table 1).



96                 ~~~~~Annex 5f9
Page 2 of 9
The proposed health care system is organized according to levels
of coMplexity for open and clooed care taking into consideration the following
objectives:
bringing the care as close as possible to the user, within a
context of cost effectiveness;
providing the services in the lowest possible level of complexity
while maintaining them at an adequate quality level;
ensuring the appropriateness of the health actions;
giving the regions autonomy and thereby contributing to greater
decentralization.
C.  METHODOLOGY
For each of the proposed establishments t list was prepared of the
services they could provide, using as guidelines the solving of health
problems in the closest level of complexity closest to the location and
promotion of ambulatory care.
By way of more speciiic criteria the following were taken into
consideration: potential demand for care (frequency), availability of
resources, the appropriateniess of the care, the complementarity ox the
activities among themselves, the cost of the services and the patient's
safety.
The list consists of 2,094 services, grouped into direct care
services, laboratory examinations, radiological examinations, radiation
treatment, diagnostic and therapeutic procedures, and surgical interventions
in the different medical specialties and dental services.
In the list there is a "yes" by each service when it can be
provided in the establishment in question and a blank when not. "Yes" for the
laboratory examinations refers to the prucess and not to sample taking.
It is important to bear in mind that the list must be applied with
sufficient flexibility to take the differing local circumstances into accotut,
while in light of techunological advances the list itself will have to be
periodically reviewed.
In establishing which areas the proposed health facilities should
serve the following different geodemographic circumstances, which prevail in
the country, were used-
-    Urban-Rural Area:  an area consisting of a city of fewer than
10,000 inhabitants plus rural zones with predominantly scattered
population.
-     Small City:  cities with between 10,000 and SO,000 inhabitants.



97                     Annex 5
Page 3 of 7
Intermediate City:  cities with betwe6n 50,000 and 100,000
inhabitants.
Large City:  ;cies with over 100,000 inhabitants, including the
major urban areas such as Santiago, Valparaiso-Viftia del Mar and
Concepci6n-Talcahuano.
The population oiz. proposed for each establishment is flexible;
the guiding principle considered for the low-complexity establiohments waa
accessibility, and for those of greater complexity, optimum size. The
suggested population corresponds to the Public Health System beneficiary
population.
D. FACILITY DZSCRIPTZOI
The proposed establishments are set out in the following table, by
levels of complexity and care subsystem:
a..;  .::.."'.. ...x.
OM*
.Z-''..'Ruta CCl'  i.           .'.icX
W~~~~~~~~~ ~~~~~~ .,...,,...
A ftrasectoral Area, which would have shared responsibility with
the community and social organizations, and an Intersectoral Area, which would
havs shared responsibilities with the other public agencies, are also
proposed.
A brief description of all the proposed establishments is given
below:
Rural Health Post (RP): The Rural Health Post is an ambulatory care facility.
It offers easy access to health care for communities of 600 to 2,000
inhabitants.



98                          Annex 5
Page 4 of 7
This facility would be run by a resident rural health auxiliary
who periodically recoives assistance from the professional team consisting
basically of a general practitioner, dentist, nurse and midwife, who may come
together or on an alternating schedule. This team supervises the work of the
resident auxiliary and coMplements his or her training, as well as provides
care. This facility will provide basic primary health care.
Rural Clinic (RC): This in an *etablishment met up in localities of 2,000 to
5,000 inhabitants with an assigned population of up to 20,000 persons. It is
ranked under the Urban General Clinic and/or Type 4 Hompital.
The rural clinic health team should comprise: a general
practitioner, a dentist, a nurse, a midwife and a rural paramedical auxiliary.
It will perform primary care level promotion, protection and recovery
activities in the adult, child, dental and maternal programs.
Vrban Goneral Clinic (W30: An Urban General Clinic can be located in small
cities as a stand-alone establiNhment or attached to a low-complexity
hospital; or in medium or large cities as part of a network of urban clinics,
in conjunction with the Health Referral Centers. The population for which it
is responsible will not exceed 40,000.
The Urban General Clinic's professional health team will include:
general practitione:., general dentists, nurses, midwives, nutritionists and
social workers. This facility performs promotion, protection and recovery
activities in the adult, child, maternal and dental programs, for the primary
care level.
Health Referral Center (MaC): The Health Referral Center is an open care
establishment, of medium ccmplexity, located in medium or large cities as part
of the network of urban clinics, where it can be a stand-alone establishment
or attached to a Type 2 or 3 Hospital.
It is technically and administratively under the jurisdiction of
a pertinent Health Service Area staffed by specialists and serving persons
referred to it. This facility should meet the demand for medium complexity
diagnostic and therapeutic services from the low-complexity establishments.
its diagnostic and therapeutic services will handle referrals from four to six
UGCs.
Diagnostla and Therapeutic Center (DTC): Diagnostic and Therapeutic Centers
are located in the headquarter cities of the Health Service Areas, with at
least one per Service. These facilities are attached to a Type 1 Hospital, or
to a Type 2 Hospital if the Health Service concerned does not have a Type 1
Hospital. They are the most complex, technological establishments defined as
open care establishments, but, they also serve the closed care of the hospital
to which they are attached.
The Diagnostic and Therapeutic Centers furnish ambulatory care to
patients referred to them by the establishments of lower complexity and to



Annex 5
99                         Page 5 of 9
hospitalized patients, in all specialties and subspecialties pertaining to
high-complexity medicine and dentistry. .They have the necessary equipment
diagnostic and therapeutic services of a high level of complexity. They do
not provide walk-in consultations.
Type 4 Hospital (34): This level hospital is located in cities of over 10,000
inhabitants whose catchment areas include the communities assigned to the
Rural and General Clinics, but not exceeding 30,000 inhabitants. It may have
an Urban General Clinic attached to it and has fewer than 100 beds.
Its current professional human resources are: general
practitioner, nurse, midwife, social worker, medical technician and
nutritionist. The level of its equipment is sufficient for it to perform
diagnostic support with basic laboratory and imaging facilities, electro-
cardiograms and study of the fetoplacental unit. It provides round-the-clock
emergency medical service.
Type 3 Hospital (H3): This facility is located in localities of up to 50,000
inhabitants, with a catchment area made up of the communities assigned to the
Rural and Urban General Clinics and not exceeding 70,000 people. It may also
be located in large cities, where it would meet the demand for hospitalization
for cases of lesser complexity and also for emergency care in such cities. It
would have fewer than 200 beds.
Activities in Type Three Hospitals are designed to meet the
greater part of the closed care needs in the basic specialties of the
population for which it is responsible. It has differentiated basic services
(pediatrics, internal medicine, obstetrics/gynecology and surgery), in
addition to basic neonatology units and intermediate care units.
Type 2 Hospital (X2): A Type 2 Hospital may be located in cities with over
100,000 inhabitants, as the sole hospital establishment, and may have a Health
Referral Center attached to it. It can also be located in large urban areas
as a supporc establishment for Type 1 hospitals. It is suggested that its
size be below 400 beds.
The hospital has a differentiated emergency service staffed by the
resident interns, and differentiated clinical services for basic specialties.
It also offers intermediate care units for basic specialties, neonatology and,
where justified, an intensive care unit. It has basic specialists and
subspecialists in high-demand areas.
If it happens to be the highest level in the Health Service Area
concerned, the Type 2 Hospital may upgrade its complexity level above that
laid down in the list of services and have a Diagnostic and Therapeutic Center
attached to it.
Type 1 Hospital (Hl): This facility is located in the main cities of the
Health Services, which forms the base hospital of each system unit when the
size of the Health Service or its geographic location so warrant. It is the



Annex 5
100                       Page 6 of 9
closed care establishment of the highest level of complexity. It has a
Diagnostic and Therapeutic Center attached to it. It should have about 500
beds, with a catchment area of over 500,000 inhabitants.
The Type 1 Hospital has organized emergency service, resident
interns differentiated by clinical service, intermediate care units and an
intensive care unit. Its human resources comprise almost the full range of
clinical specialties and subspecialtion, though it may not include some which
for reasons of cost-effectiveness should be concentrated in certain system
units strategically distributed throughout the country.
It is proposed that the present specialized institutes or
hospitals be included as clinical services with the Type 1 or 2 hospitals or
hospital complexes, and could in these cases be made national or regional
referral centers.
Parasectoral Nstablishments: This group could include: Recovery Center for
Malnourished Cases, Community Center for Care of Patients with Stable
Psychiatric Pathology, Home for the Elderly, Home for Pregnant Adolescents,
Integral Family Support Center, Basic Medical Care Center, Home for Pregnant
Rural Women, Socio-Labor Rehabilitation Center, Protected Homes and Workshops
for psychiatric patients, Predischarge Establishments for young children, and
others, in accordance with local needs.
These establishments would share responsibilities with the
community and social organizations. Even though they do not fully belong to
the public health system, they would have to be considered as means for
filling gaps detected in the care system, with the relevant Health Service
Area being made responsible for technical supervision of their funcuions.
The location of the proposed Parasectoral Establishments would
take into account basically the size of the population and the existence of
open and closed care establishments in the area.
E. RNINRRAL SYSTEM
The referral systems (Figures 1 and 2), which describe the flow of
patients between the different establishments of the system, were approached
from the standpoint of inclusion of the model in the different geodemographic
situations, and separating the flow of elective patients and those for
emergency care.
Flow of elective patients: Figure 1 represents the flow of elective patients
in the different situations. As a general rule, elective patients will be
sent to the ambulatory care or hospital establishment -- as appropriate -- of
the next-highest level of complexity, under a system of obligatory staggered
referral.
Flow of emergency patients: Figure 2 represents the emergency flow. Only
establishments providing this type of care are included. All the
establishments serve spontaneous demand and can, in principle, refer cases to



Annex 5
101                        Page 7 of 9
any establishments of higher complexity, at the referring physician'>
discretion.
It must be stressed that in both instances -- elective or
emergency -- it in essential for the proper functioning of the care system
that adequate means of communication and transport be available in each of the
establishments described.

Annex 8-1: OTLINTE OF PROPOSED MOH
FUNCTIONS
KINISTER OF EALTH
o The Minister is the highest authority within the Ministry
of Health.
o The Minister defines and sanctions the overall objectives of the MOH
and the activities which need to be undertaken to meet these goals.
o The ultimate responsibility for management of the Health Service Areas
lies with the Minister.
UNDERSECRETARY OF HEALTH
o The Undersecretary is responsible for the internal
management and coordination of all institutions within the
health sector.
o The Undersecretary operationalizes the MOH mission.
PROGRAMS DIVISION
o The Programs Division is responsible for defining the
norms, standards and health programs needed to improve the
health of the population.
o The Division provides advice and support concerning
efficiency and effectiveness in the delivery of services.
OPERATIONS DIVISION
o The Operations Division is responsible for the
coordination of strategic and operational actions between
the Ministry of Health and the Health Service Areas.
o The Division will have five subunits each of which will be responsible
for five to six Health Service Areas and will:
(i) assure there is consistency between the policies
issued by the Ministry of Health and the HSAs' execution
of these polices; and
(ii) coordinate, elaborate, and modify, as necessary,
management and operational policies at the HSA level;



119                             Annex 8
Page 3 of 3
Aimex 8-1: OUTLINE OF PROPOSED MOH
FUNCTIONS CONT'D
3=DG13T AND PLAMUN G DIVISION
o The Budget and Planning Division is responsible for the efficient
allocation of human, physical, financial, and information resources
within the decentralized health sector. I. is also responsible for
the evaluation and improvement of MOR institutional,
organizational, planning and management strategies.
INT3RNAL ADMINISTRATION DIVISION
o The Internal Administration Division is in charge of all
internal operations needed to maintain and improve the
logistics of MOH operations including: payroll, personnel,
daycare, internal comuunications, and office supplies.

Ann x : SERVICE PROVISION AGREEMENT
1.          A management contract called Service Provisign Agreement would be
used an a tool to reflect the commitments between the MOH and the HSAs on the
type and volume of services to be rendered and the corresponding financial,
human, and capital resources required to provide those services. Performance,
which will be linked with allocation of resources, would be monitored through
mutually agreed upon annual targets using a set of indicators being developed
with HSRP financing.
Performance Indicators
2.          A sample of performance indicators currently being analyzed by the
NaH (PCU) is shown below. Reference bench-mark information for these
indicators would be defined. The performance indicators are classified into
three groups: Impact Indicators (Variables de ImDacto Final - VIF); Progress
Indicators (Variables de Ooeracion Intermedia - Vol); and Exogenous Factors
(Variables Exoaenas - VEX)
A. Impact Indicator. (Variables de ImDacto Final-VIP):
3.          These indicators would measure the results of health-related
actions. They can be grouped into: Impact indicators measuring health
status (YIFa) and Impact indicators measuring the quality of health delivery
(VIFa)   (i . ~ a
-Mortality by selected causes (for example: respiratory
diseases, hepatitis, typhoid fever)
- Adjusted mortality for all causes
- Infant mortality (for infants less than 1 year of age)
- Maternal mortality
Mortality by preventable causes (for example:
intrahospital infections, lack of early health care
delivery, lack of resources)
(Note: To associate changes observed in these indicatore
exclusively with health-related actions or interventions would not be totally
right. Changes observed are caused by pumerous factors and analysis would
thus include qualitative evaluation of existing conditions in the HSA area.)
(ii) V2a
- Waiting period for patients to receive care
- Percentage of refusals
- Length of the waiting lists (number of people)
D. Proaress Indicators (Variables de oDerAcidn intermedia--
h I)e:
4.          Theme indicators measure the health-related interventions that



121                      Annex 9
Page 2 of 4
affect the VIF. They can be classified into three types: preventyIY (VOXD),
curative (VOIc) and onerational officiencv measure (VOle). Examples include:
(i) m
- Total number of vaccines by type
- Total number of check-ups for healthy children
- Percentage of professionally attended deliveries
- Number of educational activities of various types
- Environmental quality, measured as contamination levels
exceeding certain standards
(ii) 3=
- Number of ambulatory services by type
- Number of total surgical procedures and by hospital
services
(iii) VOI
- Cost of vaccines per user
- Number of days-hospital bed by clinical service
- Decrease in the number of cases by type of disease
versus the cost of preventive interventions by type
- Normal deliveries versus total
- Net (real) cost of curing diseases by type
- Number of diagnostic interventions per visits
C. Exoaenous Factors (VEX):
5.          Exogenous factors have an effect on the health status of the
target (attended) population (measured by the VIFs) but are not controlled by
the interventions performed by the services. They would provide information
to be incorporated in the analysis of HSA performance. They would help
explain certain behavior observed in the impact and progress indicators.
Bxamples include:
- coverage of potable water supply and sanitation
- degree of contamination
- growth (or decrease in growth) of the population
- average income of the population
- distribution of the population by socioeconomic
grouping
- educational levels
6.          During the Project Mid-term Review the Government and the Bank
would assess the feasibility of introducing cost-effectiveness indicators in
the Service Provision Agreements based on results of a number of ongoing
efforts that would help build the bases for the use of the cost-effectiveness
criteria for resource allocation. These efforts include, among others, Mi) a
study to estimate the cost-effectiveness ratios of a series of interventions
in the areas of smoking, cervical cance, and accidents; (ii) HSRP-supported



122                     Annex 9
Page 3 of 4
studies to determine the actual cost of the different NOH programs; and (iii)
the eotablishment of modern cost and financial accounting information systems
under the proposed project.
Initial Imlementation Phase
6.          The initial implementation phase of the Service Provision
Agreement will be carried out in 1993. Implementation will be periodically
monitored and adjustments will be instituted as necessary before the Agreement
io used in all the HSAs. The design of the initial program includes the
following features:
(1) Soscific Oblectives
a. Select the appropriate indicators to include in the
Service Provision Agreement.
b. Implement the Service Provision Agreement gradually in sample
HSAs.
c. Based on a sample of HSAs, evaluate the impact of the
utilization of the Service Provision Agreements.
(2) Nethodoloov
a. The set of progress and impact indicators to be used in the
Service Provision Agreements will be selected with the involvement of experts.
In addition to technical considerations, the selected indicators will have, at
the minimum, the following characteristics: reliability, prompt turnaround (a
minimal duration to obtain validated information), and easy collection,
management and updating.
b. The Service Provioion Agreement incorporating the chosen
indicators would be implemented in soample HAM for one year. This phase would
involve the following steps:
(i) The Agreement would be initiated at least in the five
HSAs where the new health financing ochemes are currently being pilot-tested.
At the same time the MOH would analyze the capacity of other HSA. to design,
manage, implement, monitor and evaluate the provision of services, as well as
the availability of appropriate information to monior the USA performance
taking into account the goals set in the Service Provision Agreement. This
analysis would be critical in order to support those services that are
deficient in these areas in case a decision is made to include some other USA.
in the first year of implementation.
(ii) IDH would then set goals and budgets for each HSA.
These goals would be generally quantitative in nature; whenever appropriate
qualitative goals would be considered.
(iii) The above goals would be negotiated with the HSAs.
The HSAs would send counterproposals, as necessary. After some iterations,
agreements would be incorporated in a Service Provision Agreement. The
negotiating process would require that HSAs analyze the type and volume of



123                        Annex 9
Page 4 of 4
activities required to accomplish desired goals and the resources needed to
carry out these activities. Planning and evaluation skills would be necessary
in this process. At this stage the new financing mechanisms (PAD, PPD) will
be of vital importance because they would relate production goals to the
required resources.
c. The initial phase would be monitored periodically and
corrective actions would be instituted as needed. The exporience would be
evaluated at the end of one year and if appropriate applied nationwide to all
HSAs. Also, selected indicators will be monitored in all HSAs during the
initial year. This would help establish a data base and reference benchmarks
for the subsequent full scale implementation of the Service Provision
Agreements
