Annex 10: TRAING PROGRAM
METHODOLOGY
1.          Program Demian. The Training Program aims at managing efficiently
and effectively the investment in the development of human capital needed by
the public health subsector in the short and medium term. NOH and its four
institutes (ISP, CSF, FONASA, and ISAPRES Superintendmncy), HSAs, and the
municipal health services would be included in the Program. Throe tvDeL of
tr inin would be offered: (i) senior management training; (ii) middle
management training; and (iii) clinical training. The partipating
institutions would present annually a eot of training projects using standard
formats that would be evaluated according to five known and transparent
criteria. Three criteria would be related to national priorities and two
criteria would reflect the priority and impact of the projects as judged by
the proposers. An MOH evaluation evaluation committee would rank the projects
according to a pre-established points system and reccmmend approval or
disapproval. In line with the policy of decentralizing decision-making, the
points system would 4radually assign more importance to the priorities and the
judgments of the proposers and less weight to criteria that permit the central
level to influence the evaluation process.
2.          The program's design clearly reflects the decentralization
philosophy of the new public health sector with delegation of decision-making
to operating levels, the attention to the efficient use of financial
resources, and the concern with integrating actions at different levels of the
system.
3.          Oraanization and Manaaement of the Trainina Program   Four
principles underlie the design and organization of the Program: (i) support
for the decentralization policy; (ii) ensure the effectiveness of training
actions; (iii) pursue efficiency in tho use of training funds; and (iv)
provide a stable structure and consistency with MOH's mission and the goals of
developing human resources.
4.          The organizational structure supporting the Training Program would
consist of four entities: (i) three ad-hoc evaluation committees; (ii) a
Secretariat; (iii) the Project Coordinating Unit (PCU); and (iv) the human
resources development units of NDH and of each of the participating health
services institutions. The program's Secretariat would be staffed by one
full-time program manager, three part-time professionals and a full-time
secretary. Attachment 1 contains the Terms of Reference of these four support
groupe. The TORe have been reviewed by the Bank and were found satisfactory.
S.          Selection Criteria. The annual amount of money available for
training projects would be estimated and an indicative amount would be
communicated to each participating service institution. The proportion of the
indicative amount to be spent on each one of the three types of training
programs would be mentioned. This firet stop in the procedure would eliminate
the current obstacle of service institutions not knowing if money will be
available for training purposes and how much which discourages the submission
of training projects. Indicative amounts would be correlated with the number



125                    Annex 10
Page 2 of 6
of total staff of the participating service but would not constitute a
commitment to allocate resources. Commitment would coma only after
application of the oelection criteria by the evaluation committees and formal
approval of the projects.
6.          Three initial screening criteria would be applied by (the
Secretariat to make sure that the training proposals qualify and are ready to
be submitted for evaluation:
(a) Basic Inforation and Forat: use of standard format, identifying
information, completeneoo and timeliness of submission, signature by
appropriate authority, and reference to a specific type of training program;
(b) Nuer of ProbatE  Submtitted: to make sure that each service
proposes several projects in order to avoid situations where only one large
project 13 submitted which - if disapproved - may leave that particular
service without a training program for that year. A formula would be used to
assure that each service submits on tho average five or more training projects
foz each type of training; and
(C) T&et PoDUlation: resources can only be spent for training
personnel employed by the service institution submitting the proposal. For
municipal staff the application would be handled by the corresponding HSA.
7.          Proposed training projects that meet the three minimal screening
criteria would be assessed and ranked according to a points system by one of
the three evaluation committees according to the type of training. The
evaluation comittees would be given an evaluation methodology to ensure
consistency across the three commAttees. Vive selection criteria would be
used for each type of training: the first three reflect the policies of the
central government and the last two take into account the qpe%cific and
ideosyncratic interests of the proposers.
(a) Level of Ca: high, medium and low levels of care are identified
according to the complexity of the service delivery institution. The
weighting given to each level of care provides a signal to the proposer on the
importance attached to each level from a totrL system perspective. For the
clinical training program the weightin3 wou2  be the same for all three
levels.
(b) Innatient Care verus outoatint Care: outpatient care would be
assigned more points for the first year of operation (15 versus 10 points).
(c) Consistencr with National Policies and Goalg: training projects for
senior and middle level managers would be evaluated on their consistency with
national human resources policies and goals; clinical training projects would
be asesseed on the degree that they meet specific health policies promoted by
MR. In each case the level of agreement is either maximum with 10 points or
minimum with 0 points.
(d) Priority of Prolect: each project in the three types of training
would be prioritized by the proposer and a lesser number of points would be
assigned in descending order of priority to each project.



i26                    Annex 10
Page 3 of 6
(e) Impact of Project: the maximum. number of 25 points would be assigned
to those projects that are considered by the proposing institution to solve a
specific problem of crucial importance in service operationa.
S.          Training projects for senior management would be formulated by MOH
and senior managers of -I0H and its institutes, of HSAs and of hospitals would
be invited to participate.  These projects would be eubmitted for financing
only if there is sufficient demand.
9.   MNaament Trainina for NOR Staff.  The restructuring of MOE, the
addition of senior managers, the policy of decentralizing decison-making, the
intended change in values applying a customer oriented relationship with the
executing agencies, and availability of new technologies necessitate an
intensive management development training program. Four areas of emphasis
have been identified:
(a) Decision-making: organize training sessions in problem solving and
decision making using a strategic issues management approach; promote the use
of quality circles and improve interpersonal relations;
(b) Information handling: enhance upwards, downwards and lateral
conmunications within M0H; organize monthly staff meetings to share and
discuss goals and opportunities; and issue a news bulletin regularly;
(c) Reward systems: develop mechanisms to reward individual performance
and achievement of organizational goals such as visits inside the country
(e.g. private sector institutions) and abroad to learn about alternatives in
health service delivery, or continuing education in selected topics; reward
new and innovative ideas for improving efficiency and productivity; and
(d) Structural and functional relationships: develop inter-departmental
ad hoc teams to solve problems crossing structural boundaries; rotate managers
within departments and divisions.
10.         Training Methods and Trainers.  The training programs would offer
a variety of methods: workshops, intensive courses, inservice training,
seminars, symposia, attendance at professional meetings, and formal full-time
courses in Chile and abroad. The kind of training method preferred would be
stated in the tritning project proposals. The training could be given by: (i)
the service institution itself; (ii) other service institutions different from
the one proposing the training; and (iii) training institutions outside the
public health sector. In the latter case three bids would be obtained unless
the outside training institution would be a university. The performance of
the trainers would be evaluated by the participants. The Secretariat would
consolidate the evaluations and keep a record of the performance for future
use.
11.         Onerating Procedures.  Standard operating procedures have been
defined for: (i) the presentation and approval of annual programs of projects;
(ii) the implementation of programs; (iii) the flow of funds; and (iv) the
evaluation of the trainers by the trainees. Details of these procedures are in
the Project File. The deadline for receiving the annual programs would be
July 31 of each year and the results of the evaluations would be ccomunicated
to the applicants before the end of the month of August of the same year.



127                    Annex 10
Page 4 of 6
Agreements would be signed between MOH and the successful applicants dealing
with the financing of the projects and with the commitment for timely
execution. Agreemento would also be signed between the training institutions
and the purchasers of training programs. Progress reports on training
activities would be submitted semi-annually to the Secretariat by the
participating services institutions. Monthly expenditure reports would be
submitted to the PIU by the participating institutions.
12,         Retention of Trained Personnel.  The private sector competes with
the public sector for the acquisition of skilled health services managers and
is able to offer better financial incentives. The strengths of the public
sector residb in a larger degree of stability and job security and in an
appeal and a response to the values of oervice to others. The proposed
project would develop a strategy to retain trained personnel by: (i)
emphasizing that participation in training programs increases job security and
improves the intrinsic reward of the job; (ii) proposing the development of
career ladders with increasing responsibilities related to the acquisition of
additional technical and managerial skills; and (iii) requesting a two year
commitment by the trainee to remain with the organization with an obligation
to raimburse the training costs if the commitment is broken.
13.         Samnle Trainina Plans. Three preliminary training plans have been
developed, one for management development in MDH and the H8As, one for CSF
staff for technical and managerial skills, and one more detailed and mainly
focused on technical skills for ISP. Estimates of the number of people to be
trained adding across all training methods are about 3,200 for MOH and HSAs,
about 1,100 for CSF and about 600 for ISP or a total of about 4,900. These
training programs and number of participants are indicative and are for the
duration of the project. The training program design emphasizes decision-
making at operating levels and therefore the program would be adjusted on a
yearly basis during execution. The preliminary plans constitute however a
good starting point and would assure early and rapid implementation of human
resources development programs after project approval. The sample training
plans are part of the project files.
14*         Cost of Proaram. The annual recurrent cost of managing the
program is estimated at approximately US$70,300. A one time investment cost
to purchase a computer for the Secretariat would be US$3,000. A preliminary
estimate of the base cost of the training programs over the life of the
project would be approximately US$6 million.



128                        Annex 10
Page 5 of 6
Annex 10, Attachment 1: TRAINING PROGRAM
ORGANIZATION: TERMS OF REFERENCE
OXCL%TARIAT t
The Secretariat oversees all the functions required for management
and supervision of the Training Program. In this capacity the Secretariat
will be responsible for:
o Establishing criteria, guidelines, and policies for the continued
development of the Training Program;
o Evaluating the performance of the Training Program and redesigning
the program as needed;
o Supplying information to the Health Service Areas and health
institution, regarding, but not limited to, authorized bidders,
evaluation results, and necessary documentation;
o Maintaining information system records, as well as, preparing and
distributing this information to those who may need it including
training program applicants, MOH, the Ad-Hoc Evaluation
Commissions and the PCU;
o Organizing the meetings of the Ad-Hoc Evaluation Comnissions;
 Supervising the performance of the Ad-Hoc Evaluation Commissions;
o And aWproving the results of training program evaluations carried out
by the Ad-Hoc Evaluation Commissions.
The Secretariat will be headed by a professional with experience
in the development of human resources and in management who will be appointed
by the Head of the Resources Division. This full-time program manager will be
in charge of management, coordination, and the general policies of the
Secretariat. There will also be three part-time professionals who will each
be in charge of ten health care institutions including Health Service Areas
and MDH's subordinate Institutions. These professionals will assiet their
respective units in solving operational problems and will support all training
programs. The Secretariat will also include one full-time secretary.
AD-HOC 3VAtLUATIXC  CCUISSOUNS:
The Ad-Hoc Evaluation Commissions will be in charge of evaluating
the training projects submitted each year for funding. There will be three
different Ad-Hoc 3valuations Commissions with one specializing in each of the
three types of training programs: (i) a Senior Management Commission; (ii) a
Middle management Commission; and (iii) a Clinical Training Commission.
Individual Commissions will evaluate only those projects corresponding to the
area in which they specialize.



129                    Annex 10
Page 6 of 6
The Comnissions w$il be staffed with experts in subjects related
to the respective specializations. Each Commission will be limited to eight
members all of whom will be appointed by the Secretariat.
PROJECT COORDINATING WNIT (PCU) s
The Project Coordinating Unit will be responsible for the
administration of financial payments to approved training programs.  The PCU
will also review the activities of the training programs to determine, in
accordance with standards established for HSRP, that the training programs'
use of resources correspond to the purpose of the loan. In addition, the PCU
will have an advisory role with regard to the Ad-Hoc Evaluation Commissions
whereby the PCU wlll give guidance concerning procedural and administrative
requirements. To facilitate this each commission will include a PCU member
who is appointed by the General Coordinator.
UKMAN RESOURCES DEVELOPNQMT UNXTBs
As appropriate, Human Resource Development Units will be
established in the Ministry of Health, the Health Service Areas and in each of
the participating health oervice institutions (FONASA, ISP,CBNA9AS, and
ISAPRBS Superintendency). Each unit will be responsible for the formulation
and management of policies for personnel training and development. In working
with the Health Service Areas the units will work with the Directors of the
establishments and with the Human Resources Departments in each Health Service
Area. The size of each Human Resources Development Unit will depend on the
size and the complexity of the health institutions it is responsible for. The
ZIH will decide on establishing these Units in the participating institutions
after assessing the need, scope of training requirement, and budget needed for
this Unit by the health institution.
The specific responsibilities of the Human Resources Development
Units are to:
o Ensure the development of skills which enable personnel to
function in an efficient and effective manner;
o  Identify the personnel training and development needs of the
different health institutions;
o  Encourage and promote "permanent improvement";
o  Organize and coordinate resources to develop integrated training
programs;
o  Establish systems to monitor and control individual training programs
and development objectives;
o  Evaluate the impact of the training programs and report to the
Executive Secretariat.

Annexl2: INFORMATION PROGRAM
I - Intrgduction
1.    Institutions in the Chilean health sector will faco, as a result of
Government policies supported by this project, a drastic change in their
operations, policies, and management processes.   The central ministry,  for
example, wvil need to strengthen its policy formulation, program design,
monitoring and evaluation capabilities, while gradually phasing out its
operational role in the sector. Bxecuting agencies at all levels (Health Service
Areas, Central Supply Facility (CSF), and Institute of Public Health (ISP)) will
need to strengthen their management and operational capabilities to match their
increased autonomy and accountability under Service Provision Agreements. Tne
quality of information systems in the sector will be a key success factor in
effecting these changes.   The Government has therefore developed, and this
project supports, a plan to strengthen the information systems through
administrative reform, introduction of computer-based information systems, and
staff training.
2.    This document summarizes the informatics program proposed under the
"Decentralization of Health Services Delivery" component of the HSR Project. The
informatics program is a series of coordinated activities whose scope, cost,
schedule, objectives, and technology requirements are described in detail in
corresponding documents in the Project File.
1I - Institutional groblems and broiect Cponent Activities
3.    The information systems of practically all institutions in the public
health sector in Chile are cumbersome, unreliable, or incomplete.  Staff at all
levels are both burdened by outmoded administrative procedures, and frustrated
in their need for relevant, timely information to provide patient services,
analyze policy options and make decisions. Administrative procedures have been
developed and expanded over decades, and except for very few cases, they have not
been modernized to take advantage of improvements in technology. Clear and
measurable problems result from this situation:
-    Hospital managers  learn only too late of the grossly uneven
utilization of professional resources.
-      SHA managers lacking timely accounting data overspend in certain
budget categories and are then forced to curtail priority services
to compensate for shortfalls.
-    The ailing public wastes considerable time obtaining multiple
clearances for the same service.
-     Inventories of essential pharmaceuticals and hospital supplies are
kept at very high levels for lack of adequate information on
patterns of use and availability.



134                          Annex 12
Page 2 of 11
Emergency communications must rely on outmoded and unreliable
equipment making the country highly vulnerable in cases of natural
disasters.
4.    It is clear that much more than technology is needed to solve these
problems. Existing operational and administrative procedures must be changed
before computerization can pay off, and staff must be trained to understand the
potential of computers and become skilled in their use.
5.   The project's  Information  Program  consists therefore  of technology
acquisition, system development, and staff training activities carried out with
a strategy and schedule that avoid overflowing the absorptive capacity of
sectoral institutions. These activities are grouped into four subcomponents
dealing with 1) the Central Ministry, 2) the regional Health Services, 3) the
Central Supply Facility, and 4) the Institute of Public Health.
III - Imalementation Strateov
6.   The strategy for implementation of this component recognizes the complexity
of the objectives pursued and goes far beyond the gradual procurement and
installation of computers in sector institutions. The Component is viewed as an
entire program to reform administration and streamline operations in sector
institutions. Important aspects of the implementation strategy are:
a)    The up-front planning, analysis, and design necessary to ensure
long-term integration of information systems and technology will be
done during an initial design phase.   An overall design of
information systems and data bases in the sector will be produced
(and thereafter maintained by the central Ministry); models of
common  systems  such  as  accounting,   inventory  management,
procurement, etc., will be developed; and technology guidelines and
standards will be discussed and adopted.
b)    Implementation will be in phases consistent with the absorptive
capacity of institutions: information systems of high priority will
be designed and implemented first in selected institutions.   In
successive phases, additional systems will be developed and more
institutions incorporated in the program.
c)    Management responsibility for project activities will be placed at
the institution level, with the Project Coordination Unit providing
only coordination, advice, and technical support.  Participating
institutions will autonomously procure the information technology
and services needed to put in place the information systems financed
by the project.
d)    To participate in the informatics program, however, and to receive
tochnical support and model systems, sector institutions will need
to enter into agreements with the MNH to provide the staff time,
resources, and facilities necessary for successful implementation of
the program.   In particular, institutions will need to provide
assurances that they have the technical staff necessary not just to
implement the new systems, but to operate and maintain them in the
long run.



135                        Annex 12
Page 3 of 11
*)    Substantial invesu=mt  in training will be indispeniable  for
Ministry staff to adopt the technologies and work methods required
by the now information system. Staff appear to be interested, even
eager, to go through this process.  nevertheless, the training
program, in addition to imparting now skills, must demonstrate
conclusively: a) that the new systems can increase productivity
without threatening eployment; and b) that staff participation is
indispensable in devising novel, effective work procedures which
take advantage of the flexibility of computers.
IV - 9chfdula
7.   The Znformatics Program will be implemented gradually over the first four
years of the project as shown in Attachment A.
V - Cgt
S.   The total cost of the Information Program, including financing under the
TAHRP, is approximately $14.3 million. A detailed cost table is attached to this
document as Attachment B.
VI - Manaement and Coardination
9.   The Program will be managed as other sectoral programs in the Ministry of
Health: with central design and coordination and decentralized implementation and
control:
a-    The central design and coordination functions are required to
capture obvious economies of scale in system design and
construction, and to ensure compatibility in the information systems
of the sector. For this purpose, an informatics program coordinator
and other consultants as needed, will be retained in the Project
Coordination Unit for the duration of the project.
b-   The decentralized implementation is required by the Government's
decentralization policy supported by the project. For this purpose,
the government will support the creation or strengthening of
information systems units in the various institutions, either by
appointing new staff or by regrouping scattered technical staff
currently employed.



136                          Annex 12
Page 4 of 11
VII -ubcomonents
A - Central Health Ministry
10.   The project will finance investments of $1.648 million to put in place the
information systems needed by the central ministry to function as an efficient
coordinating and guiding institution in the health sector. These systems will
affect the core business of the Ministry and link the management teams of all the
institutions in the sector.   They will be the main vehicle for monitoring
institutional performance; for collecting, processing, and disseminating sectoral
statistics; for formulating and reporting on management performance agreements;
and for analyzing, preparing, and distributing policies and plans.
11.   Four functional areas of the Ministry will be improved through the
investments in information systems:
1)    Administration will be improved through computerized systems for
accounting, budgeting, payroll, human resources, document control,
and supplies inventory management.
2)    Nanarement  will  be  improved  through  systems  to  support  the
formulation of management performance agreements, the consolidation
and analysis of accounting and budget data, and the compilation and
diosemination of sector statistics.
3)    Staff productivity will be improved through the use of personal and
shared computers equipped with analytical, word processing, and
communication software.    Instead of targeting to improve the
productivity of the entire organization, specific, current tasks in
various organizational units will be targeted for improvement
through the investments in new technology.
4)    Inter-Institutional coordination will be improved through a new
communication system which will carry and track messages, documents,
distribution lists, and pending actions between the Ministry, the
Regional Health Secretariats, the Regional Health Services, a.d the
specialized institutes (ISP and CSF). In addition, the Ministry's
emergency telecommunication system will be modernized through
investments in new packet radio, telephone switching, and power
backup equipment.
12.   The following are some of the Central Ministry's problems whose solution
or alleviation is sought by the project through investments in information
systems (full list is contained in project documents):

B - Health Service Areas
13.   Tho project will finance investments in information systems to improve the
management and operational efficiency of 23 regional health directorates, 7 large
hospitals, 1 medium-size hospital, 2 "Centros de Diagnostico Terapeutico
(CDT's)n, and I "Centro de Referencia de Salud  (CRS)".  The Government is
financing similar investments in other institutions through other funding
sources.
14.    The objectives of information systems investments in the HSAU are as
follows:
1)    At the levei of Reaional Health Directorates, administration  -
particularly procurement and supplies control -- and management --
particularly the programming and control function ) will be improved
through  new  computerized  systems  and  streamlined  related
administrative procedures. As in the case of the central ministry,
these systems will affect the core business of the Regional Health
Directorates, and thus require careful staff training,

C - Central Supply Agency
16.   CSF will be transformed by the Government into an autonomous state
enterprise. For this purpose, the project will finance critical invastments in
institutional development described under the Institutional Development Component
section.   In addition, under this component, approximately $300,000 will be
financed to improve the human resource management, inventory management,
purchasing, warehousing, data processing, communications, and financial control
systems of CS1.
17.   Institutional improvements pursued through the Informatics Program  of the
Project pertain to three critical areas:
1) QOerations. Computerized systems will be created to simplify, speed up,
and increase reliability of customer queries, order placement, inventory
management, shipping, and invoicing. CSF staZf and management will have
on-line access to information on stock, usage, price, and location of
supplies which is needed for effective inventory management, purchasing,
and order shipmer't.
2) Administratio_n. Existing systems will be improved and new ones created
for financial management, governmental accounting and budgeting, cash
management, and fixed assets management.
3)  Data processina.   CSF has been using an outside service for its data
processing needs. The project will support the development of an internal
function commensurate with the central role that computerized information
systems will acquire in the institution. This function will be staffed by
3 people (a marager and 2 experts) whose positions and budget will be
funded by the Government on a permanent basis.
18.   Informatics Program activities at CSF will be completed during the first
24 months of the project at a total cost of $300,000.   Of this amount,
approximately $138,000 is for hardware, $154,000 for software, and $8,000 for
training.  Incremental costs of $70,000 per annum will finance new technical
staff, increased telecommunications usage, supplies, and hardware/software
maintenance.
19.   The following are some of the major institutional problems at CSP, and
project activities designed to solve those problems:

D - Public Health Institute
20.   As otlher institutions in the health sector, the ISP lacks information and
management systems consistent with the expanded role and increased efficiency and
accountability pursued by the Government through this project. Procedures are
slow and unreliable; information on the status of work in progress is very
difficult to obtain for most of the laboratories and services of the institute;
statistics are compiled only occasionally at an extraordinary cost of money and
time. Staff find it hard to keep up with growing work demands and budgetary
restrictions .
21.   Institutional improvements pursued through the Informatics Program of the
project are mainly in the operations area since the ISP is financing with its own
funds, a related project to improve financial management and administration. The
project will finance the development of key operational systems such as: AIDS
patient monitoring, transplant candidate/donor tracking, laboratory test
tracking, and biological and environmental test monitoring. On-line databases
will be available in the three operating departments of ISP to support the above
systems, with data on registration and tracking of projects, products,
substances, costs, and services. In addition, the project will finance the
preparation of a new administrative manual, and related staff training, in an
effort to simplify operations before introducing computerized systems.
22.   Informatice activities at ISP will be carried out during the first 24
months of the project.   'heir total cost under the project is approximately
$314,000, of which $203,000 are for hardware, $99,000 for software, and $ 12,000
for training.

project activities designed to solve those problems:

ANNEX i3: CERVICAL CANCER
CONTROL PROGRAM
Chile is a country which has been successfully overcoming the main
health problems associated to pregnancy and delivery, transmitted diseases, and
nutritional deficiencies in children and pregnant women. Infant mortality today
stands at nearly 16 per 1000 live births. Maternal mortality is 0.4 per 1000
live births. The proportion of overall malnutrition in children under five is
eight per cent, with moderate and advanced malnutrition at 0.8 per cent. In
addition, the population structure has been evolving towards an intermediate type
pyramid with a large proportion (69*) fifteen years of age and over due to a
decrease in the birth rate, which today is 23 per 1000. As a result of these
epidemiological changes, for rearly two decades the major cause of death has been
cardiovascular disease followed by cancer, which accounts for one of every five
deatho in the country.
Mortality associated to cervical cancer The mortality rate from
cervical cancer is higher in Chile (adjusted rate of 13.7 per 100,000) than in
other Latin American countries (Costa Rica 11.1; Venezuela 10.3; Uruguay 4.9;
Argentina 4.8; Cuba 4,6) and both the United States (3.0) and Canada (2.9).
Cervical cancer, currently ranked third as the cause of death associated with
malignant tumors in women is, nonetheless, the type of cancer which accounts for
the largest lose of potential life/years among women (129 per 100,000
women/year). It ranks first in hospital discharges from malignant tumors (14%)
with a rapid growth rate of discharges for the aforementioned cause during the
past few years (from 9 per 100,000 women in 1975 to 65 in 1988).  It is also the
most readily detected and effectively controlled malignant tumor in women.
Backaround of the Cervical Cancer Control Proar. The risk of dying
from cervical cancer has not changed during the twenty-seven years that the
Cervical Cancer Control Program (CCCP) has been in operation.   Under this
program, activities for early detection were initiated in 1966 through an
agreement between the Ministry of Health (MOH), PAT.O, and the University of
Chile.   These activities have continued to date while undergoing several
modifications. The lack of impact by the CCP may be attributed to the following
causes: (i) very low coverage of only approximately fifteen percent; (ii) high
volume of repeated Pap Smears in the same small group of women as until recently
one Pap Smear per year was recommended; (iii) targeting of low-risk women (15 to
35 years of age) by the CCCP because this group has always been linked to
maternal-child health activities; (iv) precarious quality control in performing
and reading Pap Smears and at the gynecological units for cervical pathology; (v)
sporadic continued education, supervision, and control programs; and (vi)
obsolete equipment and physical facilities.
Reform of the Cervical Cancer Control Prooram.  The four main goals
of the Cervical Cancer Program will be to:
(i)      Provide coverage for women aged 25 to 64, with an
emphasis on those aged 40 to 54, who are at higher risk
of this type of cancer;



145                    Annex 13
Page 2-of 5
(ii)    Reach 80 percent of all women in the targeted age group
and provide a Pap smear test every three years;
(iii)    Ensure reliability of the Pap smear diagnosis by means
of quality control programs, supervision, and training;
(iv)    Ensure a verification diagnosis (colpewcopy and biopsy),
and timely treatment (conization, hysterectomy or
radiation as required) to all women with detected
cervical cancer.
Meeting these goals means that a total of approximately 785,000 Pap
Smears will be targeted to be performed per year. An average of 10,900 cases of
cervical cancer would probably be detected, of which 9,800 will be in the
preinvasive stage. It would be necessary to attend 39,000 cervical pathology
consultations and perform 15,700 cervical biopsies to confirm these diagnoses.
With the improvement in the detection of cervical cancer, it is
estimated that 65 percent of the cases detected would be in the preinvasive stage
(NIB I and NIB II). Approximately 5,400 of these cases would be treated on an
ambulatory basis and another 5,800 will be treated through regular gynecological
surgery (4,400 conizations and 1,460 simple hysterectomies). The 900 to 1000
cases which are expected to be diagnosed in the invasive stage (Stage I and II),
would require 500 radical surgeries and 22,500 radiotherapy sessions per year.
In approximately 110 women, the tumor would be detected in such an advanced stage
(Stages III and IV) that they may only receive palliative therapy.
Imoact of the Reformed Cervical Cancer Control Proaram. As a result
of increased detection and treatment 90 per cent of all detected cervical cancer
cases would receive timely medical attention (i.e. the remaining ten percent the
tumor would be too advanced for med4cal treatment). This is expected to bring
about a 50 per cent reduction of deaths associated to cervical cancer once 80 per
cent coverage has been attained, and an even h%gher percentage starting from this
point. See Diagram 1.
Primary Lev_l Ativities. in order to attain it's objectives Cervical
Cancer Control Program will undertake activities at the primary, secondary and
tertiary levels. Pap smears will be performed by a duly trained "matrona" at all
primary level health institutions 'health centers), so that by 1995 the current
15 to 20 per cent coverage may be increasse to 50 per cent, and by the year 2000
the 80 per cent goal may be attained. With this coverage, and a Pap smear every
three years per 110.000 women, 30.000 will be attended per year, which requires
some 36.000 Pap Smears (the additional 20% is divided up as follows: 13* for
repetitions and 7* for Pap Smears in the 25 year and under age group having
special risk conditions).  ahe following supplies are required for these Pap
smear tests:   36.000 spatulas; 36.000  microscopic slides, 420 specula, 10S
hours/week per "matrona" (4 Pap Smears/hour), full time auxiliary and one
consultation room.

Sendary Lvel Activities. At the secondary level the program will
concentrate on improving cytopathology laboratories. Zn order to avoid a high
incidence of false negative tests, those cytopathology laboratories which will
process at leant 30.000 samples per year will be strengthened.  It has been
determined that the Hoalth Services not processing this volume, will send the Pap
smars  to the  improved laboratories.   A  total  of sixteen  cytopathology
laboratories will be improved (See Box 1).
EQI L DOAORR m mGtR -,-,--iia -
WE~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
The Atacama Health Service will continue to send samples (12,000) to
the Hospital J.J. Aguirre in Santiago while samples from Llanchipal (22,000) and
Magallanes (9,000) will be processed At laboratories belonging to other health
services.
Bach improved laboratory will have the following equipment and
personnel per every 30,000 to 35,000 samples: 3 binocular microscopes; 22 hours
of work per week by, the had of the laboratory (cytopathologiet); 3 full-time
techniciano 170 Pap smars/day); 1 secretary; 1 auxiliary worker; and 120 sq0are
meters of facilities.
in addition, support will be provided for the creation of a national
rference lTboratory,a and  for quality control at the cytopsthology laboratories
at Hospital. Salvador in Santiago     w

Tertiary Level Actiyitiem. At the tertiary level the Cervial Cancer
Program will focus on outpatient clinics for cervical pathology, AnatomoPathology
laboratories, and hospitalisations for surgery.   In order to confirm the
diagnosis of patients with positive Pap smars and to provide outpatient services
for some incipient lesions, the cervical pathology consultation rooms at the
Health Services will be strengthened so that they may adequately perform
colposcopies, cryocauterisation and biopsies. For every 110.000 women of the
target population, the following supplies will be required at the cervical
pathology units: 1 gynecology table, 1 colposcope, 1 cryocautery, 20 vaginal
specula, 3 sets for cervical biopsy samples, 13 hours/week of the colposcopist
(4 controls/hour), 1 "matrona-, 1 auxiliazy, and 2 consultation rooms.
It will also be necessary to complete outfitting and staffing of the
AnatomoPathology laboratories at the different Health Services to process and
read the biopsies and cones. Towards this end, the following will be required
per 110.000 women: 1 microtome, 1 tincture battery, and pot histology, 1 part-
time AnatomoPathology physician (2 exams/hour), 1 full-time technologist, 1
secretary, 1 auxiliary nurse, 1 service auxiliary and 120 square meters of
facilities.
Lastly, the gynecological and oncology services at the project's
Health Service hospitals, will be reinforced (conization, simple and radical
hysterectomy). Others will receive support through the IDB project and bilateral
cooperation. The following equipoent will be required for 110.000 women: 3
conisation kits, 1 part-time gynecologist, 340 operating room hours (1 hour for
conization, 2 for simple hysterectomy), 1. 500 days/bed (5 days/bed per conization
and 7 days/bed per hysterectomu'). The improvement of radiotherapy services are
not contemplated under the project, as the current availability would suffice to
cover the demand generated by the CCP.

Estimado Sefior Presidente:
En relaci6n al " Health Sector Reform Project " que el Gobiemo de Chile esta en proceso
de convenir con el Banco Mundial, me es muy grato poner en su conocimiento los fundamentos,
objetivos y estrategia de las reformas polfticas e institucionales del Gobierno para el sector salud.
1. LA POLITICA SOCIAL DEL SUPREMO GOBIERNO
El Gobierno se propone establecer las bases de un nuevo consenso nacional que haga
posible un avance simultAneo del desarrollo estable de su economfa y la mejorfa significativa de
las condiciones de vida de los sectores mas postergados. Lo anterior se estA haciendo en el
marco de polfticas responsables y justas que, procurando dar cumplimiento al doble objetivo de
crecimiento y equidad, garanticen la estabilidad y el funcionamiento fluido de la economia.
La concepci6n que orienta el programa es la de un Estado activo que configura una
economfa mixta en que sector pdblico y privado se complementan adecuadamente. Se propicia
una economfa en que el Estado y la empresa privada en sus diversas formas cumplan sus
funciones propias, con criterio de eficiencia y complementaci6n, a fin de responder tanto al
interes pdblico como a la necesidad de preservar y dinamizar la iniciativa privada.
En este contexto, la polftica social enfrenta el desaffo de contribuir a la integraci6n de
la sociedad chilena para que los ciudadanos disfruten en forma mas equitativa del desarrollo.
La integraci6n supone esfuerzos serios en tomo a reducir la pobreza, asf como polfticas que
mejoren la calidad de vida de los sectores medios y en general de toda la poblaci6n. Esto
significa realizar una polftica social que comprenda, en un esfuerzo integral, el mejomamiento
en el acceso, de los grupos objetivos, a los servicios de salud, de educaci6n, vivienda, justicia,
seguridad personal, medio ambiente y recreaci6n.
1



157                          Annex 15
Page 2 of 10
El gobierno se encuentra empeflado, en el marco de las restricciones macroecon6micas,
en incrementar los recursos financieros al sector salud. El aumento de dichos recursos se hara
respetando el principio de equidad en el financiamiento, lo que implica que el mayor gasto en
salud sera financiado por medio de los recursos provenientes del sistema general de impuestos.
El gobierno se ha propuesto resolver las grandes inequidades existentes en la poblaci6n
mejorando el acceso a la salud de los grupos de mas bajos ingresos, asf como tambidn a la de
aquellos ubicados en zonas geograficas alejadas de los centros de atenci6n. El gobierno pondra
especial enfasis en que a los pacientes se les otorgue una atencion digna y humanizada.
I.   POLMICAS GENERALES EN SALUD
Las principales poifticas generales que orientaran las acciones del Gobierno en salud son:
I.          El sistema de salud Chileno es mixto, con participaci6n del sector privado y
pdblico. Al sector pu.blico le corresponde la definici6n de polfticas, normas, planes y
programas, y la supervisi6n de su cumplimiento, la fiscalizaci6n del desarrollo del
sistema privado de salud, como asimismo la contribuci6n en forma parcial al
financiamiento y ejecuci6n de las prestaciones de salud de quienes han sido definidos
como los beneficiarios del sistema.
Al sector privado le corresponde participar en el disefio de sistemas de salud
privados, que se integren coordinadamente a los del sector publico en el cumplimiento
de los objetivos nacionales en el Ambito de la salud de la poblaci6n. Asimismo,
participar en el otorgamiento de prestaciones de salud y, en la administracidn de los
recursos financieros provenientes de sus usuarios o de sus empleadores.
2.          La polftica de salud impulsara el fortalecimiento y ampliaci6n de la
descentralizacidn. Un proceso de descentralizaci6n constituye un elemento consustancial
al logro de los objetivos de mayor participaci6n y de incremento de la eficiencia. La
descentralizaci6n permitira una gestidn flexible y aut6noma, manteniendo una estrecha
coordinacion que asegure la integralidad del sistema de salud chileno. Ademas, debera
considerar las necesidades y los derechos de la poblacion en lo relativo a disponibilidad,
acceso, calidad de la asistencia, participaci6n de la comunidad en el sistema de prestaci6n
de asistencia de salud en las regiones, en el empleo racional de los recursos y en la
coordinaci6n entre subsistemas de prestaci6n de atenci6n de salud.
La ampliaci6n del proceso de descentralizaci6n se expresara en el campo de la
toma de decisiones respecto de los recursos financieros, a traves de un incremento en las
atribuciones de los Servicios de Salud.
2



158                           Annex 15
Page 3 of 10
3.          De manera complementaria a las poifticas resenadas en el punto anterior, se
reforzardn las tareas de supervisi6n y coordinaci6n, mediante el fortalecimiento
institucional del Ministerio, tanto a nivel central como regional.
4.          Se busca elevar la eficiencia productiva y la eficacia del sector ptiblico frente al
nuevo perfil epidemiol6gico del pais, asignando recursos en forma prioritaria a las
soluciones de mayor costo-efectividad. La poiftica de mejorar la eficiencia debera
considerar las acciones necesarias en lo que se refiere a modernizar los sistemas
administrativos, implementar sistemas de informaci6n para la gesti6n, e incorporar los
incentivos adecuados. La excelencia institucional que se espera del sistema de salud para
que concurra efectivamente al proceso de desarrollo, debera manifestarse en la calidad
de los servicios que se prestan a la comunidad.
5.    La reorganizacion de los sistemas y el consecuente cambio en las estructuras
administrativas se realizara en un marco de participaci6n de los agentes involucrados en
el proceso. Las reformas buscaran conciliar la urgencia de realizar modificaciones con
la necesaria prudencia que requieren estos procesos.
m. PROBLEMAS Y DESAFIOS DEL SISTEMA DE SALUD CHILENO
El sistema de salud chileno se enfreita hoy a una diversidad de problemas y
desaffos como consecuencia de cuatro problemas generales:
(i)   El cambio epidemiol6gico emergente que pone al pats en una situaci6n de transici6n
epidemiol6gica, expresada en la creciente incidencia y prevalencia de patologfas cr6nicas
no transmisibles y sus complicaciones, coexistiendo con patologlas de primer nivel.
(ii)   La insuficiencia en la capacidad de gesti6n del sector pu'blico de salud. El Gobierno se
encontr6 con un sistema publico que, en todos sus niveles ha visto deteriorarse
significativamente su capacidad y recursos para la gesti6n.
(iii)  La prolongada restricci6n financiera del sector publico que ha generado un fuerte deficit
de inversi6n (infraestructura y equipamiento), asi como tambidn ha afectado en forma
significativa la capacidad operacional del sector (escasez de insumos, recursos humanos,
falta de mantenci6n).
(iv)   El Desarrollo inorganico y descoordinado de los subsectores pdblico y privado de salud.
3

IV. PLAN DE REFORMA DEL SISTEMA DE SALUD CHILENO
El sistema de salud chileno, ha evolucionado en forma manifiestamente desigual, en los
diltimos ahios. Por una parte el subsector privado se ha desarrollado con evidente rapidez
mientras que por otra el subsector publico se ha visto sometido a significativas restricciones,
resultando de esto, una gran brecha en la calidad y cobertura de los servicios privados y
publcos. Esta brecha mirada desde el punto de vista del principio de equidad, impulsa al
Gobierno a convertir al sector salud en su primera prioridad dentro del campo social.
En el Chile de hoy, las consideraciones de urgencia y viabilidad implican, necesariamen-
te, un gran esfuerzo por iniciar una soluci6n definitiva y en un horizonte de largo plazo de la
crisis del subsector publico de salud y perfeccionar el funcionamiento del subsector privado.
Aunque el enfasis del Gobierno estA puesto, a partir de lo antes dicho, en la solucion de
la crisis del sector publico de salud, los esfuerzos globales estdn orientados a la mejorfa del
sistema de salud chileno como un todo, esto es, a mejorar el funcionamiento de los subsectores
prblicos y privados como complementarios y necesarios para el desarrollo de un sistema de
salud equitativo, eficiente, e integrado.
El desarrollo de ambos subsectores, consecuencia de los esfuerzos de este Gobierno,
necesariamente debe ser ejecutado de forma tal. que puedan ser orientados en el futuro, en
cualquiera de las direcciones que la evaliaci6n de los resultados de la evoluci6n de ambos
indiquen.
Esto es, los cambios de hoy, deben ser aplicados con la mayor flexibilidad posible, de
manera que la experiencia ganada en ellos permita corregir la orientaci6n de cualesquiera de los
subsectores hacia un funcionamiento mds costo-efectivo. Desde una perspectiva de visi6n global
del sistema de salud chileno, Ia integraci6n publico privado serf el resultado de la evoluci6n y
el perfeccionamiento de la complementariedad de ambos subsectores.
Para cada uno de los subsectores la mejorfa tiene su significaci6n especffica.
La mejorfa para el sector privado significa: (i) orientar a este sector en una perspectiva
de largo plazo en el cual asuma a plenitud los riesgos de la poblaci6n durante toda la vida de
los trabajadores; (ii) lograr un sistema de salud privado mas transparente, en el cual la demanda
pueda expresarse con la mayor simetrfa posible, respecto de la oferta; (iii) lograr el compromiso
del subsector privado en un enfrentamiento en forma integral de la atenci6n de salud, es decir,
con acciones de fomento, proteccidn , recuperacidn y rehabilitaci6n de directo beneficio para
sus afiliados. En otras palabras, debe, el sector privado, disehlar productos que resuelvan en su
totalidad los riesgos de salud de la poblaci6n a la cual sirven; (iv) finalmente, el desaffo que el
sector privado debe enfrentar es, disefiar mecanismos que permitan controlar la evoluci6n de los
costos, para que pueda, en lo sucesivo, ofrecer productos a los niveles de precio que el pats estl
en condiciones de pagar.

A su vez el esfuerzo de mejorfa para el subsector pdblico estA basado en tres pilares
fundamentales: (i) Readecuar el sistema de atenci6n de salud con el objetivo de dar respuesta
efectiva a las necesidades que surgen del actual perfil epidemiol6gico chileno; (ii) Perfeccionar
el modelo de gesti6n del sector pdblico, con fin de fortalecer su capacidad de gesti6n en todas
las tareas que hoy ejerce: Normar, supervisar, financiar y ejecutar; (iii) Reformar el sistema de
financiamiento al interior del sistema publico de salud con el fin de adecuar los incentivos a las
metas de salud y gesti6n sectorial. Para la operacionalizaci6n de estos tres pilares de mejorfa
del subsector pdblico, el Ministerio de Salud, con la inestimable colaboraci6n de un sinndmero
de profesionales, ha preparado el Proyecto de Reforma del Sector Salud (Proyecto HSRP).
La ejecuci6n de este proyecto permitira invertir en la gente, al mismo tiewn '0 que mejorar
significativamente la eficiencia social en la gesti6n de salud en Chile. Esto, - travds de un
profundo esfuerzo de modernizaci6n de la gesti6n, basado en una descentralizaci6n que potencia
la integralidad del sistema pliblico de atenci6n de salud, imposible de implementar sin las
inversiones consideradas en el presente proyecto.
El proyecto HSRP contiene dos grandes areas: Desarrollo Institucional y Plan de
Inversi6n.
El componente de desarrollo institucional del proyecto HSRP tiene como objetivo
modernizar la gesti6n del sector en el ejercicio de todos los roles que le cabe desempefnar, esto
en un ambiente de creciente descentralizacion. Las acciones contempladas en el son:
1.-   La Reestructuraci6n del Ministerio de Salud.
La reestructuraci6n comprende: (i) La revisi6n de la estructura organizacional para
concentrar al Ministerio en sus tareas normativas y supervisoras. Se creara para elo la Divisi6n
de Operaciones, asf como los Comit6s de Desarrollo, de Operaciones y Ejecutivo; (ii) Disefno
e implementaci6n de un mecanismo denominado compromisos de gesti6n, que permitan observar
las acciones de salud ejecutadas por los Servicios de Salud en la direcci6n del uso de recursos
y en la de su relaci6n con las metas que se pretende alcanzar, asf como acordar, entre Ministerio
y Servicios, la entrega de los recursos y oportunidad a que se obliga el Ministerio para el
cumplimiento de las metas acordadas; (iii) Adicionalmente se considera el diseflo de un
instrumento t6cnico adecuado que permita dimensionar las necesidades y disponibilidades de
recursos humanos en los Servicios de Salud.
Las acciones anteriores se complementargn con la ejecucion de acciones de mejommiento
de la capacidad gerencial y de gesti6n de los niveles de direccion intermedia y administrativa,
tanto del Ministerio de Salud, como Servicios de Salud y otras instituciones dependientes, a
trav6s de la capacitaci6n, la que se efectuard vfa un mecanismo de fondo concursable, "El
Programa de Apoyo a la Gesti6n", el que se sehalz mas adelante.

2.-   Reforma al Sistema de Abastecimiento dej Sector Publico de Salud
La reforma al sistema de abastecimiento del sector pdblico de salud tiene por objetivo
mejorar tanto la operaci6n como la gesti6n del sistema. Para ello se ha estimado necesario
implantar, a nivel de la Central de Abastecimientos (CENABAS), las siguientes acciones: (i)
eliminar las presentes restricciones administrativas y financieras a traves de la transforrnaci6n
del actual servicio en una empresa aut6noma del estado; (ii) Mejorar la eficiencia y
productividad a traves de una mejor administraci6n de sus bodegas, sistema de transporte y
poiftica de precios.
3.-   Mejoramiento de la Capacidad de Gesti6n del I.S.P.
El mejoramiento de la capacidad de gestion del Instituto de Salud Pu'blica (ISP), dene
como objetivo fortalecer el rol de apoyo cientffico-tdcnico que cumple el ISP al interior del
sistema de salud del pafs a la vez que elevar el nivel de su capacidad operativa y de gesti6n. Las
principales acciones a ejecutar son: (i) realizaci6n de un estudio de factibilidad de un eventual
"Joint venture" con firmas privadas para mejorar el desarrollo de la funci6n de producci6n del
ISP; (ii) realizar un estudio de costos de prestaciones y disenlo de fijaci6n y actualizaci6n de
aranceles, y metodos modemos de cobranzas; (iii) realizar un estudio del area de abastecimiento
del ISP que apunte a mejorar las modalidades de adquisici6n de productos y manejo de
inventarios; (iv) ejecutar un programa de modemizaci6n de equipos de laboratorio.
4.    Reforma de los Sistenlias de Financiamiento
La reforma a los sistemas de financiamniento intrasector, tiene por objetivo abordar tres
aspectos fundamentales para los Servicios de Salud en relaci6n con esta materia: (i) la asignaci6n
de recursos financieros desde el nivel central; (ii) la recuperaci6n de costos y (iii) la
determinaci6n de costos de prestaciones.
La reforma de la asignaci6n de recursos financieros al interior del sistema publico de
salud, tiene como objetivo implantar un inecanismno de financiamiento de las prestaciones de
salud que sea eficiente en la asignaci6n de recursos. Para ello se ha optado por una estrategia
de avanzar rapidamente corrigiendo las deficiencias de los actuales (FAP y FAPEM) a traves
de su reemplazo progresivo por nuevas modalidades de pago. Con este fin se piensa introducir
un sistema di pago prospectivo por atenci6n de un grupo de patologfas, denominado Pago
Asociado a Diagn6stico (PAD) que reemplace progresivamente al FAP. El objetivo del PAD
es incentivar una administraci6n mds eficiente de los recursos pagando por patologfa resuelta y
no por prestaci6n realizada. De igual forma, en lo que se refiere a establecimientos de atenci6n
primaria municipalizados, se irmplementara una modalidad de financiamiento "per capita" que
reemplazara al actual FAPEM.

En lo referente a recuperaci6n de costos, esta tiene como objetivo implantar un Sistema
de Recuperacion de Costos (SRC) en los establecimientos del sector pdblico de salud. Con este
prop6sito se realizaran reformas que moditiquen las condicionantes institucionales que afectan

y controlar su cumplimiento por parte del SNSS, proveer a los Servicios de Salud de tecnicas
e instrumentos que permitan incrementar la venta de servicios a no beneficiarios, diseflar
mecanismos que permitan resolver problemas econ6micos y financieros generados por
enfermedades cr6nicas o catastr6ficas de los beneficiarios.
Se realizara, ademas, un estudio de costos que tiene por objetivo determinar los costos
de las prestaciones, de manera tal que permita readecuar los actuales aranceles para que reflejen
los costos efectivos de las prestaciones.
5.-   Programa de Apoyo a la Gesti6n
El programa de apoyo a la gesti6n tiene como objetivo actualizar las habilidades y
destrezas del personal del sector pdblico de salud, para la realizaci6n de una mas efectiva y
eficiente atenci6n a la poblaci6n beneficiaria, a traves de la creaci6n de un fondo concursable
de capacitaci6n a ser administrado por el Ministerio de Salud.
6.-   Sistema de Informaci6n
La implementaci6n del sisteina nacional de informatica en salud tiene como objetivo la

Salud, Instituto de Salud Piblica y Central de Abastecimiento; apoyar fuertemente al control de

elevandolo a un nivel de operaci6n moderno acorde con el actual estado informatico del pafs.
7.    Programas Especfficos
En programas especfficos, podemos distinguir dos programas; Programa de Cdncer
C6rvico Uterino y Programa de Educaci6n en Medio Ambiente, los que se sefialan a conti-

asociados.

El plan de inver i,nes contenido en el HSRP tiene el objetivo de lograr la readecuaci6n
del modelo de atenci6n de salud de acuerdo a las riecesidades que surgen de nuestro actual perfil
epidemiol6gico y, al mismo tiempo, cubrir vacfos de atenci6n en zonas de poblaci6n pobre.
Esta readewuaci6n se orienta hacia la redefinici6n y operacionalizacidn de los niveles de
complejidad de todos los establecimientos del sistema publico de atenci6n de salud, asf como una
normalizacion de la red de sistemas de referencia, tanto para pacientes electivos como para

i.    La creaci6n de Centros de Especialidades BAsicas, de referencia intercomunales (CRS),
a los cuales podran referir los consultorios de Atenci6n Primaria Municipalizados para
atenci6n de especialistas y que estarAn a cargo del Servicio de Salud.
ii.    El reforzamiento de toda la Atenci6n Ambulatoria, a traves de la creaci6n de Centros de
Diagn6stico y Tratamiento Amnbulatorio los que tendrAn autonomfa administrativa y de
recursos, respecto al hospital al cual estAn adj untos.
iii.   La modernizaci6n y reparaci6n de la red asistencial de tres regiones del pafs, pan
resolver los mas importantes "cuellos de botella" detectados en ellas y aumentar la
productividad y calidad de la atenci6n de salud en establecimientos ya existentes.
Este conjunto de medidas permitira ordenar el s;tema de atenci6n de salud y, por lo
tanto, dar mayor racionalidad a la planiticaci6n de inversiones y 41 desarrollo de cada uno de
los Servicios del sistema publico de atenci6n constituyendose, asf, en la estrategia mas costo
efectiva para enfrentar el emergente presente y fituro de nuestro perfil epidemiol6gico.
El Plan de Inversi6n en Salud corresponde a las inversiones en infraestructura y equipa-
miento, para satisfacer las necesidades de salud mas prioritarias, detectadas en las Areas geogrAfi-
cas incluidas en este componente, las que cubren a rnds del 50% de la poblaci6n.
Las Areas geogrAficas en que se iniciara, incluyen las regiones de El Loa, Metropolitana
y parte de la regi6n de Los Lagos Sur.
Este plan contempla, entre otras acciones extensamente descritas en el proyecto
presentado al Banco para su financiamniento, la construcci6n de 5 centros de Referencia de Salud,
5 Centros Diagn6stico Terapeuticos, 4 hospitales (El Pino, Santiago Oriente, San Jose y San
Ram6n). El Plan de inversiones se complementa con un vasto plan de reacondicionamiento y
reparaciones hospitalarias que encuentran en distintas etapas de ejecuci6n en el Proyecto Tecnical
Assistance and Hospital Rehabilitation Project firmado con el Banco en Diciembre de 1991.

V. Financiandento del plan de reformas del Sector Salud
El Gobierno tiene la capacidad financiera para sostener el esfuerzo de ejecuci6n tanto de
las reformas polfticas e institucionales como del plan de inversiones en el sector salud en los
pr6ximos seis ailos. Una reforma tributaria aprobada en forma consensual en 1990 ha implicado
un crecimiento significativo de los ingresos fiscales los que permiten, en 1991 y 1992, un
incremento de 12 % y 13% respectivamente, en el gasto social. Especfficamente, el gasto publico
en salud ha crecido significativamente en los dos ultimos anios, lo que refleja 'a alta prioridad
dada por el Gobierno al aumento de la inversi6n en salud. Al mismo tiempo para el gobierno,
es un principio de su manejo macroecon6mico el que el crecimiento se enmarcard estrictamente
dentro de los lfmites que define el crecimiento del producto y consecuentemente, de sus ingresos.
La reforma del sector y el programa de inversiones implicarl un significativo
compromiso financiero del Gobierno tanto para cubrir los gastos de inversi6n como los gastos
recurrentes. Para cubrir los mencionlados requerimientos tinancieros, el Gobierno proyecta un
gasto Fiscal en Salud mfnimo situado entre el 1.0 a. 1.2% del PGB para el resto de la ddcada.
Al mismo tiempo, se espera que las ganancias de eficiencia dadas en la implementaci6n de las
reformas sectoriales moderen el aumento de los costos recurrentes provenientes de las
inversiones Se esperan ahorros netos o rectursos adicionales, entre otros de: (i) aumento de
ingresos por el mayor numero de camas de pensionado en los hospitales publicos, (ii) mejorfa
en los sistemas de recuperaci6n de costos; (iii) administraci6n eficiente de los recursos humanos
y suministros, (iv) reduccion de la demanda por atenci6n intrahospitalaria de pacientes dada la
nueva disponibilidad de centros ambulatorios bien equipados, y reducci6n de los promedios de
estadfa intrahospitalaria. El Gobierno esta, al mismo tiempo, probando en un proyecto piloto,
las recomendaciones sobre reformas a los sistemnas de asignaci6n de recursos financieros al
interior del sector. Se encuentra eoi tramite legislativo el cambio de sistema de financiamiento
del nivel de atenci6n primaria. Se espera que estas acciones llevarin a mecanismos de pago
realistas para la atenci6n de salud e implicaran una atenci6n de salud mas eficiente y de mejor
calidad para todos los chilenos.
Estimado Sr. Presidente, las politicas enunciadas constituyen un esfuerzo sin precedentes
del Estado chileno en las dIltimas dos decadas por mejorar en forma sustantiva las condiciones
de vida de la gente de forma tal de posibilitar el desarrollo nioderno y democratico de nuestro
pafs. La reforma del sector salud es el fruto de un extenso y complejo proceso donde se funden
las aspiraciones de profesionales y pacientes, las necesidades de modernizaci6n del sector y los

consistente.

Annex 16: PCU FUNCTIONS AND
ORGANIZATIONAL CHART
IUNCTIONS OF TIE  PCU:
A Project Coordination Unit (PCU) has been established to assist in
the oversight of project implementation. The PCU reports to the Chief of the
Budget and Planning Division of the Ministry of Health.
The functions of the PCU are to:
(a) Coordinate all activities needed for the execution of the
project;
(b) Provide all of the teci-nical and administrative
assistance that project execution would require;
(c) Prepare the norms and procedures for the planning,
submission, administration, bidding, and execution of the
project activities in accordance with the rules agreed
upon by the Bank and the Government;
(d) Prepare the terms of reference, hire under terms and
conditions satisfactory to the Bank, and supervise
consultants who will assist in implementing the project
components, oarrying out the technical assistance studies
and pilot-testing selected reconm.endations;
(e) Assist and supervise the MOH institutions in the
formulation of bidding contracts for approved
subprojects; supervise the drafting of contracts between
the suppliers and the MOH institutions for the execution
of the subprojects;
(f) Ensure that the bidding competitions for works, equipment
and/or studies are carried out in accordance with the
rules and procedures agreed on by the Bank and the
Governmrent;
(g) Approve on a preliminary basis the documents produced by
the consultants and request final approval of these
documents by the MOH;
(h) Advise the MOH institutions on the formulation and
management of the health sector reform subprojects,
providing training and support for resolving any problems
that may arise in the various phases of preparation and
implementation;



167                           Annex 16
Page 2 of 4
(i) Ensure that the works, equipment and/or studies under
execution are in line with the requirements of the work
programs for each project;
(j) Coordinate and control the flow of funds for subprojects
under execution, reporting on the needs and progress of
disbursement to the Office of the Under-Secretary for
health and to DIPLAP;
(k) Require from the NOH Institutions periodic reports on the
physical and financial status of the subprojects;
(1) Maintain a record of the status of approved subprojects,
the amount approved and disbursed, and progress
indicators for each subproject under execution;
(m) Verify that the progress reports from the MOH
institutions are reliable;
(n)  Act as MOH counterpart for the World Bank missions with
respect to the preparation, coordination, submission,
negotiation, execution and other activities related to
the project;
(o) Advise the MOH on matters concerning the project,
reporting periodically on their physical and financial
progress, and proposing corrective measures;
(p) Assemble the necessary information to request from the
bank reimbursement of the funds utilized to axecute the
subprojects;
(q) Prepare progress reports and any special reports
requested by the World Bank.
INTERNAL ORGANIZATION OF THE PCUt
The PCU will be headed by a Project Coordinator who will be
assisted by four subunit heads: the operation coordinator; the coordinator for
technical design and procurement; the coordinator for institutional
develcpment; and the administrative coordinator. The PCU organizational set-
up is shown in Attachment 1. The PCU staff will be comprised of up to six
senior level professionals and thirty professional support staff. The
functions of these PCU members are described below.
Office of the Prolect Coordinator General. The Project Coordinator
will be responsible for the technical and administrative management
of the NOH-World Bank projects. The following four units will
report to the Project Coordinator:
Onarations Unit. This unit will coordinate all
operations with regard to the health service areas.



168                           Annex 16
Page 3 of 4
Institutional Develooment Unit.  This unit will overoee
the implementation of the Training Program, Information
Development Program, and other aspects of the
decentralization of health services delivery. This unit
will also be responsible for the execution of all the
studies specified in the HSRP component for the
decentralization of health services delivery.
Technical Supervision Unit. This unit will provide
advice and supervision to NOH institutions or external
consultants with regard to the architectural design of
facilities and the procurement of equipment for the
project.
Administration and Finance Unit.  This unit will be
responsible for the administrative mancagement and
financial control of the MOH-World Bank projects

ANNEX 12: MONITORING AND EVALUATION
OF THE HSRP PROJECT
PRELDIINARY DESIGN
1.    Two types of indicators will be used to monitor and evaluate the HSRP
project:
(a) Progress indicators which would monitor the rate of implementation
of the project's activities; and
(b) Outcome indicators which would monitor progress towards the
attainment of the project's objectives.
2.    The above indicators would be related to the performance indicators that
would be incorporated in the Service Provision Agreements between the MDH and
the HSAB.
3.    The following sections presents a preliminary list of indicators that
will be analyzed and developed for HSRP monitoring and evaluation. The
benchmark reference data for each of the indicators would also be established.
It may be noteid that, as appropriate, the indicators would be used to measure
progress of: (1) an entire project component, (2) an HMA, (3) a facility,
and/or (4) a clinical service unit. The proposed coverage of the specific
indicators presented below is indicated by the corresponding numbers in the
parentheses.  The 2oLrss indicators would be monitored every six months and
the outcome indicators once every year.

ANNEX 20: ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT
MEASURES
The project will have an overall positive impact on environment.
Most of the investments could be considered as environmentally neutral, none
of them has been identified as potentially damaging and several will have a
definite pesitive impact, especially on the metropolitan environment.
A. Safety
The project implementation poses no particular safety problem,
either to the surrounding population or to the construction workers.
Construction sites are located in medium or low density areas or are
adequately isolated from surrounding houses. Hospitals and centers included
in the project are low or medium rise buildings and construction hazards will
remain below average. Chilean safety regulations on constructions are rather
strict and well enforced. Law 16,744 that makes precise provisions for
individual and collective protective measures to be tiken in all construction
tasks, will apply to all project civil works.
No radioactive materials will be handled under the project besides
standard radiological equipment and short-life radioisotopes.
S. labestos
Use of asbestos materials, in particular cement-asbestos, is common
practice in Chile. However, in response to Bank's concern, no asbestos
materials will be used in the project. To this effect the PCr will:
(a) specify in the terms of reference for detailed designs that no
asbestos materials will be utilized in the construction of
facilities, and
(b) control the compliance when reviewing bidding documents-
technical specifications and construction plans.
C. Wacte Disposal
This is usually the major environmental risk of health facilities.
In this case, the hospital and centers to be constructed will replace run-down
facilities equipped with obsolete machinery. The large additional capacity of
the new facilities - and therefore, the additional quantity of waste - will bfs
largely offset by the technical improvements in the design, equipment and
procedures of modern hospitals.
(1) Solid Wast*
The problem has been re-assessed recently for the preparation of
Bank and IDB investments projects. The PCU, in consultation with HSA, has
prepared norms for the collection, handling, storage and disposal of hospital
solid waste in specially designed Solid Waste Treatment Centers.
Implementation of these criteria and procedures in the project facilities will



178                          Annex 20
Page 2 of 3
serve as a model for other 3DH facilities which will be brought up to
standards progressively.
In general the expectod benefits - reduction in volume, absence of
hazards and nuisance - will be obtained from the separate collection,
processing and final disposal of different hospital solid wastes:
(a)  medical/surgical mcterials - contaminated bandages, linen,
syringes, disposable materials, etc... to be sterilized before
disposal;
(b) biological materials - blood and blood derivates, organs,
surgical and obstetrical wastes, etc... to be incinerated;
(c) inorganic inert waste - metal, glass, ceramics, packages,
etc... to be duMped to regular landfills, together with;
(d) camxm domestic refuise - papers, plastics, ashes, leaves,
etc...and finally;
(e) food refuse to be collected by the regular municipal
collection service.
In order to nenure the proper treatment of solid waste a Solid
Wast Treatment Center (C.T.R.8.) will be constructed in each new hospital
with ore than three hundred beds. Bach center will have two entrances: one
for coamo  domestic refuse and  nother for contaminated waste. There will
ales be two exits. One exit will be used exclusively for sterilized garbage
containers and 'trucks, while the other will be for removal. of trash to
landfills. 2te movemnt of waste to and from the center will be undertaken by
appropriately trained personnel.
At miniun each C.T.R.8. will include:
(i) a trash compactor;
(ii) receptacles for storage of domestic waste, which are
washable, inpermable to insects and rodents, and have
natural ventilation and drainage;
(ILI) equipment to sterilise contaminated waste;
(iv) an incinerator;
(v) an area to wash waste removal trucks and containers;
(vi) restrioom and changinv. rooms for tha personnel;
(vli) a supply room; and
(viii) electricity, steam, water and a sewage system.
Hospitals with less than 300 beds will not have compactors nor
incinerators because the type of equipment needed for hospital waste is



179                          Annex 20
Page 3 of 3
excessively expensive. Instead, these snaller hospitals will store the waste
in hermetically sealed receptacles.  There will be separate areas for domestic
waste (0.175 m2 per nospital bed) and for contaminated waste (0.050 m2 per
hospital bed).
(IL) Liquid Xffluent
With the exception of biological fluids discussed in (b) above and
a few laboratory chemicals and reagents, liquiC efflu-nt from hospitals and,
even more so, ambulatory facilities do not require speaial treatment before
sewage. All subprojects have received feasibility approval from the water
supply, oewage and power authorities.
(iii) Fuses and Air Pollution
In this area, major benefits are to be obtained from the project.
Obsolete and disrepaired heating systems, kitchen, laundry systems, do not
have exhaust filters and at present release large amounts of fumes. While
this has little importance in semi-rural areas or in well ventilated cities as
Puerto Montt, it contributes to the air pollution in the Santiaqo Metropolitan
Area. Modern, more efficient systems, provided with filters '-r..Ld
considerably decrease the release of fumes and air pollution.
D. Water Resources Protection and Management
Due to its peculiar hydrography and geology, Chile has long been
conscious of water protection and management. Health facilities do not
present special risks for ground water contamination as discussed above.
Preservation of surface waters has prompted the authorities to adopt strict
regulations on canalization, ditching, deviation, and evacuatior- of running
waters affected by constructions.  These regulations will be especially
relevant in the case of project sites in wet climate locations such as Puerto
Montt or Castro.
S. Urban Planning, Transportation and Housing
Studies carried out during preparation have shown that the
implantation of new facilities and the expansion of others follow the lInes of
urban growth in the Santiago Metropolitan Area. Communications,
transportation and other public services will be available without distortion
of the urban development plan. The PCU maintains consultation with the urban
planning authority on Project facilitiea and their possible impact on future
urban expansion. Finally, additional staff required by the project is minimal
and redeployment of staff is not expected to pose any significant housing
problem.
